THE RED PAGE.
The Wisdom of Nietzsche .
From “Thus Spake Zarathustra."
OF THE NEW IDOL.
The State is called the coldest of all cold mon-
sters. And coldly it lieth ; and this lie creepeth
out of its mouth : “I, the State, am,the People.”
It is a lie ! Creators they were who created the
peoples and hung one belief and one love over
them; thus they served life.
Destroyers they are who lay traps for many,
calling them the State : they hung a sword and a
hundred desires over them.
Far too many are born: for the superfluous the
State was invented.
Alas, even within you, ye great souls, the State
whispereth its gloomy lies ! Alas ! it findeth out
the rioh hearts which are eager to squander them-
selves !
Ay, it findeth out even you, ye conquerors of
the old God ! Ye got wearied in the battle, and
now your weariness serveth the new idol.
The new idol would fain surround itself with
heroes and honest men ! It liketh to sun itself
in the sunshine of good consciences—that cold
monster!
What I call the state is where all are poison-
drinkers, the good and the evil alike. What I
call the state is where all lose themselves, the
good and the evil alike. What I call the state is
where the slow suicide of all is called “ life.”
Look at those superfluous! They steal the
works of inventors and the treasures of wise men:
their theft they call education —and for them
everything turneth into disease and hardship.
Look at those superfluous! Diseased they
ever are, they vomit bile and call it newspaper.
They devour but cannot digest each other. _
They all strive towards the throne: that is their
madness—as though happiness were sitting on the
throne ! Often mud sitteth on the throne; often
also the throne sitteth on the mud.
Madmen they are all, to my mind, and climbing
apes, and over-hot. 11l smelleth to me their idol,
that cold monster; ill smell they all to me, these
idolaters.
For great souls earth is yet open. For hermits
in pairs, many seats are yet empty round which
floateth the odour of calm seas.
For great souls a free life is still open. Verily,
he who possessetli little is possessed still less : a
modest poverty be praised !
Where the state ceaseth there beginneth that
man who is not superfluous: there beginneth the
song of the necessary, the melody that is sung
once and cannot be replaced.
Where the state ceaseth— look there, I pray, my
brethren ! Do you not see it, the rainbow and
the bridges to Beyond-Man ?
OP THE FKIEND.
■‘There is always one too many about me” —
thus thinketh the hermit. Always once one—
that maketh in the long run two.
For the hermit a friend is always the third one ;
the third one is the cork that hindereth the con-
versation of the two from sinking into the depth.
Alas ! there are too many depths for all hermits.
That is why they long so much for a friend and
his height.
Our belief in others betrayeth what we would
fain believe in ourselves. Our longing for our
friend is our betrayer.
“Be at least mine enemy:” thus saith true
reverence that dareth not ask for friendship.
Thou wouldst not wear clothes in the presence
of thy friend. It is to honour thy friend that thou
presentest thyself unto him as thou art ? But he,
therefore, wisfeth thee to go unto the devil.
He who maketh no secret of himself shocketh :
so much reason have ye to fear nakedness ! Ay,
if ye were Gods, ye might well be ashamed of
your clothing! , ~
For thy friend thou canst not adorn thyself
beautifully enough : for unto him thou shalt be an
arrow and a longing towards beyond-man.
Didst thou ever see thy friend asleep so as to
learn what he is like? What is thy friend’s face
at other times? It is thine own face seen in a
rough and imperfect looking-glass.
In iinding out and being silent the friend shall
be master : thou must not wish to see anything.
Thy dream shall betray unto thee what thy
friend doth when he is awake.
Let a finding out be thy sympathy : in order
that first thou inayest know whether thy friend
seeketh sympathy. Perhaps in thee he liketh
the unmoved eye and the look of eternity.
Let thy sympathy with thy friend be hidden
under a hard shell, on it thou shalt break thy
tooth in biting. Thus thy sympathy will iiave
delicacy and sweetness. ,
Art thou unto thy friend fresh air and solitude
and bread and medicine? Many a one cannot
loose his own chains and yet is a saviour unto his
friend. , t . ,
Art thou & slave ? If thou be, thou canst not be
a friend. Art thou a tyrant ? If thou be, thou
canst not have friends.
Far too long a slave and a tyrant have been
hidden in woman. Therefore woman is not yet
capable of friendship : she knoweth love only.
In the love of woman there is injustice and
blindness unto everything she loveth not. And
even in the knowing love of woman there is still,
along with light, surprise and lightning at night.
Yet woman is not capable of friendship : women
are still always cats and birds. Or, in the best
case, cows.
Yet woman is not capable of friendship. But
say, ye men, which of you is capable of friend-
ship ? ,
Oh ! for your poverty, ye men, and your avarice
of soul! As much as ye give unto your friend I
will give unto mine enemy, and will not become
poorer thereby.
There is comradeship; oh, that there were
friendship !
OF LITTLE WOMEN OLD AND YOUNG.
Everything in woman is a riddle, and everything
in woman hath one answer: its name is child*
Man is to woman a means: the end is always the
child. But what is woman for man ?
Two things are wanted by the true man : danger
and play. Therefore he seeketh woman as the
most dangerous toy.
Man shall be educated for war, and woman for
the recreation of the warrior. Everything else is
f °Over - sweet fruits the warrior liketh not.
Therefore he liketh woman; bitter is even the
sweetest woman.
Woman understandeth children better than man
doth but man is more childlike than woman.
In the true man a child is hidden that seeketh
to play. Up, ve women, reveal the child in man!
Let woman he a toy pure a., delicate like a
jewel, illuminated by the virtues of a world which
has not yet come. . , T
Let a rav of starlight shine m your love ! Let
your hope be called : “Would that I might give
birth to beyond-man ! ”
Let bravery be in your love ! Little else doth
woman understand of honour. But let it be your
honour ever to love more than ye are loved, and
never to be the second.
Let man fear woman when she loveth : then she>
sacrificeth anything, and nothing else hath value
for her.
Let man fear woman when she hateth : for in
the heart of their heart, man is only evil, but
woman is base.
Whom doth woman hate the most ? Thus spake
the iron unto the loadstone: “ I hate thee most
because thou attractest, but art not strong enough
to draw unto thee.”
Man’s happiness is: “I will.” Woman’s hap-
piness is : “He will.”
“Behold, this moment the world hath become
perfect! ” —thus thinketh every woman, when she
obeyeth from sheer love.
And woman must obey and find a depth for her
surface. Surface is woman’s mood, a foam driven
to and fro over a shallow water.
But man’s mood is deep, his stream roareth in
underground caves: woman divineth his power,
but understandeth it not. . .
Thou goest to women ? Remember thy whip!
OP CHILD AND MARRIAGE.
Marriage: thus I call the will of two to create
that one which is more than they who created it.
I call marriage reverence unto each other as unto
those who will such a will.
Alas ! that soul’s poverty of two ! Alas ! that
soul’s dirt of two ! Alas ! that miserable ease of
two !
Worthy and ripe for the significance of earth
appeared this man unto me, but when I saw his
wife earth seemed unto me a madhouse.
Yea, I wish the earth would tremble in convul-
sions whenever a saint and a goose couple.
This one went for truths like a hero, and at last
he secured a little dressed-up lie. He calleth it
his marriage.
That one was reserved in intercourse and chose
fastidiously. But suddenly he for ever spoiled
his company : he calleth this his marriage.
Many short follies —that is what ye call love.
And your marriage maketh an end of many short
follies —being one long stupidity.
Your love unto woman, and woman’s love unto
man : alas ! would it were sympathy with suffer-
ing and veiled Gods ! But generally two animals
find each other out.
OP LOVE FOR ONE’S NEIGHBOUR.
Ye throng round your neighbour and have fine
words for that. But I tell you your love for your
neighbour is your bad love for yourselves.
Love unto the most remote future man is higher
than love unto your neighbour. And I consider
love unto things and ghosts to be higher than
love unto men.
Unable to endure yourselves and not loving
yourselves enough: ye seek to wheedle your neigh-
bour into loving you and thus to gild you with his
error.
Would that ye could not endure any kind of
your neighbours and their neighbours ; were that
so, ye would need to create your friend and hi&
enthusiastic heart out of yourselves.
Ye invite a witness, if ye wish to speak well of
yourselves, and having wheedled him into think-
ing well of you, ye think well of yourselves also.
One goeth unto the neighbours because he
seeketli himself, another because he wisheth to
lose himself. Your bad love for yourselves
maketh for yourselves a prison of solitude.
I teach you not the neighbour, but the friend.
Let the friend be for you the festival of earth and
a foretaste of beyond-man.
I teach you the friend and his too-full heart.
But one must know how to be a sponge, if one
would be loved by too-full hearts.
I teach you the friend in whom there standeth
the world finished, a husk of the good, - the crea-
tive friend who hath ever a finished world in his
gift.
OF THE BITE OF THE ADDER.
If ye have an enemy, return not good for evil:
for that would not make him ashamed. But prove
that he hath done you a good turn.
And rather be angry than make him ashamed.
And if ye be cursed, I would have you not bless.
Rather curse a little also !
And if a great wrong be done unto you, straight-
way do five small ones in return ! A horrible sight
is he who is oppressed by having done wrong un-
avenged.
It is higher to own one’s-self wrong than to
carry the point, especially if one be right. Only
one must be rich enough for that.
I like not your cold justice ; from the eye of
your judges the executioner and his cold iron ever
gaze.
Say, where is justice to be found which is love
with seeing eyes ?
Arise ! invent that love which not only beareth
all punishment, but all guilt as well !
Arise ! invent that justice which acquitteth
everybody except the judge !
But how could Ibe just from the heart ? How
could I give unto each what is his ? Let this be
enough for me : I give unto each what is mine.
OF THE PREACHERS OF DEATH.
Bull is earth of superfluous ones, spoiled is life
by the much-to-many. Would they could be
tempted away from-this life by ‘ eternal life.’
There are the terrible preachers of death who
carry about within themselves a beast of prey
and have no choice except voluptuousness or self-
laceration. And even their voluptuousness is self-
laceration.
There are the consumptive of soul. When
scarce born they begin to die and long for the
doctrine of weariness and renunciation.
They would fain be dead, and we would ap-
prove of their will ! Let us beware lest we
awaken these dead ones or damage these living
coffins! ...
Whenever they meet with a diseased or an old
man or a corpse they say : * Life hath been
refuted.’ .
But only they themselves are refuted, and their
eye that seeth only that one face of existence.
Wrapped in thick melancholy and hungry for
those little accidents which produce death they
wait with clenched teeth.
Or: they reach out for sweetmeats, and so doing
mock their own childishness: they cling to the
straw of their life and’ mock because they are
hanging on a straw. .
Their wisdom is: “A fool he who remameth
alive ; but to that extent we are fools ! And that
is the greatest folly of life ! ”
And let this be the teaching of your virtue:
“ Thou shalt kill thyself ! thou shalt steal thyself
away ! ”
“Lust is sin,”—the preachers of death say—
“ let us turn aside and produce no children ! ”
“Giving birth is toilsomesay the others—
“ Why give birth ? One giveth birth to unhappy
u«es only ! ” And they also are preachers o
death. .
Or: “ eternal life ” : that is the same unto me,—
if they only pass away quickly !
The Ethic of Nietzsche.
ITI'RIED'BIOH NIETZSCHE, who died at
A Weimar in August, had fifty-six years of
life, forty-five of life intelligent. In 1889
his brain gave way ; and for eleven years
he lived like a child in tho care, of his mother and
of his sister. No history of degeneracy accounts
for the disaster: his family was singularly hale.
The machine, as Ellis says, was too highly charged
with inherited energy, and worked at a pressure
which ultimately brought it to perdition.
Nietzsche was born in Saxony, the son of a long
race of Lutheran ministers ; but racially lie was
and knew himself a Pole, a Slav. He was
a brilliant student, aspired to universal know-
ledge ; and his force of character was early
manifest. In 18d9 he was named a classical pro-
fessor at Basel. He left his university to take
part in the war against France as nurse and
ambulance-bearer, being unable to obtain univer-
sity, permission to fight; and in the nervous strain
and hardship of war his sister finds the source
of subsequent ill-health. His mental .life was
markedly influenced first by Schopenhauer, then
by Wagner ; but in 187(5 he took his own course,
and maintained it* till the end. In 1880 he re-
signed his chair at Basel, and was liberally pen-
sioned. Thereafter lie wrote and travelled, de-
voting himself to the formulation and expression of
his system of philosophy. Gradually his fevered
brain lost control of its machinery. He developed
megalomania, and affirmed himself a unique per-
sonality—the Prophet of the New Man, the Over-
Man, Beyond-Man, Man-of-the-Future. “Inmy
Zarathustra I have given to men the deepest book
they possess,” he wrote; and in Nov., 1888, he
wrote to George Brandes : “I swear to you that
in two years the whole earth will be writhing in
convulsions. I am Fate (ein Verhaiignissj.” A
letter of Jan. 4, 1889, to the same friend, was
signed “ Der Gekreuzigte,” “ The Crucified One.”
A few days later he became permanently insane.
* * * *
That would not be wholly a fanciful parallel
which called Nietzsche , a modern Prometheus,
defying the Gods and flung into Tartarus. Only,
the fire which Nietzsche brought to men was not
stolen from Heaven, but flamed in his,own brain.
By the strength of his intellect, the styeep of his
knowledge, the stimulus of his personality, the
vitality of his teaching, he deserved the title of
Great. He has shaken the idols of civilised cen-
turies on their pedestals ; and though they may
neither fall nor totter visibly, the marks and
effects of Nietzsche’s blows remain. Those who
are fitted to receive his gospel receive and will
continue to receive it.
It is no absolutely new gospel: there are hints
and portents of it in a hundred places; but
Nietzsche gave it his own definite shape, his own
aggressive force. He was a. man of his time,
and he built on the accumulated ideas of his time,
as we all build. Complete originality of thought
or doctrine is impossible to any man born of man
and woman, living among men and women:
ancestry and environment mould us inevitably.
What can be said of Nietzsche is that his ancestry
and environment fitted him to swim on the crest
of the rising wave of human evolution, and lie
swam with the foremost—maybe a little in ad-
vance of the foremost. Before he succumbed to
his mortality, he had fought beyond the limits of
mortality fixed by the Past, and his sinking head
had seen a glimpse of the heights to which mortal
men might rise in the Future. He preached the
Humanity of the Future, and he showed the way
up.
What is a man to live for ? How can be get and
give the most and best from his life. Heaven and
Hell are chimeras, said Nietzsche: live for
Earth ! But which Earth—that of the senses?
that of the spirit engrafted on the senses7—for
Self or Others? for the. Individual or the Com-
munity ? For all, said Nietzsche ; since human-
ity embraces all. Live in sense and spirit “live in
Self for Others ; live with the Individual for the
Community ; live at your fullest to the utmost;
and, above all, Live f Dogmas of self-sacrifice to
gods or men are poisonous ; religions of renuncia-
tion on earth for heaven are seeds of death ; Live
and Rejoice in Life, and work for the Life to
Come on Earth, not in a mythical paradise ; strive
to the goal of the Earthly Future, work for the
Better-Man, the Beyond-Man, who can be made
by our effort and control as far superior to the
mail of to-day as the man of to-day is superior to
the ancestral ape.
The . Past is past, said Nietzsche ; the Present,
for all that we can do to help it, is also for the
most part Past; but we have the Future in our
grip to make or. mar: let us work for the Man of
the Future, and give our children’s children cause
to bless us. As a tree falls, so will it be; as a
man 1 ves, so will he die ; as a man dies, so will
his descendants be to all human eternity.—; —
And Nietzsche never married. But his brain-
children are worth many myriads of undistin-
guished body-children.
It is clear, preached Nietzsche,—it is admitted
even by the mass of intelligent people,--that
the sacrifice of the Earth which has invented
us to the Heaven* which we have invented
is a blunder and a crime. The gods are
dead ; yet their shadows remain, and for many
a long day we will have to go on fighting
those shadows. Nietzsche fought, and gave no
quarter. With close argument, biting satire, he
denounced that Faith which poisons life by teach-
ing that death is the only good. Above all things
lie preached the Gospel of Strength, strength in
one’s self, strength which gives nothing away to
nourish the horde of human weaklings, brain-sick
or body-sick; but which reserves itself for the
possessor, and gives itself freely for the Better
Humanity to come. Sacrifice for others ? yes ;
but for what others ? Not for the contemporary
rabble whose weakness and uselessness are fixed
beyond hope of benefit, for the consumptive
of soul and the diseased at heart, for the
cripples of the body and mind who swarm
superfluous in over-breeding cities ; but for those
who will profit by the gift of strength, for the
children in the womb of the Future. And, re-
member, said Nietzsche, that in order to give
strength, one must have it, mud; conserve it, must
not squander it without hope of return. Nothing
for nothing; but the something may be a well-
based hope.
In the strife of Individual and Community,
Nietzsche was all for the Individual. Vet the
secret of happiness, of getting the best out of
life, was not to live in one’s self, for one’s self,
absolutely ; but to live in one’s self, for one’s self,
that one might have more to give to others who
would be worthy the gift—-to the Men-Beyond-
Man of the Future. The regimenting and level-
ling of the democratic community Nietzsche held
in horror. Men were not equal; why should
the Better be sacrificed to protect and perpetuate
the Worse? The State with its equal regard for
all was at best a makeshift. Asylums and hos-
pitals for the past-living, past-curing, were waste
of strength which should be held to create more
strength. When a man is no longer fitted to enjoy
life, let him die, and make room for the living, the
alive. And if he is useless and afraid to die, let him
be destroyed. The pity given him, the effort wasted
in preserving him, are so much energy diverted
from the logical human task of nourishing a
stronger and bettor humanity. Everywhere in
Nature the fittest and strongest survive ; and it is
thus only that Man lives at all, because he is the
highest product of earthly evolution. But where
Man has power, led by false religions, false morals,
he deliberately defies the law of Nature, permits
and encourages the unfittest to survive. That is
useless in the Present; and it is a crime against
the Future. Let us be ruthless as Nature, and
suffer only the strong to live.
Whence Nietzsche praised war and the warrior
as the embodiment of strength : in the warrior’s
destruction, he held, is the germ of better
creation. Whence he counselled the Self-
development of every individual to the highest,
careless of the claims of weaker men to
existence except in so far as they were co-ordin-
ated with self-development and the interests of
the Beyond-Man to come. Whence he preached,
in a word, that enlightened selfishness which
takes from life all it can get, —just as does the
brutal selfishness which underlies the conventional
morality of self-sacrifice,—-but which'is differen-
tiated from brutal selfishness because it takes to
give—to all who are worthy the gift. Thus in
gaining one would be giving, and in giving one
would gain strength to give more.
* * * •
This suggests in part some of the tenets which
Nietzsche advocated at his best epoch. His teach-
ing is included in a dozen or more volumes, of
which the collected German edition is not yet
complete, and which have been only partially
translated into other languages. The develop-
ment of his thought was consistent, but the books
which contain his thought at different periods
are not always consistent with one another or
with themselves.- Often one meets a half-
truth and has. to look far for the com-
plement. The vital truth which Nietzsche
saw clearly, and which is the spirit shining
through his imperfect expression of its details, is
that when the Christian religion and its congeners
were intellectually overthrown, the Christian mo-
rality fell with them. And upon its ruins Nietzsche
has erected a system of human ethics to which
there are many sentimental objections, but to
the kernel of which no conclusive intellectual ob-
jection has yet been devised..

Various Verses.
A Melb. street ruffian was recently given
three months for using a sling-shot on a home-
going citizen, with hospital results. On a subse-
quent charge of having the sling-shot in his pos-
session he got six months. So it seems that, in
the eye of the law, it is twice as criminal to carry
a dangerous weapon as it is to use one !
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to tneir advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buver of all other Australian erems. *
Dunedin (M.L.) City Council follows the
offensive practice of taking its garbage down to
the Heads and dumping it overboard at ebb-tide.
The filth, or a deal of it, gets washed up on Port
Chalmers beach, and that borough’s inspector
eventually “went for” the unclean city, laying
three informations. The S.M. decided, however,
that the by-law under which they were laid was
inapplicable, so the mephitic practice will con-
tinue as aforetime. If Port Chalmers could dis-
cover a law under which it could legally take its
garbage up the harbour and drop it opposite
Rattray-street wharf it might do more than any-
thing else to bring Dunedin’s dirty city fathers to
their senses.
The blessings of the weird system which
divides one ordinary-sized city into 10 or 27 or 43
municipalities turn upj in unexpected places some-
times. Richmond (Vic.) council desires to grant
permits to establish bonemills, fellvnongeries, and
emporiums for the boiling of tripe, pigs’-trotters
and cow-heel in its suburb, so that the conse-
quences will float down the Yarra and make Mel-
bourne sit up suddenly in its sleep. And it flatly
refuses to take any notice of Melbourne’s objec-
tion to the smell of pigs’-feet and cow-heel on the
ground that Melbourne is a different municipality.
The charming fiction that things don’t smell the
same in a different municipality is one of the bye-
products of the system, so to speak,
At the trial of the Boer Lieut. Cordua,
shot at Pretoria for conspiracy to abduct Lord
Roberts, it was admitted by the prosecution that
one Gano was sent out as a tempter (agent-provo-
cateur), and the legal agent of the British Govt,
endeavored to justify this act on moral grounds.
In replying to the contention of accused that he
had been induced to enter into the conspiracy by
the English detective, the prosecuting lawyer de-
clared that the Govt, was justified in resorting to
such expedients, and quoted Australian Judges as
a ffording precedents in holding that the police were
entitled to lay traps for the purpose of effecting their
ends. “Australian Judges! Good on you,
Tim ! What a noble advertisement this ghost
of the Bromley-Dungey case is for our bleeding
country !
The Open Road.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wine to the brim of the goblet, and no one to
whisper, ‘ 1 Stay ! ”
Coin and enough to squander, and no one to say
me nay;
Never a gamble questioned, and never a kiss
denied—
If this is your vaunted freedom, let me be chained
and tied.
Never a wife to wait one, and never a child to
cheer,
Never a love to follow, and never a God to fear ;
Never a pride behind one, and never a hope
before —
If this is your vaunted freedom, go back to your
chains once more!
I would be done with the laughter, and follow the
human cry,
As a freed horse follows the teamster that turned
him adrift to die ;
I would be done with the careless life that is all of
it nought but care,
I would barter this gilded freedom for the cruellest
chains that were.
Hither and thither and outward, journeying near
and far,
With never a range to bound one and never a sea
to bar,
Never a home to hold one, never a toil to claim—
If this is your vaunted freedom, by God ! I am
sick of the name !
There stretches one way to freedom—Courage !
O, trembling hand !
—And how can a man go roving, laid six feet under
the sand ?
And how can a man go roving, heaped earth at
his head and his feet,
—No room for a world to beckon, no room for a
heart to beat !
N.s.w.
WILL OGILVIE.
A Motley Legion.
A motley legion has come from the region
Beyond the utmost sea—
Saints and sinners, losers and winners—
What can its purpose be ?
There’s a white-faced priest, and a score at least
Who follow on his singing,
Knights and churls, and boys and girls
Of fair up-bringing.
There’s a Beast to the core, with at least a score
Who wait on his nod,
Fighting and chasing, fleeing and facing,
And laughing at God.
And the rest are a rabble, that drivel and gabble,
And shout or are dumb,
As the frenzied priest or the turbulent Beast
Seems to o’ercome.
Praying and wrangling, healing and mangling,
What can their purpose be ?
Saints and sinners, losers and winners
—The name of the legion is ME.
N. S. W.
D.

Yankee Doodle at the Front!
An incident in the Boer war as related by a returned Officer.
regiment of our Australian infantry was travelling (as depicted in
the smaller photograph) to Belmont. At Graspan we were
ħ
h
ĥ
entertained by the
garrison of Gordon
Highlanders sta-
tioned there, and
when taking our
departure by rail
were accorded the
customary musical
send - off by the
garrison band,
which, on this occa-
sion, consisted of
two drummers and
a piper. As our
train moved off I
heard the cry on
all sides from our
boys —'‘Yankee Doodle!!’ ‘Yankee Doodle!!’ and there in the distance
was an almost exact representation of the Yankee Doodle tobacco brand
so familiar to all Australians. The band after seeing us aboard were
returning back along the road to camp, and as we passed they sturdily
marched and played, whilst we, with the familiar tableau before us
reminding us so of home, cheered wildly. I instantly took out my kodak,
and, although the train was moving fast, secured a fairly good picture,
with the idea that perhaps my friends (the Dixsons) in Australia would like
to have it. On many a weary mai’ch afterwards, when smoking the army
regulation tobacco, did we long for just one pipe of the old far-off favorite.”

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 10, 1900.
A Policy for the Common-wealth.—The
Federal Public Works System.
When the Federal Government has hung up
its legislative hat in its legislative halls, and
assumed formal control, it will presently
have to develop a Public Works policy. As
owner of Australia’s postal, telegraph, and
telephone service, as its principal railway-
maker, and possibly its principal harbor-con-
structor, it must needs be a mighty builder
throughout the land. Whether it will build
for cash or on tick is a question which has
to be left to its own good sense to decide.
Hitherto it has been in some sort of way a
daily-paper axiom that the Australian is a
weak-kneed, degenerate creature, with a
streak of cowardice in him which makes it
impossible to hope that he will pay his way
and take thought for the future as a
Britisher or American, or any other superior
being, might possibly do. Newspaper pro-
prietors and Fat Men generally have de-
manded that public works shall be built out
of loans so as to save them a little immediate
taxation, and have laid it down as a gospel
that the people won’t stand being taxed suf-
ficiently to pay their way. The people have
been told so often that they are too limp to
stand enough taxation to pay their way (t hough
it isn’t put in exactly these words) that
they have almost come to believe it. When
a Government a shade more honest than the
rest borrowed a little less than usual for un-
productive works, and of necessity taxed a
little more to make up the difference, the
people didn’t really notice any change, but
the Fat Man’s papers told them they were
tottering under a huge tyranny, till they
thought they noticed it. So they threw the
more-honest-than-usual Government out of
office, and the next one took off the extra
taxation and went back to the extra borrow-
ing. The people didn’t notice any relief,
for the relief was really imperceptible, but
the Fat Man papers told them that they
were now able to breathe again after being
bowed down to the dust by a crushing load
of imposts, and being told this so often the
people at last almost began to feel as if they
had been relieved. The Fat Man press has
made the cultivation of national cowardice
and the discouragement of self-reliance one
of the leading features of its policy—with the
worst kind of result.
* * * *
There was never any need for Australia to
borrow a single copper for public works ; and
though the past, unfortunately, can’t be un-
done there is no need for Australia to borrow:
a single copper more. The business doesn’t
pay. Victoria is, in proportion to popula-
tion, the least indebted of the Australasian
States, and this is how its record stands : It
has borrowed in all about £48,000,000 —that
is the amount it received in cash, though it
owes about £49,000,000. It has already
paid in interest about £43,000,000, and the
country is young yet; in another 20 years
the same £48,000,000 of debt will have cost
about £80,000,000 in interest. New South
Wales has borrowed about £65,000,000, and
has already paid about £43,500,000 in in-
terest ; Tasmania has borrowed £8,400,000
and nearly £5,600,000 of it has already gone
in interest. The heavier recent loan flotations
of the two latter provinces have kept their
borrowings further ahead of their interest
payments, but taking the three together
very nearly the whole of the influx of
British capital in the shape of Government
loans has gone back to England already in
the shape of interest payments. In about an-
other five years it is probable that the three
States will be able to look back on their past
financial record and make this solemn re-
mark :
If we had built all the public works (railways, tele-
graphs, fortifications, public buildings of all sorts, har-
bors and sundries) in N.S.W., Victoria and Tasmania out
of taxation we would not have required to tax our people
to the extent of one penny more than they have paid in
taxation under the borrowing system, and we would not be
burdened with £125,000,000 of debt; nor would we be
loaded down with an annual and eternal interest liability
of £4,375,000 a year. All this incredible burden is money
wasted and utterly lost for the want of a little courage
and a little foresight. There isn’t a penny more in the
three States now than there would have been if we had
never borrowed anything at all. The money which came
from England in loans has all gone back to England in
interest, and left us just where we were—except that we
owe £125,000,000 with nothing to show for it, and have
to pay £4,375,000 a year with nothing to show for it.
♦ • • - *
The trouble arises out of the fact that the
men of years gone by wouldn’t face their re-
sponsibilities when they were young, and if
some of them are still alive and have to face
them when they are old it is only poetic jus-
tice that they should do so. If they put their
policy into actual words they probably
said something like this: “If we build
£50,000,000 worth of public works in the
next half-century out of our own means, that
signifies that we must pay £50,000,000
within the half-century out of revenue. If
we build £50,000,000 worth of public
works in the next half-century out of bor-
rowed money that also means that we must
pay £50,000,000 (for interest) within the
next half-century out of revenue, and there
will be the stupendous drawback that we
will still owe the £50,000,000, making
£100,000,000 in all. The difference lies in
the fact that if we pay for the public works
out of revenue, we and our children will pay
about equally. If we pay for them out of
loans, we will pay less and our children will
pay more. And if it is a question of making
things a little easier for ourselves at the ex-
pense of making them an enormous amount
harder for our children we decide, without
hesitation, to make them easier for our-
selves. In a general way our verdict is :
Damn our children ! ”
• * • •
And these fine old characters of 20 and 30
and 40 years ago went in for having a good
time, and damned their children cheerfully.
Here is the combined record of N. S. W., Vic-
toria, and Tasmania at four different dates
in their career :
1870.
Influx of Loan Interest .. .. £1,202,288
Money.. .. £1,809,600 Balance— Money
coming into
the country .. 607,312
£1,809,600 £1,809,600
Then the boom grew bigger and more
riotous, and this is how things were with the
three States a decade later :
1880.
Influx of Loan Interest .. .. £1,762,960
Money.. .. £3,738,638 Balance — Money
coming into
the country .. 1,975,678
£3,738,638 £3,738,638
Then the boom grew larger and wilder still,
and for the last year of the next ten N.S. W.,
Victoria, and Tasmania showed these re-
sults :
1890.
Influx of Loan Interest .. .. £3,629,453
Money.. .. £7,788,690 Balance— Money
coming into the
country .. 4,159,237
£7,788,690 £7,788,690
Up to this time the three States had adhered
manfully to the policy of floating larger and
larger loans, so as to cover the outflow of
interest and leave a balance of borrowed
money over to keep the boom going. But
suddenly Britain became chary about lend-
ing, and what made the smash, so far as
concerned N.S.W., Victoria and Tasmania,
three years later was this :
1893.
Influx of Loan Interest.. .. £4,625,275
Money .. .. £2,782,905
Balance going
out of the
country .. 1,842,370
£4,625,275 £4,625,275
The simple and sufficient reason why the
collapse was worse in some places than in
others was that the States didn’t come to
that famous temporary stoppage in their
borrowing frenzy with equal suddeness.
The respective figures were these :
1893.
New loans Interest -Mpf- i oag
coming in. going out.
N.S.W. ..£1,929,580 .. £2,425,987 .. £496,407
Victoria .. 606,721 .. 1,876,138 .. 1,269,417
Tasmania .. 246,604 .. 323,150 .. 76,546
* * * *
There has been a balance going out of the
country ever since 1893. It has been more
or less according to circumstances ; in some
years an extra spurt of foreign borrowing has
made the outflow smaller than in others, but
the outflow is always there, and it is always
large. Also, as Australia can’t make
up its mind to leave off borrow-
ing, the outflow always tends to grow
larger. Superficial people of a consolatory
disposition point to the fact that the railway
earnings cover a large part of the interest
bill, but that makes absolutely no difference
in the result. Whether the money that goes
out of the country in interest is taken from
the people in railway charges, or income-tax
charges, or in any other charges, it is the
same old outflow and the same old drain on
the country’s aggregate resources. Forty
years ago, when the borrowing disease
was still very young and the bill hadn’t
yet come home to roost, there were neither
land nor income taxes in N.S.W., Victoria,
or Tasmania ; nor was there any need of
them. Now all three provinces have got
them badly, and the struggle to pay interest
on borrowed money is the reason. If all
three provinces had levied land and income
and other taxes at their present rate about
1860, and applied the proceeds to the build-
ing of public works, and used the net revenue
from these works (which would not then
have been swallowed up in interest) for the
construction of more public works, it is
quite probable that the three provinces
would have had all the public works they
have now, and have had no debt at all,
and no interest to pay, and they would be
about able to abolish their land and income
taxes by this year 1900, instead of struggling
to find new taxes to put on. If the shiftless
and irresponsible and shoddy old pioneers
had taken the same care for their children
that they did for themselves, and had been
willing to carry their fair share of the tax
burden, that is how things might have been.
It is pleasant to remember that some of
these persons lived unreasonably long, and
the burden they shirked when they were
young has fallen on them with an extra thud
when they are old.
* • * •
And as to how things are : When the
Federal Parliament takes over the business
of railway and telegraph and many other
kinds of construction, it has to decide
whether it will be another shoddy pioneer
or not. If it likes to double the direct taxa-
tion now existing, and use the extra income
as a Public Works Fund, it may find in 30
years that the debt has considerably dimin-
ished, and that the income from new works
constructed out of revenue will keep the
Public Works Fund going to such an extent
that the new extra taxation, as well as a lot
of the old taxation, can be taken off perman-
ently. On the other hand, it may go on as
at present, in which case the extra taxation
will come along just the same —a little later,
certainly, but, on the other hand, when it
arrives it will come to stay, for it will be
required to meet the further enlarge-
ment of the interest bill, and the interest
bill goes on for ever. In fact, the
present generation has just the same
question before it that the last one had.
If the Federal Government decides to build
£150,000,000 worth of public works, in the
next 50 years or so, out of revenue, it has to
find £150,000,000 out of revenue in that 50
years to do it with. And if it decides to
build £150,000,000 worth of public works in
the next 50 years out of loans, it will have
to find about £150,000,000 in the same 50
years out of revenue to cover the interest,
with the stupendous additional disaster of
having £150,000,000 of permanent debt
at the end of the period. It will have to
pay about the same sum out of revenue in
the same time whether it borrows the money
or whether it doesn’t borrow it. If it
doesn’t borrow, the burden will be spread
pretty evenly over the half-century, and
there will be po £150,000,000 debt at the
end of it. If it does borrow, the burden
will be a little lighter at this end of the
half-century and an enormous amount
heavier at the other end, and there will be
a permanent liability of £150.000,000 after
that. The beasts that perish, having no
foresight, would doubtless adhere to the
old loan policy on the ground that it
makes things a little easier to-day, and
though it makes them a vast amount worse
to-morrow r , that is a thing for to-morrow to
reckon with—for we are only beasts grazing
in the fields of the immediate present, and
there is no certain future. TheFederalParlia-
ment is expected to be a little higher than the
beasts, so it may, perhaps, view the matter in
this rather different light : A public work
costing £1,000,000, if built to-day out of
revenue, only costs £1,000,000. The same
work, if built out of foreign loans at 3| per
cent., costs nothing to-day, but within the
next 50 years it costs £1,750,000 in interest
alone, and in 100 years it costs £3,500,000 in
interest, and if the money is repaid at the
end of that time it has cost £4,500,000 in
principal and interest. And the million
of local capital thus “set free for private
investment,’' as the phrase goes, lies idle
in the cellars of some bank, and isn’t in-
vested, and earns nothing. It isn’t a question
as to whether Australia can afford to pay its
way without loans. The much larger and
more urgent question is whether it can
afford to go on floating loans —whether it is
rich enough to pay millions, or 9 mil-
lions, or 18 millions, for a railway worth
only a million. Also whether any nation of
this size is rich enough to keep fully
£20,000,000 over and above its real require-
ments lying idle in its banks, and costing
fully £700,000 a year in interest, without
yielding any return whatever.
* » * *
For very many years Great Britain has
been gradually paying off its back debts
while Australia has paid off nothing—the
small amount contributed from revenue to
various sinking funds has been more than
counterbalanced by new debt incurred to
make good revenue deficiencies. There is
no special reason why one branch of the
Anglo-Saxon family should go up in a
financial sense while the other goes down
—save this reason : Britain strikes its popu-
lation with direct and indirect taxes (local
and national) to the extent of about
£3 10s. 6d. per head per annum. Aus-
tralia and Tasmania levy taxes (local
and national) to the extent of about
£3 2s. 6d. per head per annum. That extra
Bs. per head makes the whole difference.
If Australia had taxed itself at the British
rate from 1860 onwards it need never have
had any debt. If it taxed itself at the
British rate now it could gradually free
itself of its burden. Unluckily it has come
to be a boast in some of these States —espe-
cially in New South Wales—that they are
among the most lightly-taxed countries on
earth ; and they are getting deeper and
deeper into the mire through trying to live
up to this record. The Bulletin trusts
that the Federal Government will up-end
these things. If it can truthfully say, two
years hence, that Australia is one of the
most heavily-taxed countries in the world—-
that the heap of useless cash in its banks is
at last being used to some good purpose—
and that it is employing the proceeds of (he
extra taxation wisely to obviate further
borrowing and to pay off the liabilities of the
dead and malodorous past, that will be
something worth boasting about.
George Reid’s Dry Dog.
George Reid, the remarkable old gentleman who
was once Premier of N.S. Wales, went on a
crusade recently as far as Adelaide, to tell the
people about the beauties of the Freetrade policy
which he never dared to practise during his long
term of office. In the Australian States which
have tried Protection the majority is mostly for
Protection, which seems to suggest that the policy
is one that can stand inspection. But the one
State where the majority howls for Freetrade and
votes for Freetrade is a State which never
tries New South Wales has never
had Freetrade this century. It is about 70
years since it had even pure Revenue-
Tariffism. It hasn’t many large manufacturing
industries of the kind which are exposed to
foreign competition, but those it has are almost
all protected. Its sugar-growers, brewers, tobacco,
cigar and cigarette manufacturers, confectioners,
wine-makers and various other industrial workers
are all protected, some of them very heavily. Its
penny daily papers are protected to the extent of
50 per cent., some of them by gratis special trains,
and all of them by differential postal duties.
These are not revenue imposts in any sense;
and if George Reid had believed in his own
political lie that Freetrade made the country
richer he would surely have taken these off
and made the country richer, and gained credit
thereby. Even supposing that real Freetrade is
impossible for reasons connected with the revenue,
and that pure Revenue-Tariffism is the nearest
approach to it that can be looked for George
Reid’s situation is curious. He was put in office
with a huge majority to establish pure Revenue-
Tariffism. He and his party laid a large hairy hand
on its joint and several waistcoat-button and swore
that Revenue-Tariffism meant wealth, and that
Protection meant poverty, misery, and wretched-
ness. They had a chance to make themselves
solid with the people, and to get a permanent hold
on their billets and salaries, by making N.S.W.
rich and prosperous by pure Revenue-Tariffism.
Yet they retained a whole lot of Protectionist
duties ; by their own solemn swear and assurance
they wilfully damaged and wronged the country
and themselves; and they persistently protested
that they were practising Freetrade when they
were not. Finally, George Reid himself, in
almost his last bid for office, practically threw
overboard in words the creed he had already
thrown overboard in deeds, and declared on a pub-
lic platform that his faith in Freetrade had waned
grievously since he came into office.
And now the great political impostor is stump-
ing Australia preaching again the faith he
practically abjured, and telling of the advan-
tages of the policy he didn’t follow, and bidding
for the position of Freetrade Federal Premier in
order that he may again practise Protection sur-
reptitiously, and deny that he is doing it. At
Adelaide the Great Wobbler told the people that
the first essential was to vote for men who,
whether Freetraders or Protectionists, had honest
convictions. Judging by his own record the only
honest conviction G. Reid will ever have is when
he is fined at the police-court some day for for-
getting to pay his dog-tax. Also he informed the
public that the fact of a thing being old is in itself
pretty satisfactory evidence that it is rotten, and
seeing that practical Freetrade dates back to the
times of the Missing Link, before man had indus-
tries to protect, or sense to protect them, the
statement seems reasonably true. He told them
that “trusts” were impossible in England be-
cause of Freetrade, which, in view of the huge
trusts that have been growing up in England
within the last two or three years, is the sort of
assertion which only a person with the brassy
assurance of the great Freetrader who practised
Protection would be capable of. Also he went on
like this :
South Australia has had the great national blessing of
protection for 14 years. The baby ought to be off the
breast by this time, but if they tried to take even 1 per
cent, off the duty they would hear how the infant
squealed after it.
According to this wondrous logic, the fact that a
country which lias tried Protection finds it good
and sticks to it is a proof that protection is
BAD.


There was no mistake about it, there was no
smarter, more enterprising, more capable people
in this world than the people of Victoria. (Cheers.)
They used to be able to run rings round New South
Wales until they got salvation. (Loud laughter.) He
would show what they were doing now. He would be
merciful to Victorian friends, and instead of comparing
Victoria with New South Wales he would compare her
with little Tasmania. During the last nine years Tas-
mania has increased her population hy several thousands
more than Victoria from 1891 to 1899. Victoria’s increase
was only 30,000 ; Tasmania’s was 37,000. (Cheers.)
According to this, the fact that one Protectionist
State has increased faster than another Protec-
tionist State proves that Protection is bad.
In 1870 Victoria had 228,000 people more than New South
Wales. In 1899 New South Wales had 193,000 more than
Victoria. (Cheers.) He did not forget the greater size of
New South Wales. He was only showing that Victoria,
with all her resources and her grand people, had not
made this protectionist policy a brilliant success.
(Cheers.) If any people in the world could they ought to
have done so. (Cheers.) From 1891 to 1899 the arrivals
in New South Wales were 30,000 more than the depar-
tures, while in Victoria the departures exceeded the ar-
rivals by 127,000. It seemed cruel to throw puppies into
water, but they learned to swim. (Laughter.) In 1861
the Victorian trade was double that of New South Wales.
In 1899 the New South Wales trade was £54,000,000
against Victoria’s £36,500,000. In 1871 the New South
Wales shipping trade was only 145,000 tons more than
Victoria. In 1899 it was 1,650,000 tons more. (Cheers.)
More men were working to-day in the factories
of New South Wales than Victoria. There were more
women at work in Victoria.
It sets one wondering again why George Reid
didn’t drop that much-talked-of pup into the
water and let it swim. For not only did he keep
on protecting many of the N.S.W. industries, but
he was the greatest spoon-feeder of industries
with borrowed money in eastern Australia. In
his five years of office he spent on an average
£1,500,000 a year of loan money in finding work
for, and paying wages to, the people of N.S.W.,
against the Victorian average for the same period
of about £300.000 and the Tasmanian average of
about £160,000. He found constant sustenance
with borrowed cash for over 100,000 people, in-
cluding those directly employed by the State and
their direct dependents, and saying nothing of the
multitude of tradersandothersindirectlysupported
by these 100,000 —while Victoria only spoon-fed
about 20,000. Turner practically cut off the supply
of loan subsidies and told his dog to.swim and be
self-dependent, while George Reid dry-nursed
about one-twelfth of the population directly
and one-sixth directly and indirectly with ex-
traneous assistance. The difference between the
increase of population in N.S.W. and Victoria is
almost wholly accounted for by the people
whom George Reid coddled with loan funds as
compared with those that George Turner didn’t
coddle. All the days of his political life the
Great Political Fraud went about with his dog
under his arm, and preached about the advantage,
of dropping it into the tank and letting it care for
itself.
* * . * *
And, after all, things went none too well in
N.S.W., probably, because George Reid’s Protec-
tion was only half-hearted and his mode of carry-
ing his dog unscientific. Wages, as shown by
Coghlan’s annual statistics, went steadily down.
Thirty millions of cornstalk money disappeared
through the slump in property-values. There
was no visible . decrease of prices when
Dibbs’s duties were taken off. The num-
ber of women employed in N.S.W. fac-
tories more than doubled in George Reid’s
spell of office, and he says that is a bad sign.
The total value of all the products (agricultural,
pastoral, manufacturing, &c.) of N.S.W. de-
creased a little, while those of Victoria in-
creased. It is impossible to mark out any
area of 87,000 square miles in N.S.W. which
can even begin to compare in wealth with the
87,000 square miles of Victoria. Victoria’s com-
merce has certainly not increased as N.S.W.’s has
done; it decided long ago that it was better
to manufacture its own products than to be a
porter on a wharf, piling its raw material on to
foreign-owned ships and unloading finished pro-
ducts that come back in return. Yet in propor-
tion to population, Victoria’s Bommeree is now 50
per cent, larger than England’s, and England’s
commerce is the pride of Freetrade doctrinaires.
Also, N.S.W.’s commerce is smaller in proportion
to population that that of S.A. or Westralia.
And in proportion to the area of the country,
Victoria’s exports are more than twice as large
as those of N.S.W.; there has to be some relation
between exports and area, for the wheat, wool,
beef and other produce exported necessarily depend
on the amount of ground there is to grow produce
on. When last heard from N.S.W. had 9000 more
paupers than Victoria —the figures are given in
the latest issue of Coghlan’s “Seven Colonies.”
Victoria can impose a legal minimum wage and
maintain an Anti-Sweating League to ensure that
its workers shall be decently paid. N.S.W. has
neither ; its spoon-fed industries, apparently,
can’t afford to pay a legal minimum wage. The
fact that the only Australian State which can
venture to abolish starvation wages by Act of
Parliament is also the one which has the most
scientific Protective system is the grandest
advertisement that Protection could well ask for.
* * * -*
Protectionist Victoria, like Freetrade Britain,
has of late lost some of its population by excess of
emigration. Its trouble is temporary ; it only
commenced when George Turner resolved to
drop his dog into the water by practically ceasing
to spoon-feed the population with loans and
pay them wages with borrowed money. Britain’s
trouble is constant —at least it has gone on now
for nearly a century. When a comparatively
crowded Protectionist country like Victoria (for
Victoria is enormously crowded compared with
the other Australias) exports population, it shows
the awfulness of Protection; when a compara-
tively crowded Freetrade country like Britain
does the same, it shows the energy and adven-
turous spirit born of Freetrade. And N.S.W.
kept its population, partly because that great
spoon-feeder, George Reid, found them employ-
ment and wages out of borrowed money, and
partly because he found them employment and
wages in protected industries while bitterly
cursing Protection, except on the famous occasion
when he turned round and faintly cursed Free-
trade instead.
* ¥ * *
The Bulletin would like to have this constant
controversy about the relative merits of partially-
protected N.S.W. and wholly-protected Victoria
put on a more understandable basis. At present,
it is a controversy without rules. So it asks-these
two questions:—
(1) Is it an understood thing that a Protectionist State
of 87,000 square miles must hold more people and grow
more produce for export (without the ground to grow it
on) than a Freetrade State of 310,000 square miles, else
Protection is no good ? If so, what comes of the, fact that
the Protectionist United States and Germany hold more
people than Freetrade Britain ?
(2) When an allegedly Freetrade State Which doesn’t
practise Freetrade supports 70,000 more people directly,
and many more indirectly, out of loan moneys than a
Protectionist State, is that a proof of the advantages of
Freetrade ?
George Reid, with his perfectly dry dog under
his arm, and his impassioned lectures about the
advantages of dropping the animal into the water
and letting it swim, is requested to clear up these
points.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Ears and Tail of George Reid.
At Albury the other day George Reid—
N.S.W.’s good old Borrowing George -the man
who carries his own dry dog under his arm and lec-
tures on the advantage of dropping the animal in
the water so as to teach it to swim, and who
preached Freetrade while retaining so many Pro-
tectionist duties in his own tariff -passed off as
new a very weary chestnut indeed. He wanted
to why know, if Protection was good for all the
Commonwealth, it wasn’t equally good to have a
separate Protective tariff for each State of the
Commonwealth, or for each country, or each
town, or even for each street.
The Protectionists have given themselves away by talk-
ing so much about intercolonial Freetrade, because, if
Freetrade was good between provinces, surely its appli-
cation between countries was to be commended.
And by the same process of reasoning, if it is
good to have a revenue tariff barrier round all
the Commonwealth (and Borrowing George says
it is) then it is equally good to put a revenue
tariff barrmr round each State or town.
And if it is good (G. Reid has always
professed it is) for N.S.W. to have
an army, and a Parliament, and a mint, then that
proves that each township in N.S.W. should have
its own separate army and Parliament and mint.
And if it is good for there to be a Premier and a
Governor, and a military boss-commander in
Sydney, that proves, according to the logic of
Borrowing George, that there should be a Premier
and Governor and military commander in every
town and hamlet in the province. George Reid
is a rather plausible person to those who come
within the range of his baleful eye-glass, but when
his logic is analysed in cold blood he is a poor,
silly dodderer at best. Boiled down, he is some-
thing like this :
(1) You (the Protectionist party) want Australia to have
one Protective Tariff, one Federal Parliament, Federal
capital, Governor-General, military commandant, army,
&c. You are therefore fools. If it is good to have these
things for all Australia, then it must be good to have a
separate Protective Tariff round'each town, and a separate
Federal Parliament, Federal capital, Governor-General,
military commandant, &c., in each town.
(2) I want Australia to have, one Revenue Tariff, one
Federal Parliament, Federal capital, Governor-General,
military commandant, army, &c. Therefore I am a fool.
If it is good to have these things for all Australia, then it
must be good to have a separate Revenue Tariff round
each town, and a separate Federal Parliament, Federal
capital, Governor-General, military boss, army,.&c., in
each hamlet. In fact, we are both, by my own showing,
exactly the same kind bf fool. Only I am the supercilious
variety of ass who wears a superior sneer and scorns the
other ass for having exactly the. same kind of ears and
tail that I wear myself.
And here is another sample of George Reid’s
ears and tail
You (the people of South Australia) have had this great
national blessing (Protection) for 14 years. The baby
ought to be off the breast by this time, but if you try to
take 1 per cent, off that duty, just hear how that infant
would squeal.
And N.S.W., according to the man with the glass
eye, has had the blessing of Revenue Tariffism for
much more than 14 years, and is as little able to
do without it now as when it started, and if
deprived of it, its squeals, we are assured, would
be something awful. .Also N.S.W. has coddled
its people with loans for 50 years, and has got
the biggest debt in Australasia; yet judging by the
heavy increase of borrowing when Reid was Pre-
mier, it is as little prepared to do without that
coddling now as it was 50 years ago. Isn’t Reid’s
own infant nearly off the breast yet ? Isn’t
his dry pup ever going to swim ? And N.S.W.
has been protecting itself against Chinamen
with a heavy poll-tax for many years, yet it
is no more able to do without that Protection now
than it was at the first. As an even worse case,
Britain has been protecting itself with a large
navy for some centuries, and is no better able to
dispense with a navy now than at any other time.
All these things prove that South Australia should
never have had Protection, and N.S.W. should
never have had Revenue Tariffism, nor should it
have poll-taxed Chinamen, and Britain should
never have had a navy. The figure of speech
about the infant may be summarised thus
(1) After a long trial of Protection you are so well satis-
fied with it that you want to keep it. That, proves Pro-
tection is very bad indeed,.
(2) The only way you can prove that Protection is good
Is by abolishing it. But if you did that I would, of
course, say that that also proved Protection to he very
had indeed, and that you wouldn’t have abolished it un-
less it was a failure. At the same time you prove it is a
failure by not abolishing it.
(3) Yet if you tried Freetrade for 14 years, and the
Freetrade infant was still at the breast then and would
not let go, that would prove Freetrade was very good
indeed. For the argument that applies-to you doesn’t
apply to me by arty means.
And so the pot looks scornfully through his eye-
glass at the kettle, and wonts to know what the
kettle means bv it.

The Victorian Election.
So far as can be gathered from the evidence yet
available, it would seem that the hours of the
M‘Shiels Ministry in Victoria are numbered.
When M ‘Lean and Shiels, and the “intelligent
man ” Fink, and the rest of their little party of
recalcitrant Liberals, rose in sudden revolt against
the Liberal Government of George Turner, and
ejected it per aid of Duncan Gillies and his
Conservative following, they struck an unholy
alliance that was bound to lead to trouble. The
following which kept them in office.consisted of a
party of Liberals who, complained that the pro-
gressive Turner didn’t progress fast enough, and
a party of Tories who regarded Turner as a
howling red revolutionist, who wanted to tear up
the Constitution by the roots and devastate the
land. The M ‘Shiels Government started to trot
giddily along the razor-edge of impossibility by
pleasing both these parties. For the fag-end
of the old Parliament things went well enough.
Duncan’s following of reactionaries voted steadily
to keep in a Government which professed to be
more aggressively Liberal than the one Duncan
G. had helped to turn out. It didn’t present its
bill for payment too suddenly. But Parliament
expired, and the M'Lean-Shiels Government
had to go to the country with what it professed
was the most Liberal programme that the country
could ask for, and with the cordial support of the
worst Tories in Victorian politics. And the
people couldn’t help suspecting either that Gillies
supported M‘Lean because his programme wasn’t
so progressive as it looked, or else because the
programme was never to be carried out. The
cloven hoof of Gillies and the end of
Gillies’ tail seemed to be visible underneath
the document somehow. The spectacle of
M‘Lean, the alleged economist, recommended by
the Boom-budget man, was too much like the
progress down street of an intoxicated teetotaller
with a bottle sticking out of his tail pocket. On
present appearances the Turner Liberals muster
one more vote than the M‘Lean Liberals and the
Gillies Tories combined. Among those who are
gone are Postmaster-General Watt, Baronet
O’Loghlen, the dreadful Keys, of Dandenong,
Hamilton, of Bendigo, and (deeply regretted)
Hume Cook. Deakin, Murray Smith, and G.
D. Carter retired, and in each case a follower of
Turner secured the seat. Of the three old land-
boomers who tried to get back Matthew Davies
was so badly licked that he all but lost his deposit;
Munro was rejected by 321 votes ; and Bent
actually got in. But Bent, who comes in as a
follower of M'Lean, adds nothing to the strength
of the party, having merely displaced barrister
Moule, of the same way of thinking—at least
Moule thinks that way, and Bent is that way
without thinking, but he might think if he
had the apparatus. Among the new can-
didates whose defeat is a loss to the com-
munity are Kemp, who tried to oust that
political disgrace Frank Madden ; Randolph
Bedford, who failed to shift Samuel Staughton ;
Gibney, who found Pursey Chirnside too heavy
to lift; and H. H. Champion, who nearly got
there at Albert Park, but didn’t quite arrive.
Whether Champion would have been a success in
practical politics it is difficult to say, but anyhow
the spectacle of Bent in and Champion out is a
melancholy reflection on somebody or something.
Sydney’s Little Branch Railway.
The latest Sydney railway proposals of the Lyne
Government, as more fully expounded by
Attorney-General Wise, look reasonable, all
except one point—the usual point. It now appears
that the line, which has hitherto come to a dead
stop at Redfern, is to be extended parallel with
Kent-street, through the resumed area, and finally
carried over the harbour, to connect with the
North Shore system. This means that the main
Australian line will now run straight through
Sydney. There will be little extra cost of resump-
tion, for the railway will be built most of the way
through property already resumed. The buildings
to be destroyed are principally of the kind that
have been crying aloud for demolition any
time these last 20 years. The new order of
things, instead of wrecking a beauty spot like
Hyde Park, will reconstruct and improve
one of the most wretched and dismal localities in
the city, and a great part of the extra values
created by these improvements made at the public
expense will be extra values added to the public’s
property. The scheme looks most commendable
—with one remarkable exception. Tim Lyne
Government apparently can’t get rid of the idea
that Sydney’s principal railway depot should be
away at the back end of the town, so it is proposed
to construct a huge new main establishment at
Redfern, right alongside the present one, and
serve the business portion of the city with two
little wayside stations —one near Bathurst-street
and the other near Erskine-street. This seems a
vast absurdity. Sydney wants its huge,main
station about Erskine-street or in the neighbour-
hood of Wynyard-square, and the existing place
at Redfern should be a wayside station, like
that at Newtown or any other populous
suburb. Then it would be only necessary to
build one large station and one small one—not
a large station and two small ones. By the pro-
posed ai’rangement it appears that the Melbourne,
Brisbane and Western expresses will still start
from Redfern, and presumably end their
inward journey there; it is impossible to
guess why a new main station is wanted
at Redfern unless the long - distance trains
are to stop and start from it. It would be
absurd to make a lug, expensive main station, and
then have the traffic run right through it as if it
was a wayside platform out in the backbloeks,
with cows looking in at the window of the ticket-
office ; and to turn the little by-station down by
Kent-street into the principal place of arrival and
departure. So, after all the recent explanation
and elucidation, it appears that it is still intended
that all important trains shall have their terminus
in the great metropolis of Redfern, and any pas-
sengers who want to visit the casual, bushy ham-
let of Sydney shall take another train along a
branch line or extension till they reach their
destination. The habit of looking at Sydney as a
rising township, with grass and goats in its streets,
which is really entitled to a branch and a small
station of its own, seems quite incurable.
Some People -with Bricks.
Certain misguided individuals have commenced
to flood N.S.W, with literature like this —sample
from Bathurst National Advocate -
Thus it will be seen that whilst it is mandatory that
the Federal Parliament shall establish a.capitaLin N.S.W.
100 miles from Sydney, there is absolutely no provision
can force N.S.W. to give up one inch of territory for the
capital when she does not choose to. The Federal Parlia-
ment might choose Albury as the.capital; but N.S.W., by
refusing to give up any Albury territory, could make it
impossible for the capital to be located there.
The position then really is that N.S.W. and N.S.W.
alone, has the power of choosing the capital site. It
must be 100 miles from Sydney.
Bombala openly professes to rely upon the support of
Victoria to obtain the capital. Her reliance will be found
to have been misplaced. Victoria has no more voice in
the matter, further than what N.S.W.’s courtesy allows
her, than Tonga has. And the voice of N.S.W. will, we
feel assured, be practically unanimous in favoring the
Western district, for such a choice would at once serve
the highest Australian patriotism and the best interests
of the mother colony.
This sort of thing is likely to end in some pretty
hard swearing on the part of somebody. Suppose
N.S.W. in general takes up this idea. Suppose
the Federal Parliament decides on a site
(in N.S.W., of course, as per agreement)
for the Federal capital. Suppose N.S.W.’s State
Parliament replies that it has decided to have the
Federal capital somewhere else; that it won’t
surrender any site except the one it has selected, '
and that the capital must be there or nowhere.
And suppose the majority replies : “The Consti-
tution says the Federal Legislature shall sit in
Melbourne till it arranges-about the new capital in
N.S.W. There is no date fixed for removal. There
is no date fixed for selecting the Federal capital.
The drinks are very good here and the beef is ever
so much better than on Sydney side. When you feel
inclined to hand over the Federal capital site we
have selected, even if it is 500 years hence, just
let us know, and the work of building the new city
will start at onco.” The Bulletin sees, as in a
vision, a short, stout, hatless man, covered with
dust, leaving a city on foot, in great haste, pur-
sued by many people with bricks. The man isi
George Retd, who went down ’to Melbourne and
demanded the insertion of the clause which fixes,
things this way, and wouldn’t federate till lie got
it. And the people following him with the
geological testimonial are some friends of his.
Extract from the annual report of Sydney Cham-
ber of Manufactures, issued towards the end of
last year, being part of the chairman’s speech :
With Federation now practically accomplished, we
should see that the advantages claimed for N.S.W., and
admitted by the other States, are secured at the earliest
opportunity. Provision is made that the capital shall be
distant not less than 100 miles from Sydney, and that the
required area shall be given to the "Commonwealth ; it
therefore seems reasonable that the voice of the people of
N.S.W. should be heard in the matter. Undoubtedly no
site will be acceptable to the great majority unless its
natural port is Sydney. If a site like Bombala is chosen,
there will undoubtedly be a rival port established at Eden,
and the result would not only be division of trade, but a
great Waste of both private and public money.
Also, from Sydney Mail of 18th Aug. :
The proposed (Oarcoar) site creates no fresh and compe-
titive port inimical to the commercial interest of Sydney
—Queen City of the South —one of the greatest trading
centres on earth, and possessing the finest harbor in the
Southern Hemisphere. By maternal right she demands
the bulk of the Federal city trade.
The Sydney bagman-community’s jealousy of
Melbourne and Adelaide was pitiful enough, yet
it might pass as a sort of parochial patriotism.
But the Sydney bagman’s demand that the
development of another N.S.W. port shall be
blocked, in order that the port where his drapery
warehouse is may profit at the injury of another
part of his own State, is a squalid example of
selfishness and personal grab. The Sydney bag-
man is not only against the other States of Aus-
tralia, but he is bitterly hostile to all of his own
State outside of his own shop-door. N.S.W. has
practically only two ports now—Sydney and New-
castle. And the Sydney bag-fiend is mad at the
idea of his own State growing great enough to
have three, lest it should take 2d. out of his own
mean little pocket.
Jawbone Neild’s Children’s Protection Bill in
N.S.W. Assembly is going to punish parents who
“procure young children for the purpose of
soliciting alms.” That’s all right as far as it goes.
But what about the “beloved pastor” who sends
the lambs of the flock round from door to door
collecting threepenny bits for the Sunday-school
picnic, missions to the heathen and what not ?—or
about the Sydney charity manager who sends
broadcast through the post invitations to parents
to send out their youngsters to beg pennies for
“thousands of poor children ?■” These things are
simply a liberal education in mendicancy, and the
child who has been well-trained in collecting pence
for the Sunday-school, the heathen, or the
Benevolent Society, will have no difficulty in col-
lecting on its own account when it wants money
for lollies or toys—always under the pretence o”f
helping some “worthy object,”—and will thus
rake in a good deal more than the poor little
wretch who honestly or dishonestly avers that he
only wants money for “mother, who’s sick at
’ome.” . Both classes of juvenile beggars are as
often as not frauds ; but the well-dressed and
well-fed youngster is the greater nuisance of the
two, and a fitter subject for the application of
Neild’s bill,
>
S.A. Leg. Council has once again rejected the
Closer Settlement Bill—providing for compulsory
re-purchase of large estates —which Kingston lias
always made a main plank of his policy. This
time the Tory majority is larger than ever, 14
members in a house of 23 voting on Fat Man’s
side. Immense areas of splendid S.A. farming
land are held by a few big absentees and support
about one-tenth of an inhabitant to the
sq. league the vast Mootak estate at Mt.
Gambier, owned by a wealthy person in
England, is a notable case in point. Kingston
framed his measure almost exactly on M.L. lines,
and he could not have done better. Under such
legislation the Seddon Ministry has acquired the
Cheviot and similar big estates, and placed
thriving communities where previously there was
but a small population. S.A., which is burdened
with monopolists such as the South Australian
Co., Angas, Browne and Riddoch, is in worse
case than even Maoriland was, considering that the
latter has very large tracts of fine country, whereas
S.A. has rich lands only in patches. The Tory
monopolists, when Kingston first brought his
Bill along, went in for obstruction and delay, but
now that the spineless Holder is at the head of
affairs they have contemptuously thrown out the
measure. History is repeating itself. Last year
the Council rejected Household Suffrage, Closer
Settlement, Early Closing, and Workmen’s Com-
pensation. Already this year the first two have
been destroyed, and it is safe to say that the other
two will either suffer the same fate or be mutilated
beyond recognition.
*
George Reid’s statement that the N.S.W.
Premier—who is his political opponent, Bill
Lyne — is entitled to be the first Federal
Premier may be generosity towards a rival,
but as the last man who found George Reid in
a politically-generous mood has probably died of
old age before now it is much more likely that
it is an attempt to foist on the Federal Protec-
tionist party the most incompetent leader he can
find, to the party’s undoing. If, by an appeal to
provincial prejudice or otherwise, Reid can
persuade the N.S.W. Protectionists to demand
that their own unlucky Bill shall lead the party
in the Commonwealth Parliament, so much the
sooner will Reid’s own hour come. It is a
beautiful thing when you can persuade the
enemy’s army to let you select a general for it.
Just now there is a scarcity of front-rank men
among N.S.W. Protectionist politicians, and
Reid’s danger is that the party may desire to
follow a brainy chief from another State—a man
who could hold the fort against Reid and all his
fish-stories indefinitely. His appeal sounds some-
thing like this: “My dear friends, do let me
persuade, you, from the noblest motives, to insist
on having for your leader a man who will bring
your policy into discredit by his bungling, and
whom I can lick more easily than I can any other
Protectionist who is in the running for the Pre-
miership. Don't choose a man of the calibre of
Kingston or George Turner, who would wipe
the floor with me, and who would associate Pro-
tectionist policy with good, clean and successful
finance and government. Let me nominate a
commander for you—one who will hang like a
millstone round your neck, and enable me to point
out that Protectionism is synonymous with in-
capacity and failure.” This is a world where
even the noblest intentions are apt to be miscon-
strued, and this paper misconstrues the noble
Same Old George as above-mentioned.
S.A. House of Assembly has rejected the Holder
Government’s Bill for creating a popularly-
elected convention to draft a new State Constitu-
tion. It might have been otherwise if the Pain-
fully Thin Man had fought in deadly earnest for
his scheme, but Holder isn’t that kind of a
fighter. The proposal which met with more ac-
ceptance was that the making of the new State
Constitution should be done by Council and As-
sembly sitting together, thus giving each politician
a chance of saving himself and sacrificing his
neighbor. The Assembly has ignored the great
principle which underlay the Federal move-
ment —that it is for the people to de-
cide the nature of the Constitution under
which they shall live. Changes in the
several States are the necessary outcome of a
Federal union, and why should not the people
finish their work? When the people were com-
petent for the great Work of directly creating a
Constitution for all Australia, they are equally
capable of the much smaller work of draftings,
scheme of government to suit the altered con-
ditions of a mere State.
