MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess's Grand Opera Season.
lIER Majesty’s “The Rose of Persia.”
Theatre Royal “ The Absent-Minded Beggar.”
; uou Rickards’ Variety Co.
Exhibition Oval Fitzgerald’s Circus.
I linders-Street Wirth’s Circus.
Hedda Gabler, by the big Norwegian
gentleman who forgot to get his hair cut, did a
two-mghts season at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, last
week. Ihe season was thin as well as short, and
the emotions it left behind were conflicting
Ibsen has been variously described as a Genius, a
lum bug, a Crank ; probably he is something
?r tt and a little of a Diseased Bore as well.
His Hedda Gabler is a bundle of anomalies, doing
large and middle-sized insane things from ridi-
culously small motives. She is an intensely self-
centred woman, yet her great desire is to influence
the life of somebody or anybody else. She cares
nothing at all about Lovborg, the brilliant author
and glittering drunkard ; and she cares nothing at
all about kind, ordinary little Mrs. Elvsted, who
reclaims him from the beer that is long and the
rum that is short; and she is blankly indifferent
to the great and learned tome which he has written
about the causes of hash in Jupiter, or something
of the sort. But she beguiles Lovborg back to
drink, and when he has painted Christiana bright-
red, and has lost his irreplaceable manusciipt out of
his unsteady tail-pocket while whooping outside a
house of ill-fame, she burns his life’s work. She
has no real enmity towards him, or the book, or
the woman who reclaimed him ; she merely desires
to influence the course of somebodv’s life. She
could do as much by tying a rope across the street
after dark , when a man falls over a rope and
breaks his leg it often turns his life into an entirely
new direction. Then she lends the frantic author
a pistol and tells him to commit suicide “ beauti-
fully ” —to be a nice corpse, and not mangle him-
self or make an ugly splash on the floor. This be-
guiling of Lovborg to a beautiful death is a curious
contrast to her previous beguiling of him to a
miserably ordinary drunk. When she hears that
he has gone to a brothel, and there howled angrily
and coarsely at a woman of ill-fame, and then shot
himself clumsily in the abdomen, she is enraged.
She is struck with the dismal idea that there must
be something wrongabout herself, in a picturesque
or poetic sense, when the man whom she drove to
suicide dies by gunshot in the unromantic lower
stomach. So she hollers for a while in the next
room, and pounds out a loud tune on the piano,
and then shoots herself.
* * * m
Hedda Gabler is a quite unusual character.
Being quite unusual, Ibsen may have studied
human nature more deeply than other men, and
found possibilities and motives which they missed.
Or he may have invented a crudely-impossible
female out of a badly-kinked brain. He may have
drawn an animal with a fidelity never before
known, or he may have pinned the front of a
camel and the back of a goat together and made a
beastly and impossible Joss, which excites the
wild admiration of people who know no better as
a most accurate picture of an animal that doesn’t
exist. This writer thinks he has made a Joss.
People have looked at a sketch of a unicorn and
remarked how true it was to nature; and they
have looked at Hedda Gabler and said the
same thing. Also people have considered a jim
jam seriously and been impressed by the ac-
curacy of the drawing and coloring. The verdict
of this scribe is that Hedda Gabler is the jim-jam-
of a delirious dramatist who is drunk with his own
hair. People who assume that she is a human
possibility are impressed with the fidelity with
which she is drawn, just as people who start by
taking it for granted that there is a sea-serpent
may be staggered at the amazing likeness of the
sea-serpent of the illustrated paper to the one they
took for granted.
• * • •
Nance O’Neil did General Gabler’s sour
and disagreeable daughter mostly in a pale-yellow
dress, a pale countenance, and some pale-yellow
hair dragged tightly round her head. All the
same, she played Ibsen’s jim-jam exceedingly
well. There isn’t a gentle or womanly spot in
all the jim-jam’s hard character—and, as already
mentioned, Miss O’Neil played the part admirably.
Thomas Kingston made a big success of Lovborg;
and Joseph Carne’s George Tesman was a real
human being among much unreality.
* * * *
A brief spell of “ School for Scandal ” fol-
lowed, and The Bulletin desires to speak some-
what kindly of Miss O’Neil’s Lady Teazle, which
has improved in some particulars. Came and
Kingston, as the brothers Surface, were about
equally excellent, but as these two reliable artists
have never yet left a trusting Australian audi-
ence in the lurch, this is only what was expected
of them. Four characters who may be classed as
respectable were M‘Kee Rankin as Peter
Teazle, Barton Hill’s Oliver Surface, Plimmer as
B. Backbite, and Mrs. Bracy, who was rather
more than respectable in the part of Mrs. Candour.
Likewise Miss Roxy Barton was an agreeable
vision as Lady Sneerwell; while Miss Jose-
phine Thynne made a small, plump, mat-
ronly Maria with a painfully weak voice,
about as unlike the usually, accepted version of
that lady as possible. The screen was unusually
high at one end—the end Miss O’Neil was con-
cealed behind. If the advt. keeps faith with the
however, the royal and vociferous drama
of Queen Elizabeth ” will be on deck by the
time tliese remarks get into print, and the present
programme will have gone to join a certain late
politician’s debts in oblivion.
** * »
A great crowd that was dressed very
fashionably and behaved very badly went to see
the Rev. George Walters’ new play at Sydney
Palace on Thursday night, youths of noble blood
having to sit on the steps, and clergymen in all
parts of the house shedding a sacred influence
round the heathen temple. The play was much
wanting, in unity, and was still more broken by
the passing of vegetation over the footlights. No
actress could be five minutes on the stage without
a miniature flower-garden being reached up to her,
and even when there was no one on the stage the
flowers were passed over just the same. “Thou
Fool ” proved to be a sort of drama wherein the
second act had nothing to do with the first,, and
people wandered in and out irrelevantly, and
seemed much more important than they after-
wards turned out to be. It tried to be a variation
of “Joseph of Canaan,” a Sunday at Home tale
and a modern novel of the Ellen Thornycroft
Fowler school at the same time. The pious father
turned his son out. of doors because the
son did not believe in what they pronounced
as “Gard,” and immediately the son became
ragged and starved. As he was a strong, healthy
young man there seemed no great reason why he
should starve in this sudden and astonishing
manner,, except that his friend’s wife might
rescue him. The friend’s wife was a very nice
girl, but she did not seem to mind going astray at
all. The rather original agreement arrived at
was that she should go along to the starving
man’s place to-morrow in a casual sort of way,
and stay there perpetually as the unmarried Mrs.
Savage. The wife’s lady friend also thought it
a very nice arrangement, so it was left for the
hero to have remorse on the eve of his going
astray, and to wonder whether he would be a
fallen man or not. He also saw visions of his
mother about this time.
* * * »
Mr. Fred Wilson, as the married man,
spoke in a muffled voice never heard on any but
amateur boards, but the young man who nearly
sinned through a too-obliging disposition was
fairly strong. The actresses were mostly tall,
beautiful girls, and when occasionally small were
beautiful all the same. There seemed a good
number of them with nothing very much to do
except to look tall and beautiful. The summing
up of all this is, that “Thou Fool” is too frail a
thing to be worth hitting seriously. Nobody cares
to smite a confirmed invalid.
** * *
John F. Sheridan opens Sydney Royal in
petticoats on Saturday with a “Trip to Chicago.”
** # »
Mr. Will Whitburn appears nightly at the
Tivoli as De Rougemont with imitation legs
astride his turtle, while his faithful dog walks at
his side. Fanny Powers improves weekly, while
mimic Whelan adds to his repertoire a scarecrow
dance in imitation of Johnny Coleman, The
creator of Ta-ra-ra ” keeps to her old items,
and has not once fallen over herself in her wild
prancings round the stage in the glad abandon of
being out on the “ Ran-dan-dan.”
** * *
“Robbery Under Arms” still continues
to teach the youth of Australia that bushranging
is sin, though only one of the bushrangers comes
to a nefarious end, the other two being allowed to
settle down and marry their best girls. They are
not even expected to restore the sovereign which
they nobly took from the rich man to give to the
poor girl that had only ’arf-a-crown. All the same,
Jimmy Governor had better not entertain any
hopes on his own acoount.
** * #
Without doubt, last week’s production of
“ Lohengrin ” has given new dignity and import-
ance to Musgrove’s grand opera venture. At the
end of their six weeks’ season Melb. will begin to
look forward to a return of the opera forces next
year with “ Tannhauser,” “ Cavalleria Rusti-
cana, ‘Pagliaeci,” and perhaps “Un Ballo”
and one or two other florid Italian works which
the younger generation has never heard. The
second season promises to be even more interesting
than the first. 6
** * #
“Lohengrin,” the fourth item in George
Musgrove’s opera-season, was a surprise to the
many people who didn’t expect a highly-finished
productmn .of Wagner’s stately fairy drama.
Melbourne is speaking reverently on the subject.
The word has gone round that G. M.’s latest en-
tertainment is worth more than the prices of
admission, and Melbourne means to make the
best of a good bargain. Considered as a musical
show, Lohengrin,” at the Princess’s, is poetic
and impressive, and, of course, somewhat dull at
certain points. Harmonious in the sense that
stained - glass windows in old cathedrals
are harmonious, and great with the great-
ness of Beethoven’s heaviest symphonies,
lhe Wagnerian opera-drama, like the sym-
phony of the Apostle Beet., will always
irmte the objection that its meaning
might have been more forcibly expressed in fewer
notes. It goes slow, and it extends over the time
required for catching one’s last train. Wagner’s
scheme of “music of the future” is already an
admittedly solid success, despite a leaning to-
wards brazen blastfulness, but m his composition
of opera for the future the Bavarian thinker did
not foresee this blessed age of short stories, meat-
lozenges, and other condensed novelties. Wag-
ner’s mysticism in four acts seems needlessly
tedious by comparison with the swift operas of the
new Italian school, quite as dramatic, picturesque
and coherent as the respected “Lohengrin,” and
more in sympathy with vocal art. The finest
voices in the world will never sing praises to
Wagner.
* • • »
Conductor Slapoffski is an enthusiastic
boss of this notable production. He knows his
“ Lohengrin ” well, in all its lights and shades.
The 40 or more instrumentalists bring out beautiful
effects of orchestration, the chorus people are well-
trained to their important part, and though the
opera may suffer in expert German estimation by
the lowering of the pitch and the omission of, per-
haps, some weird instruments of the trumpet
family, Australians in general will not crave for a
vaster volume of sound than the finale of the first
act. Costumes are rich, the scenery by Coleman
and Goatcher is good, and the careful stage-man-
agement showed only one or two faults the first
night, chiefly in the cold, mechanical lining-up of
the lady-choristers.
* * * *
Moreover, the principals get through their
heavy tasks in a capable manner. Louis Arens,
the tenor, who was wanting as Faust, is better
suited to the part of Lohengrin. His health has
improved, and his voice, round and pleasant
within its limits, is seldom asked to sing above the
northern boundary. The broken English of Arens
worries one, but his figure, in silvery armour, is
attractive, also the distinct enunciation of Lohen-
grin’s girl, Elsa, may be said to tell the story for the
two of them. Miss Lorraine, young, fair and buxom,
sings the Elsa part nicely and acts with good dis-
cretion. Max Eugene is the full-voiced villain of
the fable, and Miss Crichton is his wicked spouse.
They give more colour to the plot than to the
music. The lady in her first costume looks very
like the Queen of Spades, and Max’s melodrama-
tic rage is considerably overdone. Charles Til-
bury, well made up, is a majestic King of Ger-
many in voice and bearing, and the ripe baritone
of Lawrence Mooney makes the Royal Herald’s
announcements go for all they are worth. The
Princess’s bill for the remainder of this week is
“Trovatore” to-night (Thursday), “Lohengrin”
on Friday, and the popular “Carmen” again on
Saturday. Next week “ Bohemian Girl,” or
“ Maritana,” or both will be produced, and these,
with further performances of “Carmen” and
“ Lohengrin ” presumably, will carry the season
to the end of its six weeks.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s they promise to get
along all right with “ The Rose of Persia,” in face
of the fact that grand opera at the rival house has
set up a trying standard of comparison for the
lighter work. Slapoffski’s orchestra deals tenderly
with the singers, whereas the less numerous band
at Her Majesty’s almost drowns the voices in some
cases. Melbourne is moderately warm in its ap-
proval of “The Rose.” If the ballet were
left out the approval would drop 15 degrees,
and this not on account of any particular weakness
in the performance, but because there is nothing
that bices the public fancy. Even Lauri’s per-
formance of the intoxicated Hassan doesn’t bite,
though it tickles one artistically. Apart from
Lauri the show lacks character. The pretty
music _ is too much of a muchness, and muchly
reminiscent of Sullivan’s earlier work. Kenning-
ham has not enough to sing or say, and Brownlow
is too serious and beautiful a Sultan for the job.
A leading lady who could do something more than
sing would give the opera a friskier aspect, but on
the other hand Miss Winston Weir’s voice is a
high-class article, which is more than can be
said for some of the other ladies. Concerning
Carrie Moore the present scribe has nothing new
to say. She is neat, winsome, tuneful and clever,
but her physique carries no weight. She ought to
devote 12 months to the task of putting on flesh
and importance.
** # *
The return of Bland Holt to Melb. Royal
is such an auspicious event that The Bulletin
feels inclined to pardon the prodigal’s first offence.
It is the offence he committed in Sydney three
months ago, and he was bound to repeat it at his
first Melb. opportunity. “The Absent-Minded
Beggar ” is a drama with nine toes in the grave.
Somebody wrote it hastily for the London market
last year, at a time when any sort of S’African
war show would pass muster, provided that the
right Flag got a triumphant waving in the last
act. Now that the Flag has been waved more
than enough, and the Boers don’t look nearly as
black as they were cabled in Nov., ’99, and
Kruger is a sorrowful, seasick person of no
importance, “ The Absent-Minded Beggar” ought
to be buried in a Jingo’s grave. All the
same, Melbourne has got to put up with this
awful thing for Bland Holt’s sake. “The
Beggar” has nothing in it to speak of, except a
hundred or two stage soldiers, thirty horses, a
barouche, and a remarkable baby. At one time a
figure which purports to be Y.R. goes through a
form of reviewing the forces in Hyde Park, and
subsequently a bogus “ Bobs ” and a spurious
Buffer review them at Pretoria, on which occasion
the combined brass bands play “ Rule Britannia”
instead of “God Save.” Walter Baker, Styan,
Albert Norman (as an unaccountable Boer), and
Brown shift from England to South Africa in the
course of the evening. Also, Bland Holt has a
foolish character to support, and Miss Florence
Seymour regrets that she is Norman’s mother, and
Miss Ross kisses the baby. Mrs. Holt, disguised
as a bugler boy, scores an irrelevant success for a
few minutes. Miss Harrie Ireland is another
joyful accident. In fact, the drama would deserve
more sympathy if it didn’t make a miserable pre-
tence of having a plot to work upon. The casual
interlopers who accept no responsibility for any-
thing that happens are the brightest features of
the business.
** * *
J. C. Williamson has commissioned
Hazen, of Sydney, to go forth to Italy and pro-
cure an Italian Opera Co. of as good vocal quality
as the co. which Martin Simonsen imported 13
years ago. Hazon came to Australia to conduct
the orchestra for that co.
* * #
The big tent of the Fitzgeralds, at Exhi-
bition Oval, was crowded again on Saturday, so it
seems that the best site for an old, reliable circus
is just where it happens to be pitched, as per
advt. Last year Lizzie, the elephant, scoffed her
light refreshment and trod her tricycle in the
Eastern district of Melbourne. This time she is
addressing the electors of the Northern suburbs
through her trunk, and the popularity of that
growing young female is not less marked. All
the same, there are nicer girls than Liz. in Fitz-
geralds’ Circus. Adelina Antonio, who lately
returned to Australia after fulfilling a long
engagement in Heaven, or somewhere, is the
most graceful trapezist this country can call
to mind. Also the lady juggler has
an appearance which adds interest to her
juggles; and Rosie Aquinaldo is probably the
most fascinating little contortion actress on earth.
Rosie looks fresh and pretty in 26 different shapes,
but her smile is not seen at its very best until she
twines both legs tenderly round her neck, and
chides her backbone for its absurd mutability.
The Dunbars, who sometimes bring off their
cleverest feat at the first attempt and sometimes
score three failures, are a favorite turn, and the
high-jumping act reduces the horsey populace to
cheers.
4t * * *
Wirfch’s Circus, which started business
last Saturday in Flinders-street, at the place
where the Fitz. canvas was spread of yore, is not
quite such avast affair as the rival institution, but
it is big enough to satisfy any reasonable appetite.
There are many gee-gees and much clever riding
in Wirth’s ring. One rider, Welby Cooke, is as-
toundingly clever. Also the elephant that shakes
mortals off its back, and a performing baboon, like-
wise the boxing ponies and the bear on horseback
are acceptable novelties as circus novelties go.
Mons. Soukie in a cage with some uneasy-looking
tigers (“ forest-bred ” the programme calls them)
appears to run a risk of getting chawed, and
Australia in general likes to feel alarmed on the
other fellow’s account. Take it altogether,
Wirth’s is a good entertainment, with only a
family semblance to the other circus. If there is
any time o’ year when Melbourne can provide
large audiences for two important circuses this
should be the time. But the time is not long,
anyhow.

announces that George D’Albert will make his
first appearance in Australia next Saturday.

It is still a matter of dispute whether the
New South Wales Treasurer has any right to include
Darling Harbor rents in his revenue, as no provi-
sion ha 3 been made in this year’s estimates for pay-
ment of this year’s interest on the price of the property ;
or whether they shouldn’t have been put to a suspense
account to pay the interest with—at least until the
resumed area became actually Government property.
Reckoning these rents as revenue N.S.W.’s State income
is £137,719 more than for the corresponding four months
of 1899 ; while reckoning only the same sources of revenue
which the State had last year the increase is £108,721. It
is a handsome increase either way.
The progress of the States is best estimated
however, by bringing the respective increases to a
common rate of so much per inhabitant per annnum. The
N.S.W. increase is taken as not including the Darling Har-
bor rents :

Messrs. Plummer Love and Co., 249 George-
street, Sydney, are large suppliers of all mining requi-
sites. They are sole agents here for the now celebrated
“ British ” brand of explosives, detonators and fuse ; also
for the Taylor Horsfield Improved National Rock Drills,
Air Compressors, etc., etc.*
* * * *
Since the start of the Broken Hill Proprietary’s
new 5000-ton mill, most of the Barrier mines have been
engaged in remodelling their concentrating plants on up-
to-date lines, and wisely show a preference for machines
which have proved most successful on the difficult fines
and slimes. The machines that can claim this distinction
are the “Luhrig” Vanners, as supplied by Messrs. W. &
J. Lempriere, of Melbourne. The South’s new mill will
include a dozen or so, and the Junction Co. has also de-
cided to erect a number of Luhrig Vanners as an impor-
tant adjunct to its large plant. Other mines would do
well to follow this good example.*

* * * *
Dear Bulletin.— He advt. in N.S.W. papers
requiring miners for Bethanga. Let me warn miners
against coming here, as there is only the one mine and
that is full-handed. Competent miners, after coming long
distances at considerable expense, are either told there is
no show or kept hanging round without getting a start.
Wages poor, ss. to 7s. 6d. per shift including shaft and
machine-men, in fact men are working on machines as
improvers at 6s. and 6s. 6d. per shift, The tunnel to the
shaft is 1700 ft in, and it’s all ladders to 500 ft, there being
no cage, and the time from starting to return to tunnel-
mouth is nearly 9 hours.—Miner.
Last year, in N.S.W., ’95 per 1000 coal miners
were killed, 1'94 per 1000 silver-lead miners, and "99 per
1000 gold, copper, and tin miners. The blood-bill in the
English coal-mines was 1'283 per 3000. Next to Broken
Hill, Cobar is the N.S.W. field which costs most life ; and
Chief-Inspector Slee explains that this is probably partly
due to the fact that conditions in those places are so bad
that skilful and experienced men prefer to work and liv e
elsewhere.

Three States (N.S.W., Queensland and Westralia) publish
monthly expenditure accounts. Those for Westralia are
only available to the end of September ; for the other two
States they are published to end of October. Figures are
as follows:

Queensland has £114,835 less revenue and £170,643
more ordinary expenditure, so things are £285,478 worse
than they were as this date last year. Westralia, for the
first three months of the financial year, had a revenue in-
crease of £72,955 and an expenditure increase of £157,872,
so things were £84,917 worse with it at 30/9/1900 than at
30,9/’99. N.S.W. has, for the four months, £108,721 more
revenue (not including income from resumed property)
and £53,310 more expenditure (not including interest
payments in connection with resumed property), so its
ordinary revenue account is £55,381 better than at 31st
October, ’99. But the huge increase in loan expenditure
more than counterbalances this. Altogether the year
has opened none too well—especially in Queensland,
which appears to have struck a memorable snag. This
is the time when a Treasurer with a genius for cutting
down expenditure shines, bub Queensland apparently
hasn’t got one.
* * * *
Every time a new N.S.W. Ministry is formed,
one of the first items of business is to issue a big batch of
Treasury Bills to cover the further additions that have
been made to the deficit. Lyne now asks Parliament for
authority to issue another mass of these temporary
securities to cover Reid’s shortages, and so the game goes
on, and the big deficit grows bigger. This time it is to
be hoped that it will be a complete cleaning-up—that it
will cover Reid’s unpaid accounts, his unpaid half-year’s
interest, and all his jobs and fakes ; also that it will make
up the entire shortage on the Civil Service Superannua-
tion Fund, Police Superannuation Fund, the Centennial
Park and Martin Place suspense accounts—in fact, that it
will be the final cleaning-up, and that no floating items
will be left to necessitate another cleaning-up later on.
N.S.W. is about tired of cleaning-up.
» » * *
The Queensland Government thinks of partially
following the example of Maoriland, Victoria, and South
Australia, and founding an apology for a State Land
Bank—to be called the Agricultural Bank. It is pro-
posed to raise money by the issue of bonds bearing in-
terest at not over 4 per cent., and to lend at 5 per cent.
Borrowers must commence repaying their principal five
years after the loan is granted, and repay it in full within
15 years. Nobody is to borrow more than £BOO, and no
loan must exceed half the value of the security. The rate of
interest is certainly lower than that usually charged by
private lenders in expensive Queensland ; still it should
be possible to borrow at 3J per cent, and to lend at
4£ if the thing is done on a large scale. But
it is proposed to do business on an absurdly
small scale, the advances being limited to £lOO,OOO.
The Philp Govt, is evidently weighed down with the idea
that a State Land Bank is a terrible and dangerous ex-
periment, and wants to fool gently with the sleeping
tiger. S.A., Victoria and Maoriland have all had their
State Land Banks in successful operation for years ; the
great Norwegian State Land Bank has existed and pros-
pered since 1852 ; the Russian Imperial Bank has been in
the same business since 1859 ; and other State and private
land banks are dotted all over Europe. But either the
Queensland Govt, doesn’t know these things, and still fan-
cies a Land Bank is a reckless and revolutionary proceed-
ing, or else it has grievous doubts about its own ability to
honestly manage even so simple a concern. It has such
grievous doubts about the permanency of its institution
that it reserves the right to start repaying the miserably
small capital of £loo,oooafter 6 years. Loans are only to be
made for the purpose of effecting improvements, and even
then only to the extent of half the value of the improve-
ments, and no attempt to supply cheap money all round,
and rescue the Queensland farmer on a large scale from
the costly private or joint-stock usurer, is contemplated.
The Queensland Govt, is probably too deeply solicitous
about its private usurer for that. If it went into the
mortgage business on a large scale—say to the extent of
£1,000,000 or £2,000,000 —it could probably run the busi-
ness at a cost of about 6s. or Bs. per cent, per annum on
the capital, at which rate it would be possible to lend at
4A- per cent, with profit. The very small institution now
contemplated will probably cost £1 per cent, on the
capital in expenses, and there will be no margin even if
the rate charged is 5 per cent. The small way in which
the Philp Government crawls at the democratic idea of a
State Land Bank is strangely different from the large way
it rushes with all its feet at its syndicate-railway jobs.

The Bank of Australasia’s balance-sheet for the
half-year ended 16th April, 1900, shows another great im-
provement, the net profits being £148,422, which com-
pares after this fashion with some previous earnings :
October, 1892 .. £80,346 October, 1896 .. £40,400
April, 1893 .. 60,742 April, 1897 .. 45,376
October, 1893 .. 60,607 October, 1897 .. 40,278
April, 1894 .. 48,571 April, 1898 .. 48,547
October, 1894 .. 45,735 October, 1898 .. 54,148
April, 1895 .. 40,854 April, 1899 .. 56,312
October, 1895 .. 41,159 October, 1899 .. 119,870
April, 1896 .. 40,568 April, 1900 .. 148,422
A dividend of 9 per cent, per annum £72,000 (the
’Asia paid 10 per cent, in 1892 ; 7£ in 1893 ; 6 in 1894 ; 5
per cent, in 1895, ’96, and ’97 ; 6 per cent, in ’9B ; 7 per
cent, in the first and 8 per cent, in the second half of ’99,
making 7J per cent, for the year ; and now 9 per cent.) A
sum of £15,000 goes in a donation to the staff, which
suffered somewhat in the bad times, so in the
good ones the management has decided to remember the
ox that treadeth out the overdraft. An addition of £40,000
increases the reserve fund to £875,000 ; £20,000 is written
off the book value of bank premises, and the
remaining £l42s, added to the previous balance of
£11,153, leaves £12,575 undivided profit to carry for-
ward. It is admitted that the earnings include sums
(how large is not stated) recovered from debts pre-
viously written off as bad, but even if considerable
allowance is made for this the improvement is remark-
able. The earnings shown represent the splendid
rate of £l2 3s, 9d. per cent, per annum on the
capital and reserve fund. Deposits and other liabilities
to the public are £17,461,412, and the cash and liquid
securities (£5,771,940) are equal to 33 per cent, on this.
* * * *
According to the cabled intelligence, the Eng-
lish, Scottish, and Australian Bank shows a large improve-
ment for the year ended 30th June, 1900, profits being
apparently £36,915, as compared with only £15,411 last
year. Owing to the condensed nature of cablegrams, it
is difficult to be certain of the amount; the ’gram says
that the profit is £36,915 “after putting £15,000 to
reserve-fund,” but this probably means that last year’s
profit of £15,411 was carried to reserve this year, as usual,
and that this year’s earnings are £36,915. Out of this
sum 1£ per cent, extra is paid to deferred stock-holders,
raising their interest from 3 to 4A- per cent., and share-
holders get a per cent, dividend—the first since 1892.

The Colonial Sugar Refining Co. closed another
profitable half-year on 30th September, 1900, and paid its
usual 10 per cent, dividend. The most remarkable
feature about this institution is the painful accuracy with
which it earns that 10 per cent, dividend every time.
Some recent results are these :
"SS 4 -
March, 1896 .. £85,167 £85,060 £lO7
September, 1896 .. 85,546 85,100 446
March, 1897 .. 85,473 85,100 373
September, 1897 .. 85,301 85,100 201
March, 1898 .. 85,726 85,100 626
September, 1898 .. 85,404 85,100 304
March, 1899 .. 85,581 85,100 481
September, 1899 .. 85,812 85,100 712
March, 1900 .. 89,408 89,254 154
September, 1900 .. 89,381 89,344 37
Either sugar is a most regular and accurate substance, or
else these are not always the real profits, and the ap-
parent accuracy is arrived at by adding to and taking
from certain inner reserves as occasion requires, or by
varying the allowance for depreciation according to cir-
cumstances, or by some other book-keeping process. The
reserves are now about £420,000, and the paid-up capital
about £1,786,880. It is proposed to increase this to
£2,000,000, and use the proceeds in paying off debentures.
When this is done the co. will be practically free from
outside liabilities.
* * • •
The Illawarra Steam Navigation Co. (Sydney)
had only moderately good fortune during the half-
year ended 30th September, 1900. The falling-off is set
down, in part at least, to the disorganisation caused by
plague proceedings, quarantine, and resumption by the
State of the co.’s property —still the earnings are only
£lOO less than they were at September, 1898, when there
were no plague-scare and resumptions. The half-year’s
profits are £2097, and the usual dividend of about 8| per
cent, absorbs £2490, which is £393 more than the earn-
ings. As a result, the undivided profit balance is reduced
from £507 to £ll4. The total reserves are now £27,114
(insurance fund £27,000 and undivided profit £114). At
March, 1896, the reserves were £32,160 (insurance fund
£31,483 and undivided profit £677), so not only has no
serious effort been made to repair the insurance fund, but
the dividends have exceeded earnings on the whole.
Under the circumstances the 8J per cent, dividend looks
a good deal higher than is fairly justifiable.
* * * *
Michael Flurscheim, the great theoretical re-
former, and one of the best-meaning men in the whole
world, has just been heard of from Maoriland. Flurs-
cheim selected Wellington as a suitable spot in which to
establish his Exchange Bank, which was to issue notes
payable in labor or goods, instead of in cash. A man who
accumulated £2O worth of Flurscheim’s notes, and who
found it difficult to get value for them, sued for cash pay-
ment. The theorist offered half in cash and half in soap.
But plaintiff didn’t want so much soap, and demanded
the sordid money of the Philistine. The court gave him
a verdict with costs, so the Exchange Bank idea may be
said to have broken down. Flurscheim, when he first
made the offer of half-payment in soap, ran a soap-factory
of his own, but that also appears to have closed up—in
fact, the break-down is seemingly complete all round.

Mr. J. C. Hassall, of 184 Queen-street, Bris-
bane, announces by advertisement in this issue that he
has ceased to be branch manager in Queensland for the
Ocean Accident and Guarantee Corporation, that co.
having made a rule that every branch manager must
represent it only, and Mr. Hassall having felt it impossi-
ble to comply with this rule. Mr. Hassall now repre-
sents in Brisbane the New Zealand Accident Insurance
Co., and the Scottish Union and National, the Cale-
donian, and the Mannheim Insurance Cos. Messrs.
Burns, Philp, and Co. also announce that the accident
business of the Scottish Metropolitan, which they
formerly represented, having been taken over by the
Ocean Co., they now represent in Queensland the acci-
dent branch of the Colonial Mutual Fire Co.
October’s top price for silver was the highest for
four years. Four things seem mainly responsible for the
firm silver-market —Bimetallist Bryan’s improved chances
in the U.S. Presidential fight (the result of which will
probably be to hand ere this is published); the discovery
that, despite the introduction of the gold standard in
India, the natives go on using silver ; improvements in
Mexican conditions, resulting in reduced exports of
Mexican dollars, and the ructions in the East (China and
the Philippines), where silver-payments are necessary.
» * • *
Broken Hill Town Council, in which Laborites
predominate, wins the first round in the long-threatened
Equity suit re the taxation of the mines—the Council
asks one co. alone to pay nearly £20,000. The co’s sub-
mitted, first of all, that the assessment had been impro-
perly made, that the valuers were incompetent, and that
anyhow they had not gone about their work properly;
but the Court holds that the rate-book is conclusive evi-
dence. The co’s, amending their statement, now put it
that the valuers returned valuations merely to fit in
with the predominating party’s ideas. So far—and the
struggle has apparently only begun the Council’s
costs have reached just short of £lOOO. And
when the cases are finished all that will be de-
cided will be that one particular method of arriving at
a mine’s taxable value is legal; it will not decide that no
others are legal, and the law is so vague that a score of
legal bases are quite possible. If N.S.W. Parliament
really does want to lessen the waste of public money in
litigation it ought, in the amending Municipal Bill, to set
out clearly the method that it does want adopted in con-
nection with this matter.

The Australian Metal Co., which did propose
to use the closed-down zinc works at Broken Hill for ex-
perimental purposes, concludes that it can do that sort of
thing cheaper abroad. It hankers after a successful
wet process.
* * * *
There is a mystery about B.H. Prop.’s an-
nouncement that it has sold 5000 tons first-grade concen-
trates for export. The story up to this was that the new
concentrating mill was needed to keep the furnaces going.
The move may be still another way of notifying S. Aus.
that her hold on the smelting industry is not particularly
tight—that, considering what a very big quantity of coke
this stuff requires in smelting, it will pay just about as
well to ship it off to a place where coke is cheap. It has
paid best all along to ship the product that has been
heaviest in zinc. And it may be that the sale is the
“ straight tip” also to the Port Pirie smelter employes to
drop their request for higher wages.
* * * •
Block 10’a developments at the reef level—
915ft.—are remarkably good. Drives have been run
along the ore for about 130 ft. In one end the stuff assays
13‘50z. silver, 15 per cent, lead and 16 per cent, zinc ; in
the other, 13'50z. silver, 16 per cent, lead and 17 percent,
zinc. This is a shade lower in silver, but 2 per cent,
higher in lead, than the average of the ore, broken from all
parts, now going through the mill. Certainly Block 10
has worked out a larger proportion of its ore above 715 ft.
than other of the bigger mines ; and the most interesting
question is—What is the value of this development as a
guide to the quality of the lode in the other mines at
915 ft. ? Perhaps it does not prove much more than that at
one point the lode at that depth is payable ; and though
is pretty fair to assume that if good ore lives to that
depth in one mine it will live also in others, there
is this other fact to make one go slow—the ores in
Block 10 above 615 ft. were much above the average in
richness, and it is at least likely that the ores below that
level are also richer than the average. The co. is now
exploring at 1015 ft. General-manager Warren, who has
been in charge from the beginning, and who pioneered
sulphide-concentration on a large scale, has just come
back from a round-the-world trip with the confession
that he has learned nothing in either Europe or America
about mechanical ore-dressing.

W.A. Mines : Things very unsettled ; looks as
if would be long ere stocks recover the positions of 7
months ago. If attention of London becomes mainly
diverted to Band scrip, as is likely, a big all-round slump
must take place in Westralians. Some will have to fall
one-half their present price before they reach a fair
value ; others will want to fall very little before they be-
come a genuine mining investment, and two or three
mines have reached that position already.
* * * *
S. Kalgurli: “ A portion of the furnace has caved
in.” Likely to happen to any furnace of this sort.
Putting up a bigger plant. Pity it wasn’t a bigger mine.
Before crushing, shares went to over £7 ; now less than
half that, and likely to go lower. It has the tail-end of
the “ Marvellous Mile.” Veins thin, ore-shoots short, not
the mine it was cracked up to be a year ago, but a good
one compared to many others close by. Will pay an
occasional div., but a long time about it.
» * * *
Lake View South : Mine manager, it is averred,
says he knows nothing of any reconstruction scheme, but
reconstruction is inevitable. More money must be put
up or the mine shut up. Latter would perhaps be the
less of the two evils, but they are slow to do such
things in London, with a mine occupying so excellent a
situation. Having a fine crushing-plant, with no ore of
its own to treat, it has done well by public crushing—
which is now falling off.
* * * *
Boulder Main Reef : Shares back to 425. ; they
reached 555. on the find of a month ago. An immensely
rich patch of telluride was found driving north at the
600 ft. level, about 200 ft. from Gt. Boulder boundary— one
of the richest patches yet seen on the field, but the drive
has passed through it. Rumored that some men in the
know made thousands on the rise, but you can’t always
believe rumor.
* * * •
L. V. Consols: £ll 10s. (£2 15s. lower than
they were a month ago which only means three-quarters
of a million.) Hard to get reliable information as to the
character of developments in the lower levels. Seems
certain that the main lode, from about the main shaft
south, has gone into the Golden Link. If one can judge
by the work in G.L. this lode appears to be poor, so it will
not be much good in the deep ground to either mine un-
less further prospecting shows an improvement.
*'* * »
Associated : £3 ss. Went to 83s. a month ago
on the strength of their “ marvellous development.” It
wasn’t marvellous by any means, but probably the shares
would be £1 lower without it. Was, at the 600, probably
a repetition of Tetley’s lode, found in driving north from
Tetley’s to Judd’s shaft, to connect the two for economi-
cal working. Too soon to say what is the value of the
strike ; looks promising, and certainly has rich ore in it.
This new branch will probably be better than the main
lode, and it is nearly certain they will find another shoot
further north. They are now going to add the necessary
plant to get a better extraction, putting in grinding-pans
and filter presses. Adelaide has still many Associateds.
• * * *
Boulder Perseverance : £lO 10s.—a drop of 30s
since the new sulphide plant started, because of the
usual troubles in starting a new mill and process, but
there seems no doubt of the ultimate success of the plant.
Their furnaces are so far working well and are a great
improvement on those on the L.V. and Associated. A
splendid mine ; big lodes, several of them, long ore-shoots
and consistent values. The ore contains more silica than
that in the L.V., Associated, and other mines surrounding
it, and for this reason needs fine grinding. From the
want of it, extraction is not so great as expected, and
from the lack of grinding appliances, the capacity of the
mill is considerably reduced. This and some other defects
are being remedied. The mine can wait, and shareholders
who are able and willing to do likewise are bound to get
a good return. There would seem to be no danger in
Perseverance at £lO.
* * * •
Paringa diamond drill, stationed on the boun-
dary of Cygnet lease, is down 600 ft.; proposed to go
1000 ft., looking for the lode of the N. Associated. “The
bore is within a stone’s-throw of the Associated Northern
main shaft.” It would take a strong man to throw that
stone. A stone’s-throw distance is very variable.
» » * •
Great Boulder, 275. The lowest price reached
for more than a year. Cause unknown. Extraction
satisfactory, but plant unable to work up to its full
capacity owing to the conveyors being faulty, and Collie
coal rather ticklish to handle in gas-making; N.S.W.
coal required to mix with it. Putting in fresh conveyors,
taking out the push-conveyors behind the Griffen mills,
putting them in front of the furnaces, and putting
shaking conveyors behind the mills. This change is ex-
pected to overcome the conveying difficulty. The
furnaces are consuming more fuel than the test in
Victoria indicated, but it is expected that when in full
work the cost of roasting alone will be under ss. a ton.
The cyanide plant is making big inroads on the old
tailings-heap at the rate of 300 tons a day, but before
this magnificent asset is exhausted the minor defects of
the sulphide plant will be remedied, and there is no
reason to doubt that the present gold production from
two sources will, for years to come, be kept up by the
sulphide plant alone. Value and permanency of mine
established in the number of lodes, in size and length,
and in depth. It is well equipped, well managed, and is
certain to be a div.-payer for years.
* • * •
Ivanhoe : Attempt made in London to sweep
away Whitaker Wright’s nominees on this board—which
is believed to consist of nothing else. Stated that the six
members of the board only hold 360 shares in the co., and
some men signing the requisition own individually more
than ten times that number. Two Rothschilds have
signed it Kaufman is supposed to be at the
back of the move. The Financial News, British
Australasian and other London financial papers
are supporting Whitaker Wright’s crowd. Thus
the B. Australasian : “ The surprising thing is that some
well-known men in the City should have lent their assist-
ance to an agitation which can only result in depreciating
the market-value of the shares of the Ivanhoe mine, and
add to the feeling of want of confidence which is once
more coming over the W.A. mining industry.” It seems
a renewal of the fight between Wright and Kaufman, and
undoubtedly a case of from the frying-pan into the fire, or
from the fire into the frying-pan.' It is an outcome of the
disgraceful dismissal of the honest Hewitson, for which
someone should be made to suffer.
* * * •
Northern Associated : 525. A big drop after a
magnificent return. “ Always sell before the crushing ”
was right here, too, although the result was a big one-
over lOoz. to the ton and over 600 tons ! Manager said to
estimate tonnage in sight at over 100,000, and value
£1,500,000. Mine developing: wonderfully well—so many
developments have taken place as to give color to, if not
absolutely estahlish, this great value, yet the market is
no higher than when the first strike was made
six months ago, and when it was impossible
to say how long it would last. Since that
time new levels have been opened, winzes sunk,
two lodes at least proved—and shares still fall.
Mine recently found some splendid ore in the main
shaft, and has been developing the same shoot from the
north end towards the same shaft; anyhow it seems to
be the same ore-body. If this be so, the ore-shoot is
very long and rich, but it appears to dip south at a very
flat angle, and underlies west very fiat also. Manager
jubilant re prospects of mine. And yet shares were
within a few shillings of present prices when the mine
had not revealed one-tenth its present value.
* • * •
The Westralian dry-blower and individual
digger have struck a new woe. Last year a Gold-Dredg-
ing and Sluicing Act was passed through the Groper Par-
liament. It authorises anybody to take up as much as
5000 acres at a yearly rent of 6d. per acre, and to hold the
ground against all comers for any number of years with
out doing any work whatever. It is merely necessary to
pay the rent and put machinery of a certain value on the
ground inside 12 months—the Act doesn’t even seem to
specify for certain that the machinery mustn’t be immedi-
ately taken off the ground again. “ Dredgingand Sluicing ”
naturally suggested operations in wet places only, so the
dry-blower paid little attention while the measure was
going through. But when a capitalist pegged out almost
the entire gold-workings at Kurnalpi under this new law,
there was such a sitting-up among the dry-blowers as was
never seen before. And it really seems a matter of un-
certainty whether these proved gold deposits, some of
them five miles away from water, can’t be grabbed, and
all the prospectors ejected, under this comprehensive
Dredging and Sluicing law. Nearly all the mining laws
in W. A. appear to be either a man-trap, or a spring-gun,
or both, and they are always exploding unexpectedly.
• • • •
Mt. Dedd-sick : Half-yearly meeting recently
held in Melbourne when the directors announced that
authority had been given for flotation in England of the
co.’s property conjointly with other properties on the
Dedd-sick field, and an examination of the field was now
being made by an expert selected by the English
investors. How nice this would read—were it not a
million to one that that’s the end of it? Meanwhile
nothing is doing on the mine, and much money is
thereby saved. This scribe has so far been unable to
discover that the mine was ever much good—or ever will
be —unless something extraordinary happens.

SOCIETY
A peculiar position surely ! Chief Justice
Darlesy tried and condemned to death Sleigh, the
Bombala murderer. At a meeting of KS.W.
Executive held on 30th ult., the C.J. attended, as
usual in such cases, to advise the Executive, and
consideration of the case was again adjourned for
the purpose of inquiring if the condemned man’s
father was at present confined as a lunatic.
Meanwhile the C.J. becomes State-Governor and
consequently final arbiter of the condemned man’s
fate. It is no argument to say that in the Chief
Justice’s new position as Lieutenant-Governor he
is practically bound by the Imperial instructions
to take the advice of his Executive. As Judge he
advises the Executive in this case, and then the
Executive advises him. The situation affords yet
another proof of The Bulletin’s long-time con-
tention that no Judge should hold the acting -
Governorsbip.
* * * *
The difficulty in the marriage-license law
of N.S. W. lies in conferring the right to marry on
“any clergyman having a congregation.” First,
what is a clergyman ? Second, what is a congre-
gation ? Any man may call himself a clergyman,
and half-a-dozen people may be a congregation.
A man may separate from his church and still
perform his clerical duties, and there is no law to
intervene. He may have a congregation of hun-
dreds one year and a mere Sunday-school class
another, and at what stage of decline can the law
step in ? In fact, this license business is a very
old N.S.W. trouble. Bishop Bailey’s was a very
big case 40 years ago. Bailey did six months’
gaol for marrying a minor, and when he came out
he was refused a license. He took the case to the
Supreme Court, and then to the Full Court, and
the refusal was sustained, but on no clear point of
law.

In connection with the shooting of Joe
Governor, there are now turning up the usual
“ premonitions,” ko:—after the fact! Mrs. Wil-
kinson, mother of the man who shot Joe, has
frequently, it is alleged, expressed a hope “that
neither- of her sons would meet the fugitives,
because she felt sure if they did that they would
succeed in taking life”; and John Wilkinson—
the shootist—is said to have expressed his con-
fidence that he would vet shoot Joe. These are
the sort of absurdities that inevitably get about
amongst people such as those who were admitted
by the police in batches to satiate their curiosity
by viewing the corpse of Governor—and of whom
one “struck a match on the coarse skin of the
dead foot -as easily- as on a piece of sandpaper.”
The English tongue sorely lacks a word to denote
a combination of ghoul and idiot.
* * * *
At the conclusion of the coroner’s inquest
on the body of outlaw Joe Governor, shot dead
like a wild dog, “ three hearty cheers ” were given
for the Wilkinson brothers. A coroner’s jury
with a gruesome verdict on one lip and a hearty
cheer on the other ! In 1840 a funeral, wending
its way to a Dublin cemetery, met the great Dan
O Connell. The party deposited the coffin on the
roadside, cheered O’Connell as he passed, and then
picked up the corpse and went on. Pretty quick
emotional transactions !
* * * *
Parkes’s idea of a deadhouse for Australian
heroes in Centennial Park has been successively
jeered at and adopted by after Premiers —by
Dibbs, by Reid, and now by Lyne.
* * • «
N.S.W. Government has chosen wisely in
deciding that the swearing-in of the Governor-
General shall take place in Centennial Park—the
noble area of which will obviate all risk from
crushing, while the amphitheatre-like rise of the
ground all round will give a splendid view of the
proceedings. Even the expected attendance of
300,000 persons will have room galore in the
Centennial.
* * * »
N.S.W. Anglican Synod cold-shouldered
the local Old-Age Pension Bill. Some of the
members have pet schemes of their own, and, if
old and needy people are pensioned, the occupa-
tion of these ecclesiastical Othellos would be gone.
Each wants to stick to his own little sphere of in-
fluence, where ever so much hat-touching is
received for a shilling or two a week. One clergy-
man even thinks that “old men are verv well
cared for already at Parramatta and Rookwood.”
Yes the general cemetery, as well as the
biggest old-people’s home, is at Rookwood. This
is real statesmanship.

* * * *
“ P.H.0.” on “ Women in Wine-bars ” :
I have been doing a round of the Sydney “ hodegas
and other wine-shops (some of them called “dives”).
The atmosphere of many of these bars is fetid
from the impregnation of the confined and cavern-
ous air with the mixed odours of wine, tobacco and
“ Homo.” Yet in many of these unwholesome dens young-
women work usually at least 84 hours a-week, and in
some cases much longer! Some are hound to then-
employers body and soul for 24 hours of the day, week in,
week out; a condition attached to their engagement
being that they shall remain at night with the employer.
The wages withal are so pitiably small that it’s no wonder
that those girls—not the veritable chattels of their master
—who leave the bars at 11 p.m., find it necessary to
supplement their incomes in a -manner needless to par-
ticularise. Amongst the frequenters of the wine-bars
votaries of Venus are more numerous than those of
Bacchus ; and it is principally the former who create the
evil moral atmosphere of the place, some proprietors even
expecting the girls who serve to listen smilingly to the
most disgusting ribaldry from customers. It’s a pity that
what should be our national beverage is not dispensed
amidst less ignoble surroundings.
* * * *
A Rockhampton paper tells a queer story
of long-drawn-out coincidences. Some 17 years
ago two sailors came to that town in an emigrant
vessel. On the way they fell in love with two of
the single girls, and left the vessel to get married.
Neither did marry, after all. Both joined the Q.
Marine Department, drifted apart for some years,
and finally drifted together again in Rockhamp-
ton. Early this year one was drowned in the
Eitzroy, and a few weeks ago the second met his
end m the same manner. The long arm—with a
vengeance!

PALE BLACK.
GIN (engaged as housemaid): “Missis, you gib it blacking till I whitewash the fireplace.”
Brisbane Courier remarks sadly that the
Queensland capital is one of the few cities, if not
the only city, in the British empire where there is
no Building Act—where you can build any kind
of unsightly wooden or galvanised-iron or bark-
and-shingle hutch in a busy street, and make it
as undrained, dark, ugly, inflammable and insani-
tary as you please. Evidently the Courier doesn’t
know Sydney. Sydney has a Building Act which
applies to about one-tenth or one-fifteenth of its
area, but over all the rest, covering about three
times the population of Brisbane, the house-
builder can, if he chooses, go back to the ways of
the Middle Ages. The Building Act can certainly
be extended to the Sydney suburbs if the local
municipal authorities demand it, but they don’t.
Said municipal authorities consist largely of land-
lords, but they are too decayed in the intellect to
understand that it isn’t to their interest to allow
people to build more wood and galvanised-iron
hovels adjacent to their properties, and thus
depreciate the letting and selling value of the
latter. Nor can they understand that the increase
m the number of tenements when population is
nearly stationary helps to keep rents low, and the
selling price of property keeps down in proportion
to rents, and that the. cheap and shoddy building
system, whereby anyone can put up some kind of
a house for about £lO, favors this multiplication
of tenements.
Writer knows of one leading street in a large
Sydney suburb where three good stone-built shops
are in a chronic state of emptiness, while three
unsightly wooden dog-boxes, run up within the
last few months, are always occupied. They can
be let so as to yield 20 per cent, on cost, for a price
that wouldn’t pay interest on the more respect-
able structures, so the latter remain empty, and
they will probably stay empty while the supply
of dog-boxes lasts. Yet the aldermanic owner is
against an extension of the Building Act lest it
should compel him to repair a certain tumble-
down cottage—the only one of his buildings which
is regularly tenanted. It isn’t to be expected
that the aldermanic property-owner will approve
of a good Building Act on the ground that it will
beautify the suburbs and make them more civilised
and more healthy, but his inability to see further
than the end of his own inflamed nose, and to com-
prehend where his own interests lie, is a trifle sur-
prising.
* * * *
The Bulletin has a valuable suggestion
for Premier Lyne, who seems to experience much
difficulty in finding suitable ways of cele-
brating the inauguration of the Commonwealth.
There is going to be far too much soldier
and war-horse and military fireworks on that
auspicious occasion, and with an eye to giving the
civil section a look-in, this paper advises that the
best use be made of Jimmy Governor now that
the Government has fairly secured that elusive
and interesting coon. Why not make Jimmy the
central figure in the grand march round ? A cage
might be borrowed from Fitzgerald’s circus in
which the colored brother could be exhibited
without risk of his breaking out and biting the
police.

Bendigo’s mayor believes that he has
booked Mr. and Mrs. York for one founda-
tion-stone, and the Solemn Asses of Society
are arranging a grand united speech-day in
which all the kids of the schools are to take
part, the ceremony to be conducted in the
presence of York and the Duchess. Already 15
Vic. municipalities are busy preparing addresses to
be fired at the Royal pair on reception-day, and
scores of two-penny-ha’penny ceremonies are
saving up for the Duke in and about Melbourne.
Evidently there is a deliberate intention abroad
in Vic. to make York earn his keep. For his own
sake it is to be hoped lie will have savee enough
to bring himself within the provisions of the Fac-
tories Act.

Everyone who supplies goods or does work
for the Duke of York or other allied celebrations
should insist on cash down or a negotiable bond
duly signed. The general rule is that when such
visits take place crowds of busybodies arrange
programmes of one thing and another, and no one
is made legally responsible for expenses. Charles
Moore, Mayor of Sydney, stuck up £54 for music
to the Duke of Edinburgh, but the musicians
never got a ha’penny. The Mayor put it on to
others, and others on to the Mayor, and the
legally-responsible person was never found.

Th it nude male-model
trouble recalls other worries
that vexed the soul of the
aspiring lady artist in the
early days, i.e., some half-
dozen years ago. The modesty
of those in authority was so
great in these mediaeval times
that when the first feminine
asked permission to join the
class that drew from the nude
they gave in only after much
difficulty. Then in a short
while their modesty raged
once more, and, having per-
mitted her to join, they veered
round and declared they could
not allow her to stay a day
longer, unless she got up a
class for ladies only. The
artist brought some dozen of
her sex as a nucleus of the
new class. Then the modest
ones draped a piece of chintz
across the room and gave the
lady artists a model all to
themselves on one side and
the gentlemen ditto ditto on
the other. As to the fact
that the lady artists had to
pass through the gentlemen’s compartment,
within sight of the forbidden model, in order to
reach their end of the room,-all parties agreed to
say nothing.

“ Layman ” : “ Puyyaka says that chlo-
roform is out of date with modern doctors, it is
only once in a blue moon that any other anaesthe-
tic is used in Melb. Hospital, the objection to
ether being that it takes very much longer to
produce unconsciousness. In America simple
chloroform is never used —always ether, or a
mixture of the two, and that has been the practice
there for the last 54 years.

Quite a number of publicans compliment
Premier Lyne on shutting up the Sydney soft-
drink shops on Sunday. They do twice the trade
on Sunday they used to do. Soft-drink shops, if
the ukase lasts, will likely open up side and back
doors also, and then all the police in the city will
be employed. Which will give the burglar a fine
chance.

In a Yic. country town,
the other day, a young lady
aroused, at 2 a.m., _ all the
inmates of the lipuse in which
she was staying, by screaming
that there was a man in her
room. Her to

caused the alarm. The absent-
minded girl had been married
on the previous day, and,
waking up suddenly, had for-
gotten the circumstance.
The hansom cab is com-
ing so much into favor in Adelaide
that to ask a lady to ride in one would
now no longer be construed into a reflec-
tion upon her respectability. This shows that the
Holy City is becoming civilised. Much the same
idea used to prevail in Melbourne in the old days,
but Sydney never shrunk from its hansom. There
may be a moral hanging round here somewhere !
* * * »
It will soon be possible to trace the pro-
gress of ex-priest Slattery through Australia by
the trail of smashed furniture that he leaves
behind him. The man whose wages are the price
of blood —nose-blood, that is—recently attempted
at Brisbane to expose the errors of the Scarlet
Woman who is alleged to sit on seven hills simul-
taneously, thereby manifesting a breadth of sit-
down altogether unusual. But apparently almost
everyone named Doolan was there or thereabouts,
and the policeman who is generally named
Murphy was swept off his feet, and the premises
were wrecked before Slattery had got through
half-a-dozen sentences. Judging by this, and
several previous like incidents, there isn’t a city in
Australia that a very small mob can’t take posses-
sion of just about any time it feels that way. It
is casually mentioned that, out of the 500 or 600
people who threw stones weighing up to 111 b.
apiece for about two hours, the wildly energetic
police force captured three. Which is very Aus-
tralian indeed.

N.S.W. Government has set a bad ex-
ample to the community by calmly disregarding
the recent Supreme Court injunction, forbidding
for the present the use of the Randwiek (Sydney)
range for rifle practice. Despite the fence which
the authorities have erected with the view of
stopping erratic bullets, these leaden visitors con-
tinue to arrive in the Coogee neighborhood. In
one recent instance the missile lodged in a resi-
dent’s front-door, and remains there for the in-
spection of any body concerned. The Bulletin
hopes to see Chief Justice Darley “run in” the
person responsible for the contempt with which
the court’s injunction has been treated—no matter
how high and mighty he may be.

* * * *
The recent report of Mr. Hanson, Acting-
Director of N.S.W. Charities, discloses a lovely
state of things as regards the administration of
tiie Metropolitan Charities Association. Out of
a total revenue of £1112 —£656 had gone for
salaries, £32 for stationery, &c., £99 for printing,
and £66 for sundries—the total administrative ex-
penditure being £964, as against £30 spent on the
poor, or less than the amount expended in
stationery ! Presumably amongst the “sundries”
came an item of £8 6s. 8d. “ for afternoon-tea in
connection with a charity conference.” What a
howling satire upon the methods of so-called
“organised charity”!
* * * *
At a meeting of the Sydney police force,
it was elicited that, owing to the utter disorganisa-
tion of the Police Superannuation Fund, a number
of officers who are past duty are still receiving full
pay, as it has been found impossible to pension
them ; thus not only wasting the public money,
but blocking legitimate promotion in the force.
This is a scandalous state of things, which Govt,
should take instant steps to remedy. No com-
munity can afford to neglect the interests of its
police; and any uncertainty about the reward for
a long course of police service will tend to keep
oat of the force the very stamp of man which it is
most desirable to enlist.

A case of “ beef-measles ” —the first

* • • •
The motives by which Sleath, M.L.A.,
was actuated in the recent row with General
French seem questionable. The information
which he has demanded has for some time been in
possession of Parliament, and had Sleath taken
'his feet off the table and looked up the necessary
documents he could have saved much time. Even
had the information not been in the possession of
Parliament it could always have been got conh -
dentially through the Minister for Defence. The
only question is : Whether Gen. French should
inform Sleath in open court, where all the world
can hear, about certain important military secrets
that Sleath knows already. It can do no good,
and may do infinite harm. But Sleath wanted to
go to S’Africa, and be a loud, blatant amateur
officer, and French wouldn’t let him. And now
Sleath is having his turn.

* * * *
At a Melb. inquest lately, where deceased
had succumbed from blood-poisoning supervening
on premature birth, it was elicited that the nurse
who had attended the lady—she having refused to
call in a doctor—was quite undiscoverable. A
neighbor and the daughter had seen her, but
couldn’t describe her, and the husband was under
the impression that an attendant of some sort was
fooling around the patient for nearly a fortnight,
but nobody asked her name or address. Just
before the patient died the nurse faded altoge-
ther, and hasn’t been seen or heard of since. The
best description that the police can get of her is
that she has two feet and some hair, and they are
looking for her anxiously to give evidence. It is
all so beautifully casual.
