A WOMAN'S LETTER.
„ Sydney, November 6, 1900.
My Dear Myee.—
Have you heard of the sensational divorce-
action? Well, if you haven’t, you possibly will.
It has an ecclesiastical flavor, and may yet be to
Australia what the Tilton-Beecher case was to
Yankeeland.
F.eet Ball, at Garden Island, was a great suc-
cess in the items of supper and decorations and
scented ponds and fairy lighting. But a gloom
like a wet, warm fog hung over it. There were
only 4UO people—the Sydney Four Hundred-
present. Wall-flowers were very numerous—the
wicked laymen hadn’t ceased from troubling the
military and the weary Governor stayed at rest at
Government House. He sent a message begging
to be excused attendance as it was his last
night in Sydney. Two debutantes were notice-
able. Miss Muffy Barton, bright-complexioned,
fluffy-haired; and a daughter qf Major Lee.
Our dear departed Governor is a man with an
original, if strange, taste. Instead of railing
South, as everyone with money and leisure is
doing at this season, he has expressed North.
Instead of Flemington lawn and track, and racing
hoofs, and silky hides, he is probably having a
quiet pull on Breakfast Creek, or he is sauntering
round Thursday Island making kind inquiries
about beche-de-mer and pearl-fishing. Anon he is
to go to Papua, and I fancy I see him seated
under a sago-palm or a wild nutmeg dreaming
over a volume of Whittier.
Our dear departed Governor is a very nice
young man; many considered him feeble and
tactless, _ but he was merely misplaced. It’s a
great point, for instance, in a gallery how you hang
pictures. How much more so the timing and
placing of an Australian Governor. In his native
lanes in Worcestershire, under the shadow of the
village church, Little Boy Blue is appreciated and
understood, but he is quite out of touch with us.
What we want in Governors is one who, in addition
to his title and money, would enter a horse for the
turf. Then the test of his popularity would be :
Hoes the crowd on the Flat cheer him when his
brumby wins a classic?
Our dear departed Governor came amongst us
heavily handicapped, in fact leg-ironed, by that
Kipling convict-quotation. Then followed the
blue and white tickets-of-leave. And just as we
were saying “They are nought but mistakes”
came the Cobar speech which filled the French in
Australasia with a desire to scrape "William the
Blunderer to death with pot-sherds.
Next came the Italians’ huff when His Ex.
played with the Boys’ Brigade or something of
the sort, instead of spending a pleasant afternoon
at a kind of funeral service for a murdered king.
The St. Mary’s sermon fiasco is counted by some
unto the Governor as unrighteousness, but un-
justly so. The mitred Maorilander with the
beard was the pickled-onion on that occasion.
But all these are very forgiveable lack-of-humour
sms. They might come under the head of Early
English faults.
His Ex.’s most inexplicable mistake—if it was a
mistake has been his attitude (as set forth, by
certain of his enemies) after the accident to the
little girl Morris. Nearly a year ago Maud Morris
was knocked down and hurt by his Ex.’s carriage
as it was being rapidly driven to Redfern Station.
Maud Morris was taken to Prince Alfred Hospital,
where she remained some weeks, and has off and
on been under medical treatment ever since. The
child s mother—so goes the tale —a working woman,
very poor and supporting her four young children,
does not seem to have had any practical
sympathy shown her by the Lygons. Lady Mary
called on her two days after the accident and
was very nice in word and manner. These
cost nothing, and are instinctive to well-bred
people. After some writing and interviewing of
Captain Smith, Mrs. Morris was given £2. Later
she received a further sum of £3, which amount it
was considered would fully compensate her for
the accident to her child. Finally the woman had
to agree to a settlement of £2O, procured her by a
solicitor. Yet the injured girl was her eldest and
only help amongst her children.
That is one side of the case—one side only. The
Governor’s side seems to be that the accident was
trifling, and was from the very first “ worked ” for
all that it was worth—and this not merely by the
person directly interested. Someone has said
that every decent man carries in his face a pro-
mise to pay. And if ever a man’s face carried
such a promise it is Lord Beauchamp’s.
Such a cultured-looking, seemingly-underfed
audience came to Hedda Gabler’s First Night and
searched that lady minutely (through its pince-
nez) for her moral and intellectual signifi-
cance. She isn’t a bit wicked in the
Seven-Deadly-Sins sense. You won’t find one
of her sins in the Fatal Seven except
Anger. Hedda is certainly angry much and often.
But she is virginal in the heartless, passionless;
soulless sense of the word. Miss O’Neil has put a
lot of somebody’s brains into her Gabler dressing.
At her entry you note her pale gold hair brushed
up slab and coiled tightly, flatly, on the top of her
head, not the tiniest scrap of baby curl appears on
her forehead or neck. She presents you with a
hard, cold outline of person and mind. Then her
wan, golden face and straw-coloured gown—it’s a
ghostly-golden, bloodless study. In the next act
she is still yellow, but brighter, and she dies in a
black satin robe and a deep-pink rose. By the
way, that bracelet that Miss O s Neil lost just after
it had been presented to her on her birthday by
Mr. Rankin has not yet been returned to the Aus-
tralia.
Rev. George Walters’ “Thou Fool” fooled a
packed house last week. Amateur Phillip Lytton
produced it, and McKee Rankin is said to have
been in front to see Lytton’s promise as an actor,
lhe amateur bears a strong likeness to Toby
Parton and has a voice like Bland Holt’s Baker,
and his promise was capital, despite the awful
material with which he mummed. McKee
Kan km must have admired Miss Madge Hope’s
imitation of Nance O’Neil. Miss Hope has a
beautiful face and a figure perilously approaching
Nance s proportions. And if she had acted her
part after some conception of her own she might
have scored. There was a sweet waif and a
capital policeman. Miss Vera Eyton did prettily,
iru 8 since gone with Coghill’s opera co. Miss
Mildred Aarons, and other girl and men amateurs,
had nO reason to be ashamed of their work.
Narrabri’s most gigantic intellects still wrestle
with the problem, “ Who is the woman, and what
her motive, that nightly stones a certain home-
stead ? ” Her name is probably Gladys, she has
no “boy,” and she is sick of Narrabrian mono-
tony, and she carries the stones in a long pocket
in an underskirt. Police, civilians, and mounted
troopers cannot discover her. Balzac says that if
the woman be only discreet enough no one may
discover anything against her.
A journalist friend of mine started house-
keeping with a young wife, an angel-faced servant
of thirteen, whom he had got from a charitable
institution, and twenty pedigree fowls. Upon a
night a fowl was garrotted and laid on the kitchen
doorstep. Every night for five nights a fowl was
killed in the same fashion, and this despite the
house-dog and master’s vigils. Finally the latter
hid in the fowl-house itself and so caught the
angel-faced damsel with a slip-knot at the end of
a short stick. She, the little Thug, operated far
more swiftly and quietly than when bringing
shaving-water in the morning. Tears and “ I just
like to hear people wonderin’ who done it ” was
her explanation.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Derby Day, 1900, seemed pokey without a
single lion in the Governor’s battue. Those of
the local smartest connected closely or distantly
with the Chirnside clan were absent, too, mourn-
ing poor young Calvert, killed in the Transvaal.
’Tis said, however, that even before the sad news
came a galaxy of society-stars had decided rather
than waste time cultivating ‘ small potatoes ’ in
town during present race-season, to retire to their
country-keeps, there to save up their smiles
and fizz for the Duke of York.
‘ ‘ Gov. Madden, in his very tightest light-grey
suit and big, red carnation posy, affected a
‘ Frencli-hen halo ’ —every hair frizzed on end so
as to produce the effect of a ‘wealth of curls.’
His liege lady, tamely dressed in blue muslin with
black-lace parasol, coaxed the blue-ribbon cleverly
round the Derby winner’s neck, and though Malster
is a dark horse, the color became him beautifully.
Pretty Miss Madden didn’t, look quite so much
like a dewy blush-rose as usual, owing to her white
veil, faintly tinged with blue, which threw a cold,
dignified shade over her dimpled face. Her Derby
costume, modelled perhaps on one worn by Nellie
Stewart at a charity fete the week before, was
white muslin threaded with blue baby-ribbon, and
drooping white-rush hat trimmed with pink roses
and blue bows. Sister Leslie’s pink-straw hat,
over-run with mauve grapes, went with pink mus-
lin and chiffon shoulder-scarf to match. That’s
the worst of these transparent yokes. Wearers
with delicate chests or blistery shoulders must
tote round a wraplet in case of need. A lace
shawl is just the floppy, feminine fal-lal to go
with trailing skirt and bell-sleeves, but Heaven
defend us from things to carry about! All very
well, when the chief end of Woman was to look
nice. Now that’s she’s got to fly round, tails to
hold up and sleeves dipping into things are expen-
sive luxuries.
“ White petticoats to the fore again ! Frilled
muslin ones sent home with all washing-frocks
this summer. White stockings, I guess, will
come next. Then bring on your sandals and
curls, and perhaps you’ll be satisfied when the
suburban woman, running for an early morning
train, drops out of the struggle to look as well as
the best of them, and relapses into the dowdy
working-bee she seems to be in all countries but
sunny Australia.
“Up to now, our cool, cloudy Spring has
restrained the rage for thinly-veiled shoulders and
arms out of doors. But the mania was manifest
on Saturday. At present, observers declare that
at local dances they can recognise she-Sydneyites
at a glance by the lacey patterns traced on their
backs and chests, in freckles and flesh-color, or
sunburn and snow.
“Nelly Stewart’s white, covered with raised
white silk embroidery, had under-skirt of white
chiffon flounce edged with white ribbon, pink
velvet waistband drawn through a group of big
‘ diamond ’ rear buckles, pink quilled parasol and
top-piece of white crinoline, pink roses and blue
bows. Under the drooping brim a big all-round
plait and lots of fringe peeped out, looking quite
red in the sun, and casting a garish glow on the
face underneath. Nellie must have been looking
well, for she-beholders gnashed their teeth at the
sight of her, and kept on saying what a ‘ stagey-
looking ’ creature she was, and deploring her lack
of corsets. From this last accusation you may
father that the culprit’s figure is worth looking at.
ler frock seemed moulded to it, and one’s eye
runs down to hip-line without being stopped
half-way by a waist-mark which hadn’t developed
in Eve’s day. Messrs. Musgrove and Arens
(Faust) were in attendance off and on, and
between times a few she-satellites joined the
group, but though rumor has it that our Nellie’s
heart hungers for feminine society, her demeanour
in the presence of petticoats wouldn’t lead one to.
suppose so.
l< Tenor Salvi (hair and moustache black as
ink) and Lauri were other ‘ stars ’ in sight.
Theatrical race-goers (male) wore gloves and
frock-coats, but their smartness wasn’t dazzling
enough to make you blink. Other men, as a rule,
looked shabby and scrubby in seedy boxers, etc.
Frock-coats and toppers seem ‘out’ and no
picturesque Wild-west style (shirt sleeves and
sombrero) ventures in as yet. Baronet Clarke, in
neat little brown shoes, serge suit and straw hat
worn askew, trotted round with Mrs. Lawson,
his absent wife’s cousin. This lady, whose
‘ points ’ are a handsome nose, a tall, smart figure,
and a ‘ good-comrade ’ disposition, sported a gown
which might just have scooped a big chic success,
but didn’t. It was plainly-made white serge with
sailor-collar of pale-blue velvet. Pink parasol and
sapphire and diamond ear-studs failed to save the
sartorial situation. Janet Lady Clarke, slim and
graceful in white silk-muslin and fine black lace
insertion, strolled about with brain-specialist
Maudsley, and did a few turns with George Reid,
with whom all women love to talk.
Manager Musgrove, who has a magnificent re-
putation for donations of beer and sandwiches
and “soft stuff” among the sore-throat chorus-
singers at tedious times of late rehearsal, gave
his race-party a swagger luncheon to help the
Derby dead-birds off their chests, and, in the
later noon, a strawberry scramble.
“Manager Musgrove has a new pair of white
kid gloves and a fresh flower on every night, to
shine in the glare of the box at the opera. The
other night, when ‘ Carmen ’ was aboard, only the
tip of Nellie’s hat could be seen behind Musgrove’s
shoulder. The fair one only shows herself in the
box when she has all the shoulder-covers off and
the war-paint on.
“ Agnes Jansen, the stout Swedish contralto
who made such a splendid old gipsy in ‘ Trova-
tore,’ doesn’t look a scrap bewitching as Carmen.
Her eyes and teeth show up well, and her expres-
sion is cheerful, but she has no grace, and
if I gave you a truthful estimate of her hip-
measurement, you’d say I was exaggerating.
Her first costume is something musliny and
streetish, and she sports a Collingwood curl,
plastered down her forehead, presumably to give
her the air of h ‘ bold-faced jig.’ The result calls
up recollections of a quaint translation of ‘ The
Mysteries of Paris,’with the frequent references
to a ‘leery cove.’ Madame Jansen’s ‘leery cove’
looks old enough to know better.
“ Carmen’s second frock, rich in Spangle em-
broideries, is wrecked by its low-cut corsage, as
unattractively crude in its revelations as a picture
in a physiology-book. Teutonic charmers do
nothing by halves, and their decolletage methods
are as thorough as the rest. The operatic co-
quette’s mountaineering get-up, with its round
Toreador hat, short-waisted bolero jacket and
brief bunchy skirts, is rather like the fat, bob-
tailed suits in which V.R. (unless old pictures
belie her) was wont to disfigure Highland land-
scapes in the days of her buxom bridal bliss.
However, Carmen looks much more attractive at
the finish in sequined black skirt, shimmering
red bodice, Spanish comb and black lace mantilla.
“ Max Eugene’s ‘ Escamillo ’ is a fine, big Ger-
man bull-fighter, but there’s no fire in him. One
feels that from being a respectable successful but-
cher he must have taken to the Toreador trade,
not from love of sport, but with an eye to making
money out of the carcases. By the way, girls
don t imagine from this that bull-beef is in request
for the table. Scribe learned the contrary from
the painful experience of a friend, who at her
mother’s request called at the butcher’s to order
steak and kidney. Butcher asked, ‘ Hox kidney,
Miss ? ’ The girl, misunderstanding his question,
replied, ‘No, not hog’s, bull’s.’ The man and his
assistant looked queer, and the customer, guessing
someone had blundered,’ suddenly exclaimed
that she saw ‘a St. Bernard fighting her toy-
terrier,’ and escaped from the shop.
“ Salvi working up to the murder of his false
love, is the best thing in ‘ Carmen.’
‘‘ the way, Madame Wiedemann (Mrs
Pinschoff), Professor of singing at the Conserva-
torium, achieved the success of her European
operatic career in the rolo of Carmen, and her
eldest daughter bears the name of the Spanish
gypsy as a souvenir of her mother’s bygone
triumph.
“Mrs. G. B Lewis’s one-night drama at the
Gaiety (under the Bijou) drew a friendly crowd
that more than filled the cosy house, and if the
array of the folks in front didn’t enjoy the enter-
tainment they had only themselves to upbraid.
Mrs. L. s Wreck of the Inverness ’ was the
drollest thing imaginable. The humor of it was
not so much m the misconduct of the curtain at
critical moments, or the occasional waits for the
prompter to intervene, as in the strange casual-
,ness of the plot. Somebody saved an infant from
a watery grave, and dropped it out of the narra-
tive simpiy because it didn’t happen to be the
right baby. Twenty years later the right baby was
.found, looking for his yellow-haired girl, who had
got lost m an endeavour to comfort humanity at
large. Also the blind uncle of the right babv
turned up from the 22-year-old wreck, and
another nice girl abandoned her home of luxury
(at least they said it was luxury) in order to share
starvation with this unaccountable ancient. A
third girl had a mania for fits in the street.
After recovering from a fit she picked up an un-
known baby, and took it home to die in her
garret, although she had already an im-
proper offspring of her own, after which
her next fit proved fatal, and the
author of her shame was put to confusion,
and the gentle mimstenng-angel gave orders for
the corpse to be removed. In fact, she took the
unknown child from its cradle and carried it off
to the undertaker or the coroner without asking
for a death-certificate. “ The Wreck of the In-
verness was the sort of drama that only happens
to us once m our career We take kindly to it at
* he *™®’., b ut we don t know how much we loved
it all till it has gone.
; ~ Mrs. Lewis, the authoress, played an aggressive
widow and Miss Athena Claudius assisted* her as
far as the awful plot would permit. The rest of
the trouble, excepting a song by Miss Cesca Vol-
lugi m the prologue, fell to Mrs. L.’s pupils The
girls, without exception, spoke their lines nicelv
and rose superior to stage-fright. The low-come-
was a bright young amateur.
Miss Wolstehholme would have been satisfactory
but for her skipsome walk, and Miss Una Russell
in the character of the yellow-haired vision’
showed all the intelligence and charm that her
warmest admirers had expected.
“Much nonsense is talked about Madame
Marchesi and Amy Castles, and what they have
said of one another, and how pupils are treated at
Madame’s famous academy. Even Nellie Stewart
appears to speak from hearsay only as regards the
little girl from Bendigo. Here are some plain
unvarnished facts, coming from an unquestionable
source: Marchesi was ‘disappointed in Amy
Castles, ’ as The Bulletin stated some weeks ago
because she had been led to expect a taller girl’
with a true soprano voice—‘another Melba,’ in
fact. But there was nothing seriously the matter
with Amy’s notes when she came to hand, re-
freshed by her restful sea voyage. Marchesi
never expressed a doubt of her ability to improve
the little woman into a first-class operatic mezzo if
the little woman has the necessary patience and
brains. Thus far Amy Castles is progressing
satisfactorily, although she seems rather absurdly
homesick, and suffers more than ever from the
habit of being ‘ ill ’ ere she can brace up her nerves
for any singing ordeal.
“ The tales told of Marchesi’s fees, ferocity,
and ‘ frill ’ are wild fabrications. It has been
said that she condemns young pupils (Amy Castles
them) to 12 months’ scale-practice before
allowing them to attempt songs at the Academy.
As a matter of fact no pupil is kept to ‘scales'’
for longer than five weeks. Marchesi is neither
extravagantly high in her charges, unkindly severe
m her discipline, nor lax in her attention to duty.
The old lady of 74 (she doesn’t look a day older
than 60) is the devoted friend and personal in-
structress of the girls. All of them,—the
stupidest and the brightest—get equal chances to
improve—the same chance that Melba got of
yore. Regina Nagel, it seems, rather grieved
Marchesi by going off to London six months
before her time. Her rich voice is now beauti-
fully smooth ; in fact it is perfect, but her vocali-
sation needs finishing touches, and the keen
critics of Dublin, where she made her first- public
appearance, pointed out to the new Australian
contralto that she has still a little, the all-impor-
tant little, to 1 earn as she goes along.”
“A dainty little girl, whose fair thatch and
remarkably blue eyes have done sentry-duty on
the Block for the last five or six years, has just
landed one of the matrimonial prizes of the
season.
“Few people seem to have heard of the fraeat
at a ‘ social evening,’ not long ago. It was all on
account of a certain professional scandal, whereby
a well-known identity suffered a severe shock.
At the head of the faction which brought him to
book was a diminutive personage, but somehow
the two brother practitioners had managed to
avoid a face-to-face meeting until an injudicious
hostess brought them together. Then the big
man smote the little one, and would have given
him an awful time, perhaps, if the other guests
hadn’t put a stop to the one-sided combat. Really
a lord of creation ceases to be an altogether
noble animal as soon as his angry passions rise.
We hear about a bygone age of chivalry, when
champions disdained to take mean advantages of
one another. Was there ever such an age?
Didn’t the bulky knight in ridiculous armour pre-
sume upon his avoirdupois in matters of
angry altercation with any under-sized braves
of the period, and wasn’t the gallant duel-
list always looking out for easy marks for his
superior skill ? I fancy that the fire-eater who
ordered pistols for two and coffee for one usually
felt sure of being able to sip the Mocha after the
event.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.
The humorous suggestion, made in all solemnity
by the dull-witted Jingoes whom the Australian
States have appointed their Premiers, to the effect
that J udas Chamberlain should have a day fixed
on which the Empire might rejoice over the
successful termination of the war, was coldly re-
ceived in England. The reply was merely that
the British Govt, did not at present contemplate
proclaiming a day for that purpose. No wonder !
J udging from present appearances, it will be time
enough to consider the question of peace-rejoicings
a few years hence. The guerilla war in the
Transvaal has begun in earnest. Thousands of
Afrikanders are still in arms and are acting, not
now against the British front, but striking side-
ways againt the thin, long-drawn lines by which
their enemies communicate with the sea. Already
tbe_ war has spread again, if the cable is to be
believed, back into Natal and Cape Colony. In
each the railway line has been cut, which means
possible distress for Pretoria and Johannesburg.
The two Boer republics are being laid waste ;
the British army at “the front” must get
its food by road or rail, or starve, and if the Boers
can still occasionally delay the bread-basket in
transit, peace isn’t in sight yet.

The following, from East London Dis-
patch (an Imperialistic paper) throws an interest-
ing sidelight on the wild yell for the shooting of
“neutrality oath-breakers” which has gone up
from the Jingo crowd :
Heilbron, in the Orange River Colony, has undergone
some remarkable experiences of late. Both the English
and Dutch forces have occupied it such a number of times
that the inhabitants hardly know, until they look at the
nag over the Magistrate’s Court, whether the town for
tiie day owes allegiance to Her Majesty, or is claimed by
Theron’s guerillas. Since May 23—four months ago—it
has changed its rule no less than seven times.
The diary of events as regards Heilbron is therefore as
follows :
Up to May 23—Under Dutch rule.
May 23—General Hamilton marched in, hoisted the
Union Jack, and proclaimed British rule.
May 24—British forces withdrew from the town.
May 26—Dutch re-occupation. Voss appointed Land-
drost.
June 2 (about) —General Macdonald and Highland Bri-
gade drove the Dutch out. British flag re-hoisted.
Grant appointed Resident Magistrate.
July 26—Heilbron evacuated by British. Re-occupied
by Theron. ‘
August 16—Generals Hutton and Macdonald re-estab-
lish British rule.
September 15—Heilbron again evacuated by the Bri-
tish.
September 16—Theron once more hoists Vierkleur.
Heilbron is a rather important town, and yet even,
there the British forces do not seem to be capable of
guaranteeing protection to those Boers who sub-
mit and take tne oath of allegiance to Britain, and
yet who, when the British depart and leave them
unprotected, are liable to death if they refuse to
join the Boer forces when called upon. When the
British are able to protect those who submit from
those who don’t, then, and not till then, there will
be some justification for the yell to punish oath-
breakers.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The story goes that last financial year the
nett profit on the publication of the Age was
£42,000.
Three ex-members for Mudgee in N.S.W.
Leg. Assembly have now died at Callan Park
Asylum.
Saul Samuel’s estate was valued at
£15,103, which goes chiefly to his widow for her
life, then to his family.
The fist of Hogan, M.P., is apparently
discernible in a news clip, explanatory of the
Wroeites. Johanna Southcott
left two chief followers, John
Wroe and George Turner.
Wroe put Turner down,
and prophesied his death,
which speedily happened.
Then Wroe went all over
Europe, and several times
to America, where he had
thousands of converts. !
Several visits to Austra- 1
lia were even more fruit-
ful. No place was so kindly
as Collingwood, the Melbourne
suburb. This locality raised for
him a large sum of money,
with which he built Melbourne
House, in Yorkshire. His
chief church was at Ashton,
in England, built with
one donation of £4OOO.
He died in Collingwood.
Soufe say he’s alive—
even on this side of the
water. Fisher, the Nuna-
wading Messiah, tried to
carry on his business.
Downes Carter, the
Melbourne grog-merchant,
orating at a little bean-feast
got up in honor of a bunch of

THE TROUBLES OF CELEBRITY.
JOCKEY : “ Can’t quite ride the weight by Satur-
day, mum. I’m doin’ my best, and, of
course, I’ll have my ’air cut again on the
morning of the race, but I’m afraid I’ll have
to carry a couple o’ pound extra.”
OWNER’S WIFE (anxiously): “Pity you haven’t
been disappointed in love lately. It would
bring you down quicker than anything.”
JOCKEY: “ That’s the worst, mum. All my gels
is as true as steel. There ain’t one of ’em
would jilt me, not if I went down on my
knees and arsked ’er to.”
“ Sir John Langdon Bony th on and Lady
Bonython, who have been paying a visit to
England and the Continent are returning to
South Australia by R.M.S. Rome”— Cable. And

Spielmann, in his history of London
Punch, shows that the yarn of £5 having been
given to an outsider, for one line—“ Advice to
people about to marry: Don’t ”—is all fudge.
Mayhew, of the staff, wrote it. A Melbourne
man, J. B. Stiffe, used to claim the credit.
The difference between David Syine and
his journalistic rivals was strongly demonstrated
during the recent Vic. election campaign. The
Conservatives went to the Argus to tell what
they were going to do ; the Liberals went to the
Age to ask what they were going to do.
Prof. Marshall-Hall, it is averred, has
written, and will shortly produce, an oratorio en-
titled “Jonah and the Whale.” The music
descriptive of the dyspeptic sounds made by the
whale in the course of its abortive attempts to
digest the prophet is said to be an artistic triumph.
No wonder that Premier Lyne has been
going slow re preparations for the reception of the
Governor-General. The N.S.W. Premier means
to snatch the Commonwealth Premiership if he
can, and the reception must be so stage-managed
as to secure the whole of the limelight for W. J.L.
all the time.
Chief Justice Darley’s Longstaff picture
drew the remark that, apart from the Chiefs,
N.S.W. Judges receive very small public recog-
nition. The Chiefs are all commemorated, but
the puisne men don’t strike the imagination. And
yet some of them have been greater judges than
the occupants of the front seat.
The mordant jealousy existent between
the N.S.W. Premier and the Mayor of Sydney re
the reception of the Governor-General and the
Duke of York accounts for many things. It ac-
counts, for instance, for the idea put forward from
time to time that the incoming potentates should
land at certain places within the Colonial Secre-
tary’s jurisdiction and outside that of the Mayor.
“Lorrie” Harnett, the massive N.S.W.
Sergeant-at-Arms, is to be Sergeant-at-Arms of
the Commonwealth. That is irrevocably fixed.
Nature fixed it in giving Lorrie a grip of 40-
policeman power and calves surpassing in shapeli-
ness and bulk anything else ever seen in silk
stockings south of the equator.
When the late champion chess-player
Whistler was in Melb. years ago, he took the
prize for the ugliest man given at an entertain-
ment in St. George’s Hall. He was a genial
fellow, and enjoyed the position hugely, his corru-
gated features, as he accepted the distinction,
assuming an expansive smile that won all hearts.
Of the seven N.S.W. outlaws on each of
whose heads £lOOO was placed, Joe Governor is
the fourth to fall to the summary bullet. Of the
four, Gilbert and Hall fell to the shooting of the
police, and Morgan and Governor to the guns of
civilians. Dunn and Clarke (Thomas) were cap-
tured by the police, and Jimmy Governor by
civilians.
Monsiguor O’Brien is influentially tipped
as Cardinal Moran’s new coadjutor Bishop. A
coadjutor has right of succession. The three
names scoring highest at the local polling will be
submitted to the Pope, who may reject the three
or appoint the lowest scorer. Dr. O’Haran has a
small chance, but the post may go to a dark horse
in Ireland.
The book of the voyage of the Duke of
Clarence and Duke of York in H.M.S. Bacchante,
has a circumstantial account of the appearance of
the Flying Dutchman, between Melbourne and
Sydney. Thirteen people saw it —a red, luminous
appearance, with the figure of a brig. The man
wao first sighted it fell from the masthead same
day, and was killed.
When the late Mortimer Franklyn, of bogus
newspaper-schemes renown, reached Melbourne,
he was stranded, and hard pressed to find a
respectable address from which to issue his
speculative proposals. After casting about,
he succeeded in persuading the Age
proprietor to give him the use of a
room in his office under the pretence
that he was able to secure large
advertising orders for the paper
from America. The advts. never
turned up, but the expedient
served Franklyu’s purpose admir-
ably for a time.

Matthew Davies, at one of his recent elec-
tion meetings, was chaffed about the stolen Vic.
Parliamentary mace. The popular version is that
the bauble was taken for a lark to a house of
pleasure by a raffish member of the Assembly, who,
becoming alarmed on cooling down, threw it into
the Yarra or buried it in a safe place. He after-
wards died in England.
When Hopetoun was Victorian Governor,
a local anarchist sent him a copy of a ferocious
original work on the destruction of Melbourne by
fire and bomb, with trimmings of savagery which
quite outdid the deeds of Robespierre and Marat.
Lord Hopetoun sent a courteous letter of acknow-
ledgement, stating that he had read the book
“ with much interest.”
“Pukaki”: You ask (B. 14/10/00)
“ What’s Stout’s game ? ” Stout is a noble fellow
—big-hearted, big-headed, and thoroughly demo-
cratic. But he must always be talking, always
posing. In this respect he is a striking contrast
to Richmond, the only M.L. Judge who ever
rivalled him intellectually. Stout, in fact, is
about an equal mixture of Lilley and Madden.
Plain Bill Robinson, a member of Vic.
Leg. Assembly for N. Melbourne 40 years
ago, had the gloomy spectacle of this General
Election before him, as he paced Collins-street, no
man Iveeding. He is an inmate of the workhouse,
the Immigrants’ Home, and complained to Par-
liament the other week that dry bread was given
him with his tea. “ The talk of the lips tendeth
to penury,” saith the Bible.
Died a few days ago at Henra (SiQ.)
George Lionel Lukin, well - known Bananaland
P.M. and gold-warden. Mr. Lukin was born in
Tasmania, educated in London, and transplanted
to Queensland in ’63. Rose rapidly from the posi-
tion of C.P.S. to that of P.M., and gained a repu-
tation for sterling honesty and courageous impar-
tiality rarely equalled. He was brother to
Gresley Lukin, of Wellington Post (M.L.), for-
merly of Brisbane Courier.
Apropos the quarrel of Sleath, M.P.,
chairman of N.S.W. Military Inquiry Commit-
tee, with Major-General French over the latter’s
refusal to make public particulars of the de-
fences, Sleath was one time in much the same
position as French is now. He was commandant
of an army of strikers, and in that capacity was
a martinet. To men who wanted to see the in-
side works his reply always was, “If you can’t
trust your officers, sack ’em.”
The Duke of Manchester's estate in bank-
ruptcy shows debts £15,000, assets £3OOO, being
the balance of the proceeds of sale of the Kimbol-
ton farming stock. The young man’s friends have
allowed him £7O a month, but he has spent double.
The Bulletin begins to feel sympathetic towards
its old friend, “ Guinea-pig ” Manchester’s grand-
son, for it must be difficult to play a dook character
on not more than £l6BO a year, especially if one is
borrowing half the money at 40 per cent.
Beauchamp was N.S.W.’s twentieth
Governor. He was also her youngest Governor.
He also ruled for the shortest period—a month or
two less than Bligh, who was deposed. The aver-
age from 1788 would give, therefore, about five-
and-a-half years for each Governor. Of course,
lieutenant-governors filled up many gaps; but
Macquarie stayed 11 years. In modern times
Hercu.es Robinson stayed seven, Carrington five,
and Loftus six. In the last seven years N.S.W.
has put through three Governors —Duff, Hamp-
-1
den, and Beauchamp. Of all the Governors
Duff was the only one who frequently declared he
would not return to England till he completed hie
full official term—five years. He died at the end
of two.
Mr. Benjamin Short, who lately returned
to Sydney from Europe, has been giving an inter-
viewer his impressions of travel. The matters
most prominent in his recollection appear to
be the house Spurgeon died in, a W.C.T.U. Con-
vention, a gathering of parsons at Keswick, a
passing glimpse of the Queen, and a wounded
Australian soldier ! Truly a frantic round of dis-
sipation ! If the venerable Ben, however, has T
herein set forth all his little relaxations while “on
the wing,” he is probably a very safe sort of
A.M.P. director.
Some men who have no luck. A year before
that genial soul Melb. University Chancellor
Anthony Brownless (Vic.) died, he was run over
by a cab, which broke his left-leg. Had it been
his right leg it would not have mattered, because
that limb was made of cork. When news was
brought to Napoleon Bonaparte that a brave
French officer had been killed in his seventh
action, after being wounded in each of his previous
six engagements, the great soldier exclaimed, in a
fit of anger, “Damn that man ; he’s got no luck !”
Vic. Mines Department “has awarded a
certificate for bravery and presence of mind to
John Maddern, miner, recently seriously injured
by an explosion in the Star of the East mine,
Sebastopol. Maddern and a mate were working
at the 1122 ft. level when an explosion took place.
Maddern lost his eyesight, and was otherwise
badly injured, yet he crept up a swinging ladder
for 22ft., and crawled along the main drive for
half a mile to bring assistance to his mate whose
arm had been blown off, and who lay uncon-
scious.” Think of it! This man who had just
been violently blinded, did a thing to bring help
to his mate that quite equals all that has ever
been done in the hot blood of battle—and they
give him “a certificate,” which may mean no
more than a license to beg, It will be a robbery
if any wounded Transvaal volunteer gets a State
pension while one is denied Maddern.
“ Itan ” to The Bulletin re Dr. Bern-
stein, who has been called upon to resign his
small Govt, billets at Lismore (N.S.W.), because
of alleged pro-Boer sympathies and seditious
expressions:—
The Richmond River district owes to Dr. Bernstein a
debt of gratitude it can never repay, because of his self-
denying efforts to promote settlement and make Lismore
habitable. He is a Polish Jew, instinctively belligerent,
and as a young man took a burning interest in the fierce
troubles resultant on the Russian annexation of his
fatherland. For his political actions he seemed doomed
to Siberia, but managed to escape and soon found himself
in Australia. Almost a quarter of a century ago he
settled in the Big Scrub. He has been a friend of the
poor, and Lismore never had, nor has it now, a better
citizen. He has been elected by the people to every post
that they could give him and which he could find time to
accept, and has in all cases most worthily discharged his
trust. Granted that he did, under excitement, say some-
thing of the Queen that he should not have said, does
not his long-tried practical loyalty as a good citizen
stand as a set-off to his sudden indiscretion? Even if
the old man made use of “ language ” (being of foreign
birth something might surely have been allowed for his
irritation at the sickening whiskied lip-loyalty of the
hour) there is the fact that he actually offered his medi-
cal services for the front, while the horror-stricken
Jingoes who reported him to head-quarters were satisfied
to yell “ God Save the Queen,” and toot penny trumpets
over the relief of Mafeking.

Aboriginalities.
Dear Bulletin.— Commenting on the
recent home-coming of some landed personage up
Coolangatta way Sydney Mail, in its best forelock-
-1 lulling manner, maundered over the good old
English style of welcoming home the master and
mistress, and remarked in a casual way that such
things on a small scale have happened before,
when some squatter has brought home a bride to
the station. Quite right—such things have
happened. On (say) Bargowlahmine station some
.10 years ago manager MTavish brought home a
new wife; and the whole of the “ peasantry and
sturdy yeomanry ’’—consisting of two white stock-
men, the storekeeper, three blackfellows and two
B_ms turned out and gave them a grand reception.
We only had a quart of rum to start with, but,
with the aid of spirits-of-wine, tobacco and strong
tea, we made it up to about a gallon of very good
grog, so that when the buggy arrived things were
a bit mixed. Just as the storekeeper began to
read an address of welcome, whilst two stockmen
held him up, Mac., seeing one of the black stock-
men winking at the bride, made a cut at him with
the buggy whip, and then the fun started. When
we woke up in the morning Brigalow Jack lay on
the floor badly tomahawked. Mao’s face was like
a frying-pan, and the missus was missing. One
of the gins had taken her to a hut about 5 miles
out, and when she came in she threatened to go
back to where she came from. Now that’s what I
call a real honest Australian “bring-in.”— Coll
Rab.

Remarkable genuine letter (dated from a
way-back Q. region), showing how strong super-
stition is yet in the land :
Deer , I might not send this before ido get Sunday’s
male, your ticket in the twelve thousand will win, i will
give you 25/- for that ticket, i wall bet you two glasses of
ginger here it wins, the 6271 ticket‘is my birth day
tickee, i never won a pris on 6 thousand yet, I won on the
7 thousand with and . I wish you had
as many tickets in this 20/- as ine, there is no one got any
interest in my shares but you, if i win my money back, i
shall be a lucky man; my little cat here got pups and i
am not going to kill them till the Melbourne Cup is over.
Dam it, every year the Melbourne Cup comes round there
is a lot of cats for me to kill ; dam the cats, if i don’t win
i will chop them up in mince meat, this is 3 years follow-
i g°t to kill cats i can hear a squeeker down the creek
now singing out win or not win i had to kill 7 cats this
morning, i burned the last one i killed to burn the witch-
craft as the old people youse to do, stick nine pins in
there ear & bum them if we dont win we chall be bust-
up, i hope we shall win in the Caulfield, i fancy we shall
& i expect fancy is all

Dear Bulletin. —At Ballarat railway-sta-
tion, t’other week, I heard a man say, “ ’M gun
up town f’r thush oh—s’long um cun back uh
Wendy.” Asked a bystander what language it
was. He replied, “N’glish, wuh s’rong wit 1”
Said that I’d never heard English like that before.
Then said he, “G’lord, nyer dun egspeck um er
talk like umchine, jer ? ” Have since discovered
that this is the local vernacular. Will some Bul-
LETiN-ist give me its technical name ?—Peteb K.

“ Canip ” : Benighted in the country
t’other day I struck a selector’s place and was
hospitably entertained. Supper was served on an
extensive table, but only two places set, at one of
which my host immediately dumped himself
down. I hesitated and looked towards the
missus, but was told “ Wire in, young fellow, and
let her rip. She can feed afterwards ! ”

“ V.” : When, some years back, in N.Q.,
a blackfellow had been kicked to death by
drunken navvies, his widow attended the post
mortem and showed great interest in the doctor’s
carvery. Presently she said “You gib it me
bone longa knee, doctor.” The doctor, three-
parts full of chain-lightning, handed over the
detached knee-caps, asking “What for you want
’em, Mary?” “I bin thinkit, doctor, after you
bury this fella I git ’nother fella Benjamin, an’
sposen Peter there he no likit, I gotum bone and
he no able to get up and come along, walk about,
walk about all the time.” Then, depositing the
keys of pedestrianism in her dilly-bag and draw-
ing her toga closer about her, the lady moved off
calmly to secure No. 2.

Dear Bulletin. —Re that statement (com-
mented on B, 3/11/’00) that eight men were killed
in a Transvaal hailstorm. I witnessed, in ’74, in
Milan, Italy, a hailstorm the “stones” of which
averaged the size of mature oranges. No human
lives were lost, because people got promptly under
cover, but several horses were killed during the 20
minutes the storm lasted—J.A.C.
* * * m
From Tibooburra (N.S.W.) ;
Out at Tibooburra, where an average of some hi lg
over 50 children are penned in a stone cell (caked a
school) 25ft. x 15ft., with the thermometer reading at
times 115• in the shade, Govt, has accepted tenders for
additions to the local court-house, a building not used,
on an average, three days in the month. The present
building (stone) has more than ample accommodation for
the business transacted, and the contemplated additions
are utterly unnecessary'. Why the money should not be
devoted to relieving the shamefully-packed condition of
the local school is a mystery to the inhabitants. The
P.AI. from Milparinka only attends once a fortnight, and
even then often draws a blank, for Tibooburra men,
through the drought, are too poor to get drunk, and too
discouraged to even say “ Blanky.”

From the drought-cursed far north-west
of N.S.W. :
7in. of rain in ’99, and not one inch since March. Fresh
beef and mutton almost unknown quantities. The salt-
bush alone keeps life in the starving stock. Out of a mob
of 200 I saw 180 die within a short distance. To put a
horse in the hotel stable costs 7s. 6d. per night. If no
rain comes this month we are finally cooked ! ”

To such large proportions lias the parcel-
post system grown out-back in these droughty
times, when ordinary team-carriage is so uncer-

Dear Bulletin, —Cycling along a level
plain between Aramac and Rockhampton I was
amused to see several emus race after me to have
a look. Others joined in as we went, until the
mob numbered over 50. They had a nasty trick
of lowering their heads and making for me with
the neck at full stretch, almost level with the
ground. I wasn’t used to emus and didn’t like
the booming noise in which they expressed their
entiments, so I made the machine go for all it
was worth, and wore my pursuers down at last.
A.bout eight miles had been covered. Solid fact !
—Kuklos.
In the late 70 s a well-known
character in Wide Bay (Q.) dis.
trict owned a dusky lady who bore
his name in an honorary sort of way
and lived with him at his fishing
camp on Fraser’s Island. When-
ever he brought the lady to town
she religiously got on a frantic spree,
and when it was time to go home
again it was no uncommon thing to
see the gentleman with the sable
dame on his back striding down to
his boat, sweet and comforting
words being sown along the high
way by two voices in active discord.
The lady would be flung into the
boat with much energy, but ten
minutes later she’d be at her oar and
her pipe. She lived many years
after her husband had married a
white wife, and passed away in the
odor of sanctity on the reputation
of having at one time been Mrs. t
* * *
They have raised a beet at
Blowalong, S.A., 36^ft long. Don’t
smile, just listen. The pump on
Jones’ block got stuffed—struck
work from age. Bill Murphy (son
of old Murphy) filled up the well
and pump with manure (stable) and
set a few beets. One struck
straight down the pump only to be
stopped at the brick bottom.
B.M. swears it would have broken
the Yankee record, 53ft, but for
the bricks. He would be proud to
know if it’s the Australian record.
Dear Bulletin,— What kind of a race is
Central Queensland rearing ? I know of more
than one bachelor-owned station on which a nice
little family of half-breeds is growing up to be a
joy and comfort to their various papas. On one
place, owned and managed by two gilded Johnnies
of the London club variety, the pair of black
housekeepers have taken it in turns for the last 4
years to increase the half - caste population.
There being on the station no conveniences for
such an interesting domestic event, last year
rooms were engaged at the pub. in the nearest
township, and a certificated nurse retained to help
the ladies through their ordeal. No doubt these
are the little things that give the necessary air of
reality to our higher civilisation and teach hum-
llityto the sisters and the cousins and the aunts
of the gentlemen who are thus not too proud to
take some savage woman—let her rear their
dusky brood.”—Buzz.

“ Ababon ” : It may be a coincidence only,
but I have never yet met a bush-schoolmaster who
could or would speak grammatically. In this
town, the children in the playground may be
heard saying “yous,” “that bees,” “yous
them there,” “it aint,” &c., while the peda-
ls 0 # 11 ® stands by, listening quite unconcernedly.
Less drill and more grammar would, I think, effect
an improvement.
In that part of northern S. A.
where drought has temporarily
gone out of business, grasshoppers
ave taken its place. The swarms
have been so thick that trains have
been hung up—one for an hour-
and-a-half —the loco, wheels refus-
ing to grip rails made greasy by
crushed insects.

He was a “ daisy ” sample of
the newly-imported Johnnie, and
lie was superintending the surface
works at the Black Horse in Mt.
Margaret district, W.A. His
usual way of summoning a work-
man was: “Come heaji, felloh,
felloh, come he-ali. Not you, fel-
loh ” (as perhaps the wrong man
would start towards him) — “you
felloh ” and his forefinger would
indicate the desired “felloh.” A
Bananaman was one day wheel-
ing a load of concrete up a plank,
the men on top waiting for him,
when, scandalised at such loss of
time, the Johnnie called out to
the man staggering under the load :
“Wun, feiloh, wun ! ” And the
fellow “ wan,” but without his
barrow and in the direction of the
astonished boss, whom he landed
neatly under the ear, laying him
on a heap of quartz. Then he con-
tinued “ wunning” till he reached
the office, where he drew his pay
and left.

The dog-stiffener was sitting
in his turn-out, in front of the
pub., when the “’possum-peeler”
paddled up. Often being a tres-
passer on the run, the latter thought
to insure himself for another moon
by asking the former to have a
drink, but the d.-s. said his horse
would not stand. The p.-p. :
“ Well, take the bob an’ get yours
while I hold the horse, an’ I’ll ’ave
mine after.” The d.-s. goes in,
calls for his drink, and then says
to the barmaid ■ “Do you see that
’ere flunkey of mine hout there,
’oldin’ the ’orse ? When ’e comes
hin, give ’im a drink. There’s the
money for it! ” And he threw
down the p.-p.’s shilling.
“Warrego”: Recently had for fellow-
travellers in a N.Q. coach one Chinaman, one
lunatic, one prisoner of the Crown, and one
policeman in charge of the two last. At starting
we had also the principal witness against the
prisoner, but he gave up the ghost early in the
journey, so presumably the case broke down.

“Alex. M. ” : Re “Dixie” (B. 22/9/00).
The mistaken notion that the bloodhound is
naturally an unusually fierce dog is probably due
to the name. Like all hounds, the b. h. is uncertain
in temper, but otherwise he is not specially
savage ; though—strange to say—a cross of this
breed with the solid-tempered English mastiff
produces a particularly ferocious animal.

An olct yam, but good : Between two
German farmers in Vic. Western District there
was bad blood, which resulted in Hans shooting
Otto’s dog. Police-court proceedings followed,
and the P.M. asked defendant if he had shot
plaintiff’s dog. “ Veil, yes,” said Hans, “ I shoot
dot tog—but let him brove it.” The magistrate
then asked plaintiff the value of the dog. “ Veil,”
said Otto, “de tarn tog vosmuddings vorth —but,
since he shood him, let him bay for. him;” “ Case
struck out ! ”

“ Wilgar ” : Very no-
ticeable in the Wimmera
district of Vic. is the pre-
sent scarcity of magpies.
When, 25 years ago, the
Durham diggers slew the first
gum, the warble of this bird filled the air, yet
to-day there is practically no magpie.
“Pelican” : I saw a curious lightning-
stroke on Mt. Morgan road, C.Q. An ironbark,
about 15in. in dia., had its trunk cut off about
18in. from the ground and again about 10ft. up.
The tree did not fall—it dumped on to its shat-
tered end and leaned against another tree. The
cut-out portion lay round in fragments.
A STREET SKETCH.
Emmanud Antonetti, the well-known hunchback Sydney
street-singer, who died suddenly last week.
“ Tomahawk ” : Passing through the
Northern agricultural districts of N.S.W. you find
universal amongst the cockies dungaree pants and
butcher-blue shirts or dungaree jumpers. In the
public-schools, also, half the boys are clad in the
same. What about butcher-blue for a uniform
for our Australian swaddies? —cool, cheap, and
appropriately-named. Also, when the Australian
call to arms comes, and the tillers of the soil have
to turn their ploughshares into bayonets, there will
be no time lost over uniforms, as the majority
will be ready to step into the ranks as they are.
• * * *
“The British Consul in German E.
Africa,” writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, “reports the complete success in that
territory of Dr. Koch’s method of rendering cattle
tick-proof. He had studied the pest on the spot
and by taking ticks off coast cattle into the in-
terior.and putting the offspring of these ticks on
healthy cattle produces a mild form of the sick-
ness and immunity therefrom afterwards.”
* * * *
On Yandama station, N.W. corner of
N.S.W., they have nearly 200 miles of wire-fence
telephone circuit. Some of the oyt-stations are
40 miles distant from the head-station, and the
lines work well. The amount of wear and tear
of horseflesh saved is enormous, and other stations
are about to follow the idea.
In Fiji, in the heathen times, it, was
considered indecent for any woman to have more
than four children, and these were only allowed
to arrive at intervals of several years. When a
missionary told King Thakombau that a certain
exalted lady had nine children, Thakombau
exclaimed—well, it wouldn’t be gentlemanly to
print Thakombau’s exclamation.

ALBERT DORRINGTON, author of “Castro a
Last Sacrament, and Other Stories ,” just
published by THE BULLETIN. (From a draw-
ing by F. P. Mahoney.)
“ Fred. B.” on the scrub-turkey wrangle :
The s.t. is a small species of turkey-bustard and lays
not more than 3 eggs in a very primitive nest. The
mallee-hen, or lowan, is a bird of another feather. Both
“ Bushie ” and “ Mutton-Bird” (B. 18 8/’00) call the latter
a brush-turkey. In anv case, however, “ M. 8.” must
have seen double when he saw 40 of'them building a nest,
which he afterwards found full of eggs. “ Bushie,” too,
is, I think, outside the mark when he says he saw 15 at
work at one nest. I have, in N.S.W., robbed as many as
27 nests once a week, and never saw more than 4 birds
working at the biggest nests. I could always count on
getting a fresh egg in a nest once a week. The eggs,
when fresh, are of a delicate pink color, but as they get
stale they turn white. The chicken conies out of the
nest fully-fledged, and goes “on its own "at once. Have
got. as manv as 30 eggs out of a nest, but only 3 were
good. Blacks do not go out after eggs until late in the
season. They prefer the eggs with chickens in (hem.
Like ‘ Bushie,” I have wondered how a lowan di posed
of her eggs in a dry season. Have often seen nests
opened out with rubbish in the hole in centre ready for
rain that never came.

Dear Bulletin. — To “ Moke ” (8.,
22/9/’00). People who know anything about
treating horses do not drench through the nostrils.
If the horse won’t swallow the draught after it is
poured into his mouth, a little water may be
poured into one of the nostrils to make him gulp
it down. Some pinch the windpipe. In-
experienced people .sometimes kill horses by
drenching through the nostrils, but the practice is
not common. —L.

Ed. Bulletin.— ln referring (20/10/’00)

Premier Seddon’s motion in favor of in-
corporating Fiji with Maoriland was passed by 40
votes to If. Before the passing of the motion,
Seddon read Chamberlain’s cablegram saying that
the British Govt, wasn’t prepared to make any
change in the present Fijian arrangements, but
he courageously assured the House that this
violent set-back was really rather favorable than
otherwise. Whereupon a good part of the House
smiled at the top of its voice. Russell in-
quired if they were going to have 16 Fijian niggers
in the House, but he was crushed by being called
a Little Englander and a type of the degraded
class whose subservience to Kruger had embroiled
the empire, &c. That was decisive, and the
Broiled Empire party went down badly.
A Q. trooper in S’Africa on the Average
war-yam :
We are beginning to receive Queensland papers now,
and the published letters from some of the men are
“ cautions.” Saw a sickening letter from one of the Ist
Contingent telling how Private got hold of 9 horses
at Kiorn Spruit and rescued a gun, and on going back to
rescue another was wounded. It was a pack of lies from
beginning to end—bow fellows can write such stuff beats
me Yet another man wrote an account of the Kiom
Spruit affair which read as if he had been in the thick
of it. As a matter of fact, he was with our supply wag-
gons and did not catch us up until night. We were not
actuallv in the fight that day, the nearest shell exploding
half-a-mile from where I (with others) was scouting, and
that is as near as any of the 2nd were to it. I hear also
that another of our men has written a most bloodthirsty
account of his adventures up Thaban’chu way, where we
scarcely saw any of the enemy.
Herewith appears the portrait of one of
the pioneers of Australian settlement, Mr. W. H.
Hill, who died recently, at Bondi, Sydney, aged
72. Mr. Hill’s early life was very adventurous.
He it was who, when assisting Allan Macpherson
to hold Mt. Abundance against the depredations
and assaults of the aboriginals ; was the last white
man to speak with Leichhardt at his camp, near
Mt. Inviting; and .he it was also who brought to
Sydney the lost explorer’s last despatches. Mr.
Hill has told present writer that, even at that
early stage, Leichhardt’s party showed signs of
helplessness and disorganisation. Hill was a fine
type of bushman —a “cattle-man,” pure and
simple, of the old tough stamp, that hardly knew
what fatigue meant. Some of his stories of early
“ dispersements ” were strongly interesting.
Blacks in those days, he said, if troublesome, were
“thought no more of than flies.” Hill was a
friend of Hargreaves, and was one of the earliest
on the newly-discovered fields. His Tyson re-
miniscences were specially interesting, and not
like so many recent Tyson yams, pure inventions.
Very important developments will follow
Tesla’s recent discovery of a method of transmit-
ting electrical power over long distances, practi-
cally without the waste hitherto caused by the
transformation of much of the energy into heat
through the resistance of the transmitting-wire to
the current. The conductivity of metals, it has
been found, is enormously increased by extreme
coldness, and, with an adequately cold insulation
of the wires, there is, it is stated, “practically no
limit to the distance to which electric power may
he conveyed.”
THE LATE J. W. SWEENEY , COMEDIAN.

Spring-Time Delusions.
FACTS vs. CONJECTURE.
A Few Valuable Hints to Those Who are
Quick to Take Advantage of Timely
Suggestions.
Just now the advertising space of the average
newspaper is filled with advertisements of spring-
time medicines, blood purifiers, etc. When it is
understood as a scientific truth that the kidneys,
liver, and lungs are the only blood purifiers of the
human body, much disappointment and money
will be saved. Dizziness, palpitation, languid
feelings, headache, backache, etc., are caused by
the system being poisoned by uric acid, which the
kidneys through overwork or weakness, do not
eliminate. The troubles mentioned are but
symptoms. The cause lies deeper. Put the
kidneys and liver in working order with that
vegetable specific, Warner’s Safe Cure, and the
symptoms and attendant troubles will disappear.
With the system in first-class condition, and the
great organs of life doing the work nature in
tended them to do, most diseases are thrown off.
It is the weakened, debilitated, poisoned body
which falls an easy prey to chills, fevers,
pneumonia, consumption, Bright’s l isease, and
death. No man or woman can afford to be laid
up with a severe illness. It is the part of wisdom
to keep well. Mr. H. Fawley, miner, of Scott-
street, Newcastle, N.S.W., understands this
perfectly. On 10th September, 1000, he writes :
“About two years ago I was greatly troubled
with pains across my loins. My appetite was
very poor, and I could scarcely get any sleep at
night. I tried several well-known remedies but
got no relief. By chance I got hold of a pamphlet
and saw a letter from a miner which seemed to
suit my case. I bought three bottles of Warner’s
Safe Cure and a phial of Warner’s Safe Pills, and
am glad to say that, when I had finished taking
these, I was completely cured, and have been in
the best of health ever since. ” Mr. Fawley’s
experience can be yours also. Will you not enjoy
it?*

So energetic have Melb. Trades Hall leaders been
this year that they have organised no less than
27 new unions, with a membership of over 6000,
besides bringing many erstwhile “ free laborers ”
into existing unions. Also, there are now no less
than 29 trades with wages boards under the Fac
tories Act, and a minimum rate of pay fixed by
law. In which connection it is interesting to
recollect that, when the Turner Govt, proposed
to put this extension power in the hands of the
Governor-in-Council, the House of Rich Landlords
fought persistently in favor of the wages-board
system being extended by resolution of both
Houses. It was only McLean’s happy idea of
giving each House the power, independently of
the other, to extend the minimum wage, that saved
the Bill. Up to now not one of the Leg. Coun-
cillors has moved to extend the minimum wage to
any trade, and if the original idea of making the
extension dependent on the consent of both
Houses had beeh adopted, progress would pro-
bably have been nil or thereabouts.
■-- ... - / -.—
“Tcrlutu ” on the lodge-doctor war :
The Vic. doctors are rushing over each other just now
to combine against the lodges, and, as usual, they have
left it too late. Only about one in every five of them has
any clear idea of what the coming struggle really means,
the great majority foreseeing only a row with the lodges
of their own particular locality which may possibly enable
them to keep the rates of payment from getting any
lower. Doctors are essentially a non-cohesive body, and
in no profession are childish jealousies more frequently
allowed to interfere with movements directed towards the
general good. The big guns take no active part in pro-
moting union, for they have given up club-practice and
have lost all personal interest in the affair. The
Medical Defence Society potters along with occasional
blasts on its little trumpet about what it will do some
day, but meanwhile it only potters. Meetings of sub-
centres are held, and occasionally an indignant lodge-
victim airs his troubles in a professional journal,
but only words —eternal words —are the outcome. The
realisation of the hard fact that they are going to fight—
not a tinpot collection of sons of toil, but a union of
powerful political societies—organised and run on poli-
tical lines, never enters the head of the average practi-
tioner. His troubles about the lodges, if he can only
grab something from a rival, meanwhile 1 The A.N.A.
having set the example of how to sweat doctors scien-
tifically, the other lodges are quick to follow it. Small
blame to them ! Nobody pays more for a thing than he
can help, and when the seller is ass enough to take a
starvation price for a popular article he must submit to
starve if he cannot fight. The Women’s Associations
which are clamoring to be taken at rates below those of
the ordinary clubs represent the thin edge of another
wedge. The concession will, of course, be granted, and
the next move follows naturally enough—the single men
will expect to come in on similar terms. At present the
margin afforded by a certain percentage of unmarried
healthy men in a club is the only thing which keeps the
affair a little above starvation rates. The average
general practitioner ought to sit down and think very
hard indeed before he next tries to undercut a rival for a
club. This scribe has the most profound disrespect for
fie common-sense of the medical profession as a body,
and he hereby lays very large odds that unless a thorough
change is instituted in the present system, or lack of
system, of medical union, the clubs will be able, inside of
three years, to get doctors on any terms they like to offer.

The Men of Gingerbread.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Old Henry Parkes at Faulconbridge
Lies sleeping sound and well;
Jack Rorertson has also gone—
Where, nobody can tell.
The Giants of the days of old
Are silent now, and dead.
Who takes their place ? Another Race —
The Men of Gingerbread.
The winged orb arises fair —
Our splendid Commonweal —
And what do they, these shapes outri
These flies upon the wheel ?
Our Sydney like a lioness
Lies drowsing by the tide —
She sees in vain her proud Domain —
Her glory and her pride—
Hacked up, and cut, and slashed away,
With savage knife and fork,
To please—good Heavens whom to please ?
To please the Duke of York.
There will be Hopetoun Avenue,
Lyne walk—and, it may be,
God only knows, but, we suppose,—
The Boulevard John See.
A traveller, in future years,
Looks round with satire fine—
“ And who,” says he, “was Mister See ?
And who was William Lyne ? ”
Old Henry Parkes at Faulconbridg*
Lies sleeping sound and well;
Jack Robertson has also gone
Where nobody can tell.
But they would turn within their graves
And curse, though they are dead,
If they could know the holy show
Made here upon this earth below
By Men of Gingerbread.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The death of R. H. D. White and the
appointment of B. R. Wise to the Upper House
leave N.S.W. Leg. Council at the same old 75.
Sydney Daily Telegraph (per “Presi-
dent ” Carey) boldly proclaims that the Federal
capital must be at the Canoblas. Now, how has
the D. T. been thus canobbled, and what sort of
milk is there in the cocoanut ?
David Syme thought “Jack” Murray
should have been fired out of his Warrnambool
seat because of his attitude on the Boer war.
Nevertheless, the all-wool democrat beat the cant-
ing Maxwell by a serviceable majority.
The triumph of the Vic. election is Labor
man Findley, in for Melbourne City, the whilom
lair of the Langtons, Murray Smiths and Carters.
Findley truly said, “ Gentlemen, you have placed
Labor at the pinnacle with the premier constitu-
ency in Victoria.”
Proposed in Queensland to release, in
honor of the Commonwealth, several of the bush-
men imprisoned in connection with strike disturb-
ances. Not to disorganise the Q. gaol system,
The Bulletin suggests that several prominent Q.
politicians be sent to take the prisoners’ places.
Already the shifting of population makes
it time N.S.W. had another redistribution of seats.
For instance, Wentworth has declined till it has
only 1495 qualified electors, while Willoughby has
grown to have 4470. There are now 17 electorates
with less than 2000 voters, while six have over
3500.
Despite the protests of the Attorney-
General, last week (Oct. 31) President Baker, as-
sisted by the Tory majority in the-S.A. Council,
clapped the closure on Labour member M‘Gre-
gor, who was trying to delay the passage of the
Snow Tram-grab Bill. Again the same Old
majority of one.
The Adelaide Calico Jimmies energetically
boosted George Reid’s visit in the interests of the
Shoddy cause. G.H.R. gave his audience a taste
of his slimness when, at the close of his address,
he asked those in favor of a legitimate tariff to
hold up their hands. Freetraders and Protec-
tionists alike held up both hands !
In N.S.W. Assembly, last week, was cited
the case of an estate, in the Paterson district, on
which 30 tenants at will had for 30 years made
improvements. They were dispossessed at the
landlord’s whim, without any compensation, and
actually sued for the value of iron they had re-
moved from sheds they had erected at their own
expense. '
Occasionally even now the Stace manages
to get hold of some of the “unearned increment ”
of land-values. Fourteen years ago a population
settled in an utter wilderness
round the Broken Hill mines—
on country worth at the most
ss. an acre freehold. Most
of the land was promptly
purchased, under the gold-
fields regulations, for small
amounts; but a few un-
considered trifles continued
to be held under miner’s
rights. For one of these, in
a back street, £Bl6 was paid
to the Crown t’other day.
And it was in an uncer-
tain and comparatively
small mining town, where
land is worth but a very
few years’ purchase, so
that the ground-rent was
estimated at £lOO a year.
Which illustrates wdiat a
glittering revenue the
Federal Govt, may derive
in a few years if it only
hangs on to the land within
its territory—land which it
will secure for a few pounds
an acre.
Melb. AGE says that
the Victorian constituen-
cies are never fickle, and
that a member of Assem-
bly that does his duty
honestly is practically
sure of his seat for life.
All the same, the average
number of Assembly represen-
tatives who lose their seats at each
general election is 30 out of 96.
Duncan Gillies once remarked that a
man’s place in Parliament always hung
by a thread, and that if he had the chance it
would be better for him to seek his career in some
decent business avocation.
The probability of Turner’s return to
power knocks a hole in the designs of those
who strenuously urge that Lyne, as Pre-
mier of the oldest State, must be sent for to form
the provisional Ministry. As Premier during the
Convention sittings, and one of the chief de-
signers of the Constitution Act, Turner must surely
be considered at least before anti-Federal Lyne.
The Bill of Affleck, M.L.A., to extinguish
the free travelling of “distinguished” people on
N.S.W. State railways was rejected in the Assem-
bly by 31 to 15. As usual half the members were
at billiards. The only Minister who voted for
the Bill was O’Sullivan. Lyne, Crick, See and
Fegan voted against. A few Laborites supported
his resolution, but Smith, Dacey and Sleath
opposed it.
The long-established practice of providing
carriages at State expense to convey N.S. Wales
M’sP. to the Governor’s levee is a villainous
robbery of the Treasury. It is just as villainous a
robbery as the erstwhile provision by the State of
carriages to take the members of N.S.W. Bar to
the levee aforesaid. By the way, has the latter
steal really been abolished, or is it still secretly
maintained ?
The Bulletin smiles when it thinks how
soft the Jingo party was in allowing the Austra-
lian Federation to adopt the Cromwellian “Com-
monwealth ” instead of the subservient “ Do-
minion ” as a name. How tough becomes the
position of those who now yell for emphasising
Queen Victoria’s Australian overlordship by
making her “Empress” or “Queen” of the
Commonwealth !
The Truck Bill flew once again through
N.S.W. Assembly last week, probably once more
to smash its wings against the walls of the Coun-
cil. Times out of number the Council has wrecked
similar Bills, or held them till a dissolution. And
still in N.S.W. goods are every day dumped down
as wages. If the Leg. Council rejects the Bill this
time the Govt, should appoint John Norton to a
seat in that Chamber.
Auckland (M.L.) Herald and Star are
just now on the horns of a very painful dilemma.
They are, probably, the two most Jingo papers in
all Maoriland. At the same time, they are vitally
interested in the maintenance of the ’Frisco mail
service. So, between their reverence for the
Union Jack and their knowledge that the ’Frisco
boats must fly the Goose and Gridiron they are in
a pitiful state of wobble.
The Victorians proved last week to have
longer memories than was supposed. Land-
boomer Matthew Davies got only 163 votes at
Melbourne and almost lost his gladsome deposit,
and James Munro at Toorak was beaten by Duncan
Gillies. He got 1197 votes; if his opponent had
been a different kind of man he would probably
have got only 200. Also, piteous to relate, old
Bryan O’Loghlen, erstwhile Vic. Premier, couldn’t
save his deposit in his old constituency, Milesian
Port Fairy.
Ex-Premier Reid says that it is Protection
which has forced the Victorian farmer to seek a
living in the mallee scrub country. Considering
that Victoria is only about one-quarter the size of
N.S.W., that one-fourth of it is rocky and
uncultivable Alp, and that it has already got more
land under cultivation than N.S.W., some people,
not so old and silly as Reid, might think they
went into the mallee because the mallee land was
good when cleared, and the rest of the land was
already occupied. But doubtless he holds that
Freetrade would make the country larger, so that
under it the Vic. farmer wouldn’t need to go
mallee-wards.
AN IDEAL HUSBAND.
“ Didn't she marry a rich old man?”
“ Yes; but such a very old man, and such a
lovely cough! ”
Maoriland has been
making another attempt at
passing a Usury Bill,
though it seems to have
little chance this session.
The chief provisions are
that, on secured loans of
not over £2, interest must
not exceed 15 per cent. ;
on unsecured loans, 25 per
cent. Between £2 and £lO
the maximum is 12 per
cent, if secured; and 20
per cent, if unsecured.
Over £lO the figures are
10 and 15 per cent, respec-
tively. The usurers are in
strong revolt, but the mea-
sure, or one very like it, is
dead sure to pass before
long.

Adelaide manufacturers propose to invite
Alfred Deakin to deliver locally a counterblast to
Reid’s airy flights in the interests of so-called
Freetrade. In local politics the few Foreign-trade
men have long been but as voices crying in the
wilderness, and for years no attempt has been
made to interfere with the tariff, save for the
annual formality of the Labor-party in moving
for “a free breakfast-table.”
It is notable that almost all the original
opponents of the Federal Convention Bill in
N.S.W., from Premier Lyne down, are now in
favor of maintaining the State Governor’s salary
at a high figure. To the argument that Federa-
tion would cause a reduction in State expenditure
these politicians, in the Bill day, replied that it
would not, and, unless the country coerces them,
they will prove that they were right.
S.A. Leg. Council has latterly passed an
Early Closing Bill, a Workman’s Compensation
Bill, and a Land Values Assessment Bill, each of
which had been slaughtered year after year in the
Lethal Chamber. The Tory majority is growing
so afraid of the popular cry for a broadening of the
franchise that it is trying to placate the multitude.
Also Kingston’s presence makes a lot of differ-
ence, for “he is such a chap to worry.”
It is highly interesting to read, in a recent
Sydney Telegraph, about a great indignation
meeting of British residents at Buluwayo
(S’Africa), which unanimously resolved that the
Kaffirs should be put to forced labor—should be
enslaved, in fact, like the aboriginals in the
slavery State of Westralia. Yet, how this coun-
try did boil with grief and rage about the Boer’s
disregard for the liberty of the same Kaffir !
From Adelaide to The Bulletin :
Reid, during his first visit to Adelaide, told some local
admirers that it is not impossible that he will be sent for
to form the first Federal Cabinet, although he did not
explain how that would come about. He once declared
that the N.S.W. Premier of the day must be entrusted
with the task, but that was when he was pretty sure of
sticking to the post. Lyne, however, is believed to have
agreed to pass the request on to Barton if it comes.
The other provinces evidently don’t know Lyne.
Q. Govt, is really good to its friends.
Railway Commissioner Gray slings an altogether
irrelevant approval of private railways into his
annual report, and the Govt.—which can’t afford
to restore the retrenched salaries of female school-
teachers —generously adds £5OO to Gray’s already
fat screw. Deputy-Corn. Thallon, the depart-
ment’s real expert and active administrator —and
a non-believer in private lines —gets no increase.
Reid made this remark to a S-.A. Crown
Minister in re an aggressive Single Taxer, who
was on the railway station to welcome him to
Adelaide :
“If the lean and hungry person who met me thinks
my freetrade is any kin to his he is mistaken. His free-
trade is nothing less than downright robbery.”
As the Single Tax element was particularly
strong in its greeting of the peripatetic politician
he astutely began his Town Hall speech by pub-
licly shaking it off.

The Lyne Govt, is pushing its valuable
Municipalities Bill along in the face of many diffi-
culties. The proviso authorising the levying of
“TOBY OB NOT TO BE? THAT IS THE QUESTION.”
rates up to 2s. Bd. in the £ is one big stumbling-
block. Yet the limit ought to be 20s. in the £ —not
2s. Bd. There is no law which says that a private in-
dividual may be made to pay his debts to the ex-
tent of 2s. Bd. in the £ on the annual value of his
property, and no more ; nor that the State shall
not take from him in direct and indirect taxation
more than 2s. Bd. in the £ of his income.

Q. Minister Drake, the political curio,
who struggled for 15 years in the democratic
cause and turned it up just as sparkling success
was rushing into his arms, and who, but for his
pathetic apostacy, would now be expected to lead
the Q. democracy in the Federal campaign, i as
some pleasant work before him. As leader for
the Government in the Upper House he must in-
troduce and commend seven syndicate-railways—
which he honestly loathes ; a pastoral-lease-exten-
sion Bill—which he hitherto opposed ; and a
number of small, strong-smelling measures framed
in the interests of syndicates and the poor rela-
tions of his colleagues.
The Bill of Hughes, M.L.C. (N.S.W.),
restricting interest on loans of £lO and under to
25 per cent., and on loans above £lO to 15 per
cent, has roused a multitude of people to make
the usual remark that interest can’t be fixed or
regulated by law. Certainly Britain has regulated
pawnbrokers’ interest for many years, and is now
trying its hand at doing the same with all money-
lenders ; and the English-speaking community of
the Isle of Man has enforced a maximum interest
rate very successfully for 200 years. Also, Britain
has taken to regulating agricultural rents in Ire-
land, and Victoria has taken to regulating not
only hours but wages as well, and as all these
things have been proved possible there is nothing
fresh or revolutionary about Hughes’s idea—in
fact, it is a rather old idea than otherwise now.
In N.S.W. Leg. Council Jack Want
carried an amendment on the Early Closing
Amendment Bill that “closed” should be
“closed for purposes of business” only. So by
this unobtrusive addition to the Bill it will be
lawful to keep a shop open and lit up till all
hours, and to get a conviction it will be necessary
to prove sale. Let the man from Japan be
consistent, and bring in a Bill to cover the ex-
pense of maintaining an army of 15,000 inspectors,
as, in order to enforce the law the way Mr. Want
has made it, it will be necessary to keep one in-
spector and several witnesses posted ontside each
shop. The whole thing is merely the last kick of
a selfish and tricky landlord gang which fears
that the enforcement of a real Early Closing Act
will compel it to put in private entrances to the
dog-boxes which it lets as “ shop and dwelling.”

ral Rutledge, made the usual ass of himself in Par-

replied:—
' I have heard, much to my regret, that books or pam-
phlets, purporting to be written by the person named,
have been advertised for sale at the book depot men-
tioned, and have also been credibly informed that such

“ Tip ” : In a late issue Sydney Catholic
Press published photos, in three positions of the
“martyr” Father Yictorine, S.J., after the
Boxers had done with him. He had refused to
apostatise. The result is something as hideous as
any madman might see in a dream. Now Father
Victorine was a brave man, an accomplished
gentleman. He would be more useful alive than
dead. His death won’t convert one Chow, but it
.has filled thousands of us with a desire to treat
the Boxers as they treated him, which of course is
naughty. As Vance Thompson, the American
critic says, “This workaday world finds the
martyr very ridiculous.”

between the attacks, which recurred very often—‘some-

very ill with my long-standing trouble, and had another
medical man, and he told me that gall stones could only
be cured by operation, I which would not consent to. In

Dunedin, New Zealand, telling me of the wonderful cures
VITADATIO had effected there, and urging me to take a
