“ F.” Ibsenises some more :
In your issue of 20th Oct. “ E.,” who is, I presume,
identical with “ Player,” puts his own words into my
mouth. He answers a statement which I didn’t make.
Thus —“ ‘ F.’ sets a 2-col. task. Ibsen has made no addi-
tion to European literature. So?” “ E.” begins his
yarn with a false premise. I asked for proofs
of Ibsen’s influence on contemporary literature,
not for proofs of his having published plays.
Then “ E.” gives a long list of countries where Ibsen’s
plays are “ read and acted almost simultaneously.” He
alludes to a number of German dramatists as professed
disciples of Ibsen. To all of which I say “ Eats 1 ” Where
does “ E.” get his information concerning Ibsen in Hun-
gary, Poland, Italy, Russia, &c. ? Is he writing of things
which he actually knows, or is he repeating the assertions
of the little Ibsen school in London, of which that very,
very awful person, Bernard Shaw, is a leading spokesman ?
Where is the evidence that Sudermann, Hartlebin, Haupt-
mann, &e., are confessed disciples of Ibsen? I ask for
information. I kneel at the feet of this mysterious
“ E.,” who knows everything about all countries
(especially Poland, Hungary and Russia), and I ask him —
Hid he get his knowledge at second-hand from an
Ibsenolator in London ? But, when he comes to talk of
simple Jones, in England, and of Pinero and “ The Money-
Spinner,” I began to feel my feet. “ E.” tells us that
Jones admits the influence of Ibsen in “ Case of Rebellious
Susan,” “ The Masqueraders,” &c. Where does he admit
it? Australia has sampled the two plays named, and it
saw’ nothing Ibsenic in them. “ Rebellious Susan,” who
appeared about 10 years later than Jones's “ Saints and
Sinners,” is an up-to-date matron in London’s gilt-
edged society, who behaves herself quite sanely.
The heroine in “The Masqueraders” is a glorified
barmaid, afflicted with moral scruples. Ibsen’s women,
I fancy, are destitute of scruples and sanity. Then
“E.” tells you that the “Money-Spinner” is the
“ average angelic heroine,” whose one little slip enhances
her sentimental virtues. Has “E.” ever seen Mrs. Kendal
play “ The Money-Spinner ? ” The girl was a decoy and
car l-sharper for her father, who kept a gambling-hell.
She mrrried a man of the kind likely to win her maiden
heait under such circumstances — i.e., the sort of man
who embezzles easily—and she makes a plucky, affec-
tionate wife to him. She isn’t in the least angelic. She
talks with her mouth full of breakfast. So much for
“ E.’s ” assertions, or some of them.
* * * *
Now, I will make a few assertions. Ibsen in England
was at first accepted as more of a crank than a humbug
by the Intelligent Many. He is now regarded as more of
a humbug than a crank. For the publicity there given
to his plays he is mfflnly indebted to William Archer
and Bernard Shaw. Archer is regarded as a sincere
writer with an Ibsen maggot in his brain ; Ber-
nard Shaw ranks as the insincere essayist and un-
acted playwright who outshines Shakespeare in his own
decadent imagination. Archer and Shaw may be said to
“ run ” Ibsen in London, but they can’t run him far.
Be ng clever literary men, they have by constant idolatry
of their little Norwegian god naturally obtained a follow-
ing among literary men of the kind who affect to per-
ceive genius where others see it not. The “ Ibsen game ”
was given clean away, as I may say, by a pretentious
journalist in Adelaide Ceitic. “ Ibsen does not write for
the multitude ; he writes for the chosen few, the brightest
minds, and makes absolutely no concession to intellects
less alert," &c., &c. Here you have the appeal to all weak-
minded persons’ ambitions of a reputation for “ higher
intellect.” If you idolise Ibsen you can take your place
among the Chosen Few. Heaven only can say who chose
the original Chosen, but you get there all the same if you
believe in Ibsen.
* • • •
The failure of Ibsen’s plays is undeniable. They have
derived extraordinary advertisement in the past from the
contemptuous astonishment of many critics, and the con-
troversy which William Archer and his clique have labored
so hard to maintain. They commend themselves to
theatrical managers by the fact that they can be cheaply
produced, and contain, in nearly every case (I believe) a
big “star” character for the leading lady. They are
audaciously and notoriously “ improper.” And yet, with
all these commercial points in their favor, no manager in
London breathes blessings on Ibsen’s name. The Intelli-
gent Many can find no food for their intellect in “A Doll’s
House,” '“Ghosts,” “ Hedda Gabler,” “ Enemy of the
People,” &c. Ibsen has been given many chances in Lon-
don. The indefatigable Archer was directly or indirectly
responsible for the production of two or three of the
plays, it is said. Had the Ibsen “ push ” been numerous
or influential they must have achieved a considerable
success for the later productions. They have achieved
only disappointment for themselves, but they cant
afford to abandon their craze. That would mean forfeit-
ing their position as leaders of the Ohosen Few.
* * * *
“ E.” tells you to examine the British drama for 10 years
before and after the raising of the Ibsen cry in London and
note the change in the character of the works. I ask you
to read the social history of Britain in the novels
and newspapers of the last 30 years and observe the
development of the New Woman —the lady doctor, the
Woman Suffragist, the “Girl of the Period.” The
heroines of the plays by Jones and Pinero which “ E.
instances as being Ibsenish are merely New Women, of
pure American descent, I take it. America was the birth-
place of the Emancipated Female—not Norway. Let
Ibsen get all due credit, however, for having been early
in the field with his “Nbra, or a Doll’s House,” published
in 1851. “ E.” tells you that the story of Nora dates
from 1879. These are gambling times when one loses
money by wagering money on horse races. If “ E.”
wants to lose some money let him accept my wager of £5
that “ Nora” was published 20 years previous to 1879.
* * * *
Ibsen started life as a stage-manager. He was a pro-
fessional showman for 10 years, from 1852 to 1862. He
knows how to build a play. The entrances and exits of
the characters are in their right places. He understands
the art of fixing the attention of his audience upon the
point he wants to emphasise. And to his early experi-
ence as a theatrical craftsman he is indebted for most of
his success in imposing upon the Ohosen Few. The
literary “genius” in his plays is undiscoverable by the
Intelligent Many, but they can see through his method of
play-making. He mixes simple human material skilfully,
as in “Iledda Gabler”—a young bride, doting husband,
dipsomaniacal former lover of the bride, another married
woman devoted to the dipsomaniac, and a would-be
seducer of the bride —and he peppers the pie with ceru-
lean preposterousness and “ hidden meanings,” and
leaves the Ohosen Few to make what they can out of it.
The voice of the Intelligent Many has spoken. [No more
Ibsen.— Ed. B.]

“ F.” : The weakness of Marshall Hall is
shown in his oft-expressed delight in “shocking
the parsons.” No artist should descend to the
folly of caring a rap whether he shocks parsons or
anybody else by his assertion of the truth that is
ill him.
Wj-L. Rees, ex-M.H.R. (M.L.), is one of
the British Israel Identification cranks, and now
goes lecturing to prove that we are the Lost Ten
Tribes “ and enjoy all the temporal blessings pre-
dicted of the ten-tribed kingdom.” And when
this scribe goes home late at night, and falls at
every corner over gaunt figures eating crusts out
of the dirt-boxes—well, if we are a special race
what box do other and less richly-blessed nations
get their crusts out of ?
Pity that Lord Hopetoun, when enter-
tained by numerous gilded Australian absentees
in London, did not open his mouth in the same
strain in which he farewelled his own tenants.
He apologised to them for leaving for Australia,
holding that absenteeism is a curse, and putting
the blame on the Empire’s needs. These were
mere words, of course, but even as such they
would have badly knocked many fat Australians
who actually live in England on Australian toil,
and pay not even an absentee tax for their indul-
gence in what Hopetoun calls a curse.
The Rich Landlords of Vic. Leg. Council
often have to fork out at election time, the
propertied voters being more keen for stimulants
to patriotism than the “ ’orny-’anded.” D. Harris’
first campaign (his only contested one) cost him
£l3OO. Some years ago J, Y. M‘Donald expended
£lOOO before he learned that his return would be
unopposed ; and T. D. Wanliss, Ballarat jour-
nalist (elected on another occasion), was unsuc-
cessful against H. Gore, though his sinews of war
took the form of an outlay of £3lOO. Yet Vic-
toria prides herself on the purity of her elections !
The journalist, needless to say, did not make his
coin out of the Press ; he won it by mining and
jarrah-timber speculations.

A Lady’s Evidence.
DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT PILLS CURED BILIOUS-
NESS AND HEADACHES.
“Havingbeen a sufferer from biliousness and
headaches, and having tried different medicines,
also doctor’s powders for the head, but getting no

„* * * *
Gun : Defeat of Lancaster in Melbourne
uup should choke people off the game altogether if any-
thing can. His win in Melbourne Stakes (Saturday) made
appear an absolute “ cert.,” and had a paralysing
effect upon the betting. In Algerian’s and Ruenalf’s
years victory in Melbourne Stakes looked a good line
to go on, but each of those nags finished out of a place.
Lancaster s chance, however, seemed a heap better. It’s
interesting to note that not one of the placed horses
figured in _ Melbourne Stakes. Worth mentioning, too,
ln Helb. Cup—Alix—is the unluckiest horse
of the season, as he filled a similar position in Caufield
Cup, and was second to War God in Hotham Handicap,
Trainer (Scobie) of Clean Sweep and Malster has had a
good time. Malster annexed both A.J.C. and N.R.O.
Derbies.
* • * *
Malster’s V.R.C. Derby was the smallest since
Seagull’s in ’66, when same number (3) started. The time
(2.48) was slower than any since Nordenfeldt’s success 15
years ago. Malster’s present owner gave 810 gs. for him,
since which time the Bill of Portland—Barley colt has won
V.R.C. Hopeful Stakes, Ascot Vale Stakes, and A.J.C. and
V.R.C. Derbies, and run second in Champagne and Easter
Stakes at Randwick, and third in Caulfield Guineas. Mal-
ster was bred by the late Mr. “ Mat ” O’Shanassy.
The race for this year’s Derby would have
looked more satisfactory if it hadn’t been run in strict
accordance with the betting. Barbarossa was expected
to make an attempt to get away with a strong lead
whilst the two favorites kept close company. But the
three ran leisurely in Indian file until Kinglike overtook
Barbarossa, and then the odds-on favorite overtook King-
like, and Ikey Mo must have dropped a good bit on the
transaction. Malster was backed with great confidence
by some people.
* * • •
Ingliston is an interesting object-lesson in Aus-
tralian horse-racing. Ingliston was backed down to 10 to
1 to win Caulfield Cup, though up to then the excellence
of the horse had to be taken on trust. His public per-
formances had not been brilliant. Well, Ingliston (7.4)
won Caulfield Cup, beating Alix, Dirk Hammerhand and
others with ease. On Saturday the distinguished Inglis-
ton made his first reappearance after his victory. The
race, the Hotham Stakes, was the same distance as Caul-
field Cup. He met Alix on only 91b. worse terms than
before, m fact he was well handicapped. Alix, backed at
8 to 1, finished a good second to War God, with Dirk
Hammerhand third, whilst Ingliston, the hero of a fort-
night ago, went out at 12 to 1 and came in last.
* • • »
N.S. Wales scored three wins on opening day
(Sat.) of V.R.C. Spring Meet. Successful nags were Ibex
(Maribyrnong Plate), War God (Hotham Handicap), and
Rock Gun (Encourage Stakes). Mr. W. H. Mate owns
first-named, which was bred at Kirkham and is one of the
cheapest nags ever sold. At the dispersal of Kirkham
stud, Ibex (then a few weeks old) was put up with his dam
Angora and the two went for 40gs. Ibex is by Gossoon
(imp.) and his dam is by Maribyrnong from The
Alpaca by Kingston. Throughout past ten years the
only N.S.W. “ prads ” to gather in Maribyrnong Plate
laurels were Arihi and Lady Mostyn. Previously Acme,
lolanthe, Narina, Segenhoe, Palmyra, Nellie, Vulcan, and
Habena won. Maribyrnong Plate has never been taken
more easily than this year, and yet the time put up (1.3)
is only £sec. slower than previous '“best” shared in by
N.S.W. nags—lolanthe (’B3) and Arihi (’94). Stable-boy
Godby had the mount. Writer doesn’t know a youngster
who gives greater promise than Ibex in every way.
Strangely enough, Rock Gun was bought dirt cheap by
present owner (Mark Thompson), who gave 50gs. for him
last yearling sales.
• • •
Punters at Warwick Parm (N.S.W.), Saturday,
fell over each other to lay 5 to 2 on Tripoli for Two-Year
Old Stakes, but Numidia (same price against) beat them.
Winner is by Medallion from Algerine—a full sister to Ike
Earnshaw’s Barbarossa, and was bought by H. Dargin at
last Randwick yearling sales for 135 guineas. The win
proved a big reversal of form. At Moorefield, a week
before, Tripoli won, and Numidia failed then to get a
“ drum.”
• • * •
Collusion 'tween Sydney betting-shops and
horse-owners waxes stronger every week. Nags are even
brought up to the course now and left in as long as rules
will stand it. Still stewards and others supposed to take
note remain blind. One day lately, not long ago, mokes
owned by one man who has long been at the game went
up by train to the course, and bets were made that he’d
start one or both that time sure, but he didn’t send
either, and crowed about it on Monday. The other
Saturday another nag (a red-hot favorite in town) was
scratched on the course—and so the thing goes on week
in and week out. Why don’t the police shut up these
shops ? They’ve got the power. They did it in Mel-
bourne effectually and in one swoop. A year or more
ago Sydney stipendiaries threatened that the next batch
of tote-men brought up would go to gaol without the
option. That is the remedy, but it isn’t taken ; fining
goes on just the same. Mo ’ll come up smiling as often
as they like with fines, but one fair dose of quod would
choke him right off.
* * * *
“Old ’Un” : Have seen no mention anywhere
of the recent death, through consumption, at Goodooga,
of that once-well-known N.S.W. contractor and promi-
nent sport, Mr. “Tom” Potts. Mr Potts (associated
with Messrs. “ Teddy ” Keys and O’Donnell) owned some
crack nags once. Few liked a good bout with the gloves
better ; he backed “Gentleman’’GeorgeDawson in some
of his Australian mills, and swore by Peter Jackson.
Writer was present at Melb. A.C. when Mr. Potts (a year
or more before the memorable Jaekson-Slavin contest)
wagered £5OO even with the late “ Jack ” Dougharty that
if ever the white and black Australians met, Jackson
would win. Mr. Potts was also mixed up in N.S.W. pro-
fessional rowing a lot, particularly as ex-world’s champion
Jim Stanburv’s friend.
*" * * *
Says the Australasian man:—“Training
horses in public is now resorted to by nearly all our
trainers, and it leads to strange reversals of form. We
have at least 10 trainers now for every one in the palmy
days of John Tait, Etty de Mestre, and James Wilson, but
very few of them seem to have much confidence in their
own judgment as to when a horse is fit. Some, indeed, do
not seem capable of training a horse to run on a certain
day as old-time trainers had to do. The horse must get
his condition by racing, and racing-clubs are asked to
frame their programmes so that horses may train them-
selves in public without the risk of incurring penalties for
big races. Twenty-five years ago horses went into re-
tirement after the Sydney Oup meeting, and when they
were seen again in Aug. or Sept, they were generally fit
to race. When Savanaka started for Melb. Cup he had
not seen a racecourse for 12 months, yet he was fit to run
for a man’s life,” &c., &c. This is taking a very charitable
view of the disreputable policy which is now the general
rule. Owners are at least as much to blame as trainers,
for the practice of starting horses in races which they are
not expected; or not intended to win. It is unsports-
manlike if it is not absolutely dishonest. Horses are run
“out of their distance” with the result that outside
punters get misled as to their real capabilities for stay-
ing, or sprinting. First-class nags, with big records, are
naturally supposed to be worth backing whenever they
run, and so the sovereigns of the public give them a
market price that looks, on paper, like a guarantee of
stable support. When these popular fancies put up in-
different performances it is assumed that they have more
or less disappointed the stable, whereas the owner, who
didn’t have sixpence on his badly-beaten “ crack,” tells his
few friends, in confidence, that the gallop, under the
circumstances, was quite satisfactory, and they had better
have a bit on next month without asking any questions
or letting the truth be known. In short the “ exercise
gallop ” is intended in many cases to choke off the public
from attempting to recover the money they have lost by
backing their fancy when it wasn’t fit.

In re Tatt’s sweep, Tuesday 30th Oct. was a
record day at Melb. G.P.O. Victorian postal notes
were forwarded by Tatt. from Hobart through the
B.N.S.W. numbering 12,343, a face value of £5,497 Bs. lOd.
—from one province for one day only ! The parcels had
to be taken from the bank to the P.O. in a cab.
* * * •
S.A. and Victoria meet this Saturday on Ade-
laide Oval in the season’s first inter-State cricket-match.
Vic. puts out her strongest team, and as Worrall and
M'Leod are playing, it is even stronger than last year.
S. A. takes the field minus Darling (who writes that he
will not handle a bat until the new year at least) and
Lyons. Probably M‘Kibbin will be included in the S.A.
eleven, which had not been finally selected at time of
writing. S.A. will most likely experiment with several
young batsmen this season, but her chief weakness is in
the bowling. “Dinny” Reedman, nearly a veteran, is
the popular tip for captain of the home eleven, but G.
Giffen, who is batting in his old form, will once again be
S.A.’s mainstay.
* * * *
Lyons will not play against Victoria this season
—the first time for over 10 years. “ Carbine ” was always
a thorn in the side of Vic., for his chief scores have been
compiled against the sometimes moderate bowling of that
State. He has made numerous centuries against Trumble
and Co., and generally for first wicket. He and Darling
still hold the first-wicket record in inter-state matches
—lB4, compiled in ’97. Lyons is retiring owing to varicose
veins, which make even club cricket painful for him.
Unlucky that S.A. should lose two of her most famous
batsmen in the same season. Giffen’s record is certainly
unique, for he played in the first match against Vic. in
'BO, and of the 22 men who played in that match he is the
only one now in big cricket.
• * » •
Rounds in all grades of Sydney local cricket
concluded Saturday. In first division Paddington won
comfortably from Redfern, Sydney accounted for Bur-
wood, Central Cumberland went under to Waverley,
Leichhardt-Balmain proved soft for N. Sydney. In other
grades S. Sydney put up 560 for eight wickets against St.
George. Hundreds were notched by Ranshaw and
Stubbin ; Ridge compiled 81. N. Sydney (2nd) made
320 for six wickets against Manly, Frank Punch (one of
the old rowing and sporting family) contributing 182 not
out. Punch’s previous innings (against Waverley) was
142, and just prior to that he ran into the eighties.
Frank pire complains bitterly that he can’t get the young
’uns to take to the boat—it’s all cricket and football with
them. Last year’s premiers in first grade (Central Cum-
berland) are at the bottom of the list so far.
• * * *
Sydney University-Petersham match in the
third-grade cricket competition last. Saturday produced
an extraordinary finish. Petersham on previous Saturday
had scored 271. On second day of match they dismissed
their opponents by about 5 p.m. for 236, thus winning on
first innings. The afternoon had turned bleak and cold,
and the Uni. skipper suggested that as there was but an
hour left for play the teams should go comfortably home,
as nothing could be gained by playing on. Petersham,
however, objected. They had been fielding all day, and
insisted on a strike. ’Varsity took the field, in rather a
bad frame of mind, but to everyone’s amazement dis-
missed all Petersham for 31. Thus they had 67 runs to
get to win and though there was only 25min. left for play
they managed by brisk batting to make them in the time.
• * * *
That light of other days, Alick Bannerman, at
mid-off in the Paddington-Redfern match, was not the
brilliant mid-off of old. Fat has the old bantam under
the whip.
* * * *
Hearne, Young, and Richardson all cut a poor
figure in the English bowling averages this year, each of
them having an average of over 20. They are beaten by
Mason and Jessop, who stand out as the best all-round
men of the year.
* » » *
Albert Trott is a breaker of records. Last year
he made one by making 1000 runs and taking 200 wickets
in the season, and this year he has repeated the feat. It
has not been mentioned, however, that in 4J months he
played 62 innings, and had close on 5000 runs scored from
his bowling—both probably records.
* * * •
Project for bringing an all-Canadian lacrosse
team to Australia is ripening and looks well. Conditions
proposed by Sydney conference have found favor with
the Canadians, who are now moving in the matter of se-
curing the wherewithal. Should they come a North
American Indian will accompany them. Sydney and
Victoria are only waiting for S.A. to approve the confer-
ence resolutions, when a definite offer will be made, and
date of visit fixed.
* * • «
The big M.L. distance-swimmer Hoey got back
to Sydney this week after doing Paris and Britain. Euro-
pean conditions were against him, so he swam not there
and returns to Maoriland suffering from severe indiges-
tion and heart-palpitation—a family trouble. Hoey says
he’ll quit swimming and stick to fruit-growing henceforth.
* • • •
Late American news tells that Australian crack
swimmer Percy Gavill was beaten by U.S. top-notcher
M‘Cusker. They were to swim best of three distances—
quarter-mile, five miles and mile M'Cusker won first,
and Cavill became exhausted in next, having to be taken
out of the water. Cavill must indeed be “off” for
M’Cusker to prevail at a quarter-mile as (both fit. and
well) latter wouldn’t be in the same street with Cavill.
Latter went away with little or no money, has had few
(if any) shows since of making much, and so may have
been poorly trained.
*.* » ’ *
Prior to last mail leaving Eng., prof. “Joey”
Nuttall, world’s champion swimmer, challenged all
comers. Gage was taken up, through the papers, by an
“unknown,” who stipulated as distances 100, 220, and
440yds. Nuttall wanted them otherwise, and negotia-
tions collapsed. Unknown explained that he was an
amateur and didn’t care to forfeit his status without big
inducement. All English fore-front non-pros, publicly
denied connection with the matter, and as Australian
Freddy Lane remained silent, scribes singled him out as
the ambitious one. Perhaps he was—the distances
named are those he likes best.
* * * *
Details of British furlong swimming supremacy
in Sept, are to hand. Race was at Birmingham, and
proved the closest thing ever seen between the world’s
100yds. record-holder (Derbyshire) and Australian F.
Lane, now on his way to Sydney. The others weren’t in
it. At 150yds. Lane was 2yds. to the good, but the Eng-
lishman gradually overhauled him afterwards, and they
dead-heated in new world’s-record time of 2.34 4-5. Pre-
vious best (2.381-5) stood in Lane’s name, and was done
when he won same event last year. There was no swim-
off, hence title is shared in by both.
Australian sculler Towns, after beaming Wray,
threw the gage down to the world, particularly Jake
Gaudaur, but the wily Canuck won’t be drawn from his
retreat at Rat Portage. Ned Hanlan’s nephew, Ed.
Durnan, looked like filling the breach when latest mail
left. A year or two ago Durnan was with the Yankee
pot-hunting section, but now occupies a high place among
the pros. Proposal is for a £2OO aside match, over a
three-mile course (with a turn), in Toronto Bay (Canada).
Towns may not take to the turn unless he thinks Durnan
a bit soft.
• • * •
Crack Australian sprinter Stan. Rowley, just
home from British championships and Paris Olympian
Games, has a woeful tale to tell of English A.A.A.’s lack
of hospitality. Nobody came near him for days though
they knew he was there, and in response to his request
for a ticket of admission to the sports, they billed him for
10s. entrance-fees. Another time he had to pay Is. at the
gate in face of fact that he was a competitor. Yankee
reps, had a heap the best of the deal with their army of
trainers and colored “ rubbers,” all paid by American
athletic bodies. They weren’t even asked to think for
themselves ; trainers did all that and instructed the nigs,
how and when to groom their men. Only difference
between a Yankee amateur and a pro. (as we understand
him) is that former doesn’t actually compete for money ;
but he gives himself up wholly and solely to training and
has every expense paid. Rowley went to Paris with the
English reps., but the A.A.A. sent not a single trainer
over with them, while the Americans had their dozens of
attendants. “ What chance had the Britishers or myself
under those conditions ? ” asks Rowley.
m * * *
Match for scratch-pulling supremacy of N.S. W.,
at Sydney Gaiety Athletic Hall Monday night, was a soft
thing for Sydneyite Larry Ryan, who had too much up
his sleeve for Victorian Jim Olancy. Conditions—best
two in three pulls, hickory stick 2ft. long and ljin dia.
Word “ Go ” had barely been given for first tug when
Clancy (apparently taken by surprise) was brought up
standing. Next pull only lasted a second or so longer
and same thing occurred. Ryan thus won the match.
’Twas a huge surprise for Clancy’s side, who thought
their man had a “ moral,” but he simply wasn’t in it a
moment. Clancy scales about 18st. and has an immense
upper structure, but dwindles away in the under-pins,
which is where the leverage comes from. Ryan weighs
somewhere about 17st., is proportionately built and owns
good, strong legs ; besides, he knows more about the
tricks of the trade than t’other man, who would perhaps
have shaped better had his own slow, steady, straining
method been adopted by the opposition. Writer never
saw a man look more sheepish than did Clancy after that
first pull. Ryan seeks a “ cut ”at anyone in Australia
for money.
* * * •
Yic. cyclist Rowlands, reported as having died
at Rutherglen, promised well, and looked a genuine
coming champion. His second to the dusky Gordon, in
Sydney in a scratch race, with Gordon all out, as well as
the fact that he won many handicaps from short marks,
indicated big possibilities. Rowlands was only 21, and is
said to have ruptured a cranial blood-vessel.
“Old Timer”: Not satisfied with the fiasco
attendant upon the resurrection of black Pluto recently,
Sydney bruising promoters exhumed another dead ’un on
Saturday night, when Nipper Peakes squared up to little-
better-than-novice Bob Turner. Nipper was decently
interred a dozen years ago and should have been allowed
to rest there. Perhaps ’twas thought that a good dead
’un was anyhow better than the duffing live ’uns of the
present time. And so it almost proved, for as long as
Peakes’s condition stood to him his coolness and skill
were more than a match for t’other who, (strong enough
and game enough) hustled and banged like one possessed
but rarely landed and then without much steam. Nipper
moved round as little as possible and only sent a punch
along when the opening was clear. He knew a heap too
much about the game for Turner and so was enabled to
stand up till the tenth round ; then, serving out a very
sultry 3 min. he had Turner going but couldn’t finish
him. After this Peakes was cold pie for the Sydneyite.
AVeakening to nothing in l°ch round, Nipper caved in.
It’ll be an out-and-out crime if he isn’t allowed to stay
buried for good now.
* * * »
Latest from Yankeedom is that the once-mar-
vellous pug.-freak Griffo is settled beyond recovery—he’s
no sooner out of gaol than he’s in again. Chicago police
were talking about sending him out here when the mail
left.
Erstwhile champion light-weight pug. of Aus-
tralia, “ Gentleman ” George Dawson (now flourishing in
Chicago), has been interviewed re Fitzsimmons, whom
he seconded and advised in his two latest battles
with Ruhlin and Sharkey. “ Fitz. is an old man, and he
is a cripple,” said Dawson. “ His hands are never right
and his arms are always out of order. He can’t straighten
his right arm now, but when the time comes for a fight
he’s always ready—age makes no difference to him. I
believe he’ll always be the greatest fighter we have seen.
No matter how old he is, if he can be gotten into condi-
tion and his arms don’t go back on him altogether, he’ll
always be a champion. In Australia, Fitz. fought differ-
ently—he was much more clever then than now. He
seems to have discarded cleverness for his present peculiar
methods ; then he was one of the most skilful boxers I
ever saw.”
* * * *
Few cyclists have any conception of the vast
number of Dunlop tyres annually turned out by the
Dunlop Tyre Co. of Australasia, Ltd. Last year, some
75,000 tyres were sold throughout the Australasian States
—proof conclusive that these tyres are popular with
Australian cyclists ;in fact,"it may safely be asserted that
nine-tenths of our cyclists ride these tyres.*
The mendacious small female with her tale of out-
rage turned up again the other day at Maclean,
N.fcJ.W. A girl, aged 13 years 8 months, told a
lodger in her father’s house that the father had
outraged her. Whereupon the lodger laid infor-
mation with the police, and the father was
arrested. Then the young lady preferred a similar
charge against the lodger. She made numerous
contradictory statements, and perjured herself
quite unconcernedly at the Police-court inquiry,
but at the end was firm in her assertion that her
father had attempted, and the lodger had com-
mitted, the offence. The girl’s mother, who was
absent at the time of the arrests, deposed that the
girl had told her the charges were all lies, but she
had made them because the police insisted on
asking her questions. The father was discharged,
and the lodger committed for trial, but the Crown
Law Officer on circuit declined to proceed with
the case, and bound accused over to appear when
called upon. The same old Australian small girl !

The Taree (N.S. W.)people were virtuously
indignant “that Jimmy Governor is kept in a
small wooden cell, badly ventilated, and situated
on open ground, at Wingliam, when there is a sub-
stantial brick gaol at Taree.” Cruel thus to rob
the Taree people of their solitary chance of
achieving distinction !
“ W.” writes :
Your correspondent (Oct. 27) was inaccurate in stating
that no letters or speeches criticising the Snow tram-
grab have been admitted into the Adelaide dailies.
“ The Advertiser ” has printed columns of Proud’s in-
vectives, and has never yet refused insertion to anything
he has written and sent to the paper on the subject.
Also the meetings in favour of nationalisation, held
under his auspices, have been well reported.
Editor Bulletin. —Kindly allow me, as
one of the fraternity, to draw public attention to
the heart-breaking hours worked by Sydney deli-
very-carters. It is quite common to see carters
going home at 10 p.m. and later ; and now, coming
on Christmas, the hours will often run to mid-
night. Even as it is, our time averages 90 hours
per week! —so surely the benefits of the Act
should to some degree be extended to us. We
are not exacting, as we only ask for a maximum
of GO hours per week, with one day off in the
month. — Winkers.
Melb. City Bench fined a young woman
£2, or 14 days, lately for kissing a kharki-clad
warrior without his permission. In justice to the
damsel it should be stated that she was slightly
sober at the time.
“ Viti ” writes from Fiji :
Dear B. —The Suva people are much exercised over the
erection of a plague camp of grass huts on the swamp
adjoining their four-pub. township, and have appealed
over the matter to Joe of the eyeglass. (Plague has,
luckily, not got ashore here as yet, but it is sure to find a
landing here some day, while as for lepers, you can have
a fresh one every day.) South Sea trippers should be
reminded that a 20min. walk in the right (wrong ?) direc-
tion from Suva wharf will land them at the plague or
leper camps, prison, graveyard, or lunatic asylum. This
arid other matters, such as stopping the 'subsidy of £l5OO
to the Canadian mail-boats—against the wishes of the
residents—in consequence of which said boats ceased to
call at Fiji, and granting £lOOO out of our £BO,OOO revenue
to the big drum and banner party without consulting
anybody, has made the Crown colonists kick. Some
would like to bring Seddon in and send Sir George else-
where—as witness the latest move for federating with
Maoriland, and the sending of our respectful sympathies
to the Queen re Edinburgh’s demise through Right Hon.
Richard of Wellington—ignoring our local Ex. altogether.
Only two members of N.S.W. Parlia-
mentary Labor-party, of whom W. J. Ferguson
was the more conspicuous, worked for the Com-
monwealth Bill, even at the second referendum ;
and, perversely enough, his championship of it has
now been used against him at his first Federal can-
didature ! The nominee of the Barrier Labor-
unionists will almost certainly be returned unop-
posed for the Broken Hill seat; and for that
nomination there were two candidates—Ferguson,
keen-witted and keen-tongued ; Josiah T 1 omas,
dull and prosy. Though, at the second referen-
dum, the Laborites were nominally free to go as
they pleased, Ferguson has never been foi’given
for going frankly against the majority of his
party ; and so the brainy man with some indi-
viduality goes under before a color-less automa-
ton, to whom the nomination has been given.
Hobart Mercury actually takes the
trouble to print letters from local Jingoes hoping
that over the re-election of Campbell-Bannerman
(he opposed the war) to the Commons, “ there will
be throughout the colonies such an expression of
disapproval, reprobation, condemnation, scathing
censure as shall make the recreant voters feel the
lash of outraged public opinion,” and calling upon
the Hobart “ electors to meet in force and pass
resolutions, to be cabled ’Ome, thus letting the
glorious mother-country see,” &c., &c. All which
is perfectly lovely coming from residents of such
a frowsy little outpost of the Empire as Tasmania.
The “ outraged public opinion ” of Britain’s for-
mer most squalid convict-dumping-ground is truly
a thing to tremble at. Especially is the opinion of
Hobart, the constituency which lately returned to
Parliament Captain Miles I Campbell-Banner-
man has represented Stirling for 32 years, and its
hard-headed electors probably know their man
and their own minds a little better than do the
howling descendants of Port Arthur-land.
On Friday night last a pathetic, lonely
figure might have been seen leaving a train at
Spencer-st. station, Melb. He carried a bag and
a rug, and wore a most despondent look. It was
a former Premier of Victoria, Baronet O ‘Loghlen—
he.was Attorney-General in the fiery Berry Ministry
—just back from Port Fairy, where he had lost his
seat and.his £5O deposit. No man could be more
extinct in politics than the veteran Bart. The
papers had not even commented upon his dis-
appearance. It was not so much that he was
defeated as that he dropped out, like a cinder out
of the bottom of the grate. The poor old man
seems to have quitted politics as he quitted many
other professions. He was civil engineer, then
farmer, then merchant, then barrister, and then
politician. He established one good precedent.
He was elected for Clare in the Commons while
holding the office of Vic. Attorney-General. The
Commons declared his seat vacant, as an office of
grofit in Vic. was held to be an office under the
rown.

The most awesome of the proposals for
celebrating the Hopetoun arrival with “dignity”
is one to the effect that all N.S. W. aldermen shall
attend “ in distinguishing civic robes.”
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co. reports
that grain transactions are limited. The lower values
quoted at Mark Lane (which now turn out to have been
due to labor troubles instead of depression in the trade)
have had a lowering effect locally, and 2s. 7d. to 2s. Bd.
per bushel is the best that can' be obtained for F.A.Q.
and prime s 'es respectively. Harvest prospects as
indicated last .. _k.
Sandeman’s Australian wines are becom-
ing more and more popular every day, and anyone
visiting Sydney should call at Sandeman’s, 241
Pitt-street, and sample the wines and look over
the cellars. The amount of wine that these cellars
contain, and the size of the cellars themselves, is
a revelation to a stranger. Even the thirstiest
veteran feels despondent when he is faced by the
fact that he would have to drink hard every day
for a million years or so to make any perceptible
impression on the stock.*

At Poverty Point.
' J. F. Runciman, the Saturday Review musical critic—
Marshall Hall’s great friend—has been seeing Lalla
Miranda in Brussels. He gives a poor account of her per-
formance as Ophelia in Ambrose Thomas’ Hamlet.
Thus : “ Alas ! Miranda went mad the other night, and,
as prima donnas always do when they go mad on the
stage, she duly sang rapid scales and arpeggios calcu-
lated to drive mad every one in the theatre; and she
could not interest the audience for a moment. ‘ Lucia ’
would give her a better chance : in truth * Hamlet ’ gave
her no chance at all. I love not artists of the Miranda
type ; but I feel a certain human pity for them when they
perspire over stuff like ‘ Hamlet ’ and at the end find the
audience indifferent.”
Owing to a break-down of the train from Timaru t’other
week Donald Macdonald reached Ashburton (M.L.) a
couple of hours late. He had made up his mind to miss
his Boer yarn at that peaceful spot and go straight on to
Christchurch, but Ashburton was not to be robbed of its
dissipation. The inhabitants turned out to meet their
brither Scot at the railway station. They implored him
to carry out his contract for the sake of the Empire, and
because all the seating space in the local hall had been
sold. Also, they hinted delicately that they would hang on
to him if he tried to escape. So the weary Mac. clothed
himself in kharki and took the platform at 10 p.m., finish-
ing his lecture on the right side of the next day. The
end of all this will be a loss to Australian journalism, pro-
bably. Macdonald, with eight or ten thousand pounds
in hand, will turn squattah and become a leading authority
on sheep and things.
“ Kalgoorlie ” : Brough appears, during his goldfields’
tour, to have been under the impression that prices of
admission could be raised ad lib. in this benighted dis-
trict. For instance, he advertised a season of 13 nights
at 7s. 6d. reserved stalls, and ss. any other portion of the
house. The 7s. 6d. was right enough for a comfortable
seat and a good view of a first-class co. in a first-class
play ; but the toilers, who, after all, are the chief support
of any show, emphatically objected to paying ss. for a
limited view from behind a sea of straw hats and ostrich
feathers. Consequently “ Niobe,” with which the co.
opened, fell flat, owing to the emptiness of the seats from
which the applause principally comes. After two nights,
the prices were reduced to 7s. 6d., 65., 45., and 35., and
matters improved wonderfully. But Brough vows, I hear,
that “ he will never come here any more.”
Lilian Tree is to make an early reappearance—pro-
bably in Brisbane
Daisy Harcourt, Rickards’ latest importation, is doing
coster-“ lydy ” songs at Adelaide Bijou. Miss ’Arcourt
is a robust golden-haired artiste, who comes on with red,
white, and blue stockings and ( inter alia) a fringe,
and promises to give, in the immortal words of Alfred
Bennett, “ great pleasure to all ” And, to give her due
credit, she does nothing rude.
Young Clive O’Hara, son of the Melb. medico, is doing
the illusionist business as “ the Great Clive ” with Ric-
kards’ show in Adelaide. He performs most of “ Dante’s ”
well-known tricks very well indeed, but his stage talk
and business badly need livening up, With both Dante
and Carl Hertz it was in no small degree the “ business ”
which made the show.
The latest war-lecturer is Uitlander Samson, of Melb.
Argus, who opened for two consecutive nights at Melb.
Athenaeum last Tuesday and threatens to repeat the
operation round the suburbs, if not restrained. Samson
gives a brief, mild, uncolored resume of the operations,
gathered from news sources ; and as he dwells lightly on
the various British repulses, and spreads himself on the
final victorious marches, he should do fairly well, if he
can persuade his lanternist to run the machine in agree-
ment with his lecture. The man with the single eye-glass
tells an interesting tale of the consternation in the office
of the great daily across the way when, after Spion Kop
had been captured and jubilant leaders thereon and notes
on the position of affairs had been set-up, at 2 a.m. a
cable came through, “ Spion Kop abandoned,” and all
was mourning and anathema, and everything had to be
Bet-up afresh.
Looks as if Williamson and Musgrove have “ interred the
tomahawk.” The latter advertised that the box-plan for
“ Oarmen ” would open next day “in order not to clash
with the booking to-day for the first night of * The Rose
of Persia.’ ”
The Corpse of N.S.W. Marine Board.
The discredited N.S.W. Marine Board, which
permitted the notorious Atacama and other simi-
lar vessels to go to sea and drown their miserable
crews, has been dead for nearly a year—super-
seded by the new Dept, of Navigation—but even
from its grave it stinks. N.S.W. Public Service
Board has just presented to Parliament its report
of an enquiry which shows that some of the most
important officers under the Marine Board—
pilots, inspectors, shipwright and engineer
surveyors—have been for years receiving open
or surreptitious “tips” from local ship-owners
and ship-agents, as a reward for special services
rendered, or possibly because the owners and
agents found it paid to keep sweet with these
officers. To quote the Public Service Board :
The perusal of the evidence produces anything but
pleasant reflections. To find that one of the most im-
portant State departments has for many years been
honeycombed with illicit practices is very disappointing
to anyone who desires, above all things, pure administra-
tion in the public service.
These “ illicit practices,” so the former heads of
the Marine Board declare, were carried on with-
out their knowledge. Yet some of the persons
inculpated defended themselves partly on the
ground that “ tipping ” was a common and recog-
nised practice. But Commander Lindeman
former Secretary to the Marine Board, says he
knew little about it; and Captain Hixson, former
President, knew no more.
He had a sort of vague recollection of Mr. Selfe en-
gineer surveyor, coming to him about the matter, and it
is very probable he said, “ Well, I don’t want to know
anything about it,” but he could not say whether he did
or whether he did not. He would not wish to be taken
as authority for the wholesale demand and receipt of fees.
He certainly would not have given a formal approval to
the practice.
On the other hand, Mr. Henry Selfe, engineer-
surveyor to the Board, now under the Navigation
Department, said that
The first fee he took into Mr. Cruickshank, who ad-
vised him to send it back ; he then saw Captain Hixson,
who, in answer to his question as to whether he should
keep it, said “ I don’t want to know anything about it.”
He kept that fee and the other two which he subsequently
received.
The Public Service Board draws special attention
to the case of Mr. Selfe,
Which seemed to show the want of a right perception of
the proprieties attaching to the important position he
holds, which stands out in such startling contrast to the
evidence given by his chief, Mr. Cruickshank. They
refer particularly to that portion of the evidence where
Mr. Selfe says, “ When I got that letter (containing a fee)
from Mr. Prosser, and saw the kindly way in which it was
worded, I considered it would be an insult to him if I did
not take the cheque. Mr. Cruickshank said, ‘ I would
not take it.’ I said, *1 cannot afford to be so beastly
virtuous as you are. I consider I have earned it, and
I shall stick to it.’ ”
With regard to the fees paid to pilots, says the
Board, “no worse result has probably accrued
than that the State has lost some fees for harbour
pilotage, which have gone into the pockets of
these officers”—and that is surely bad enough.
But, continues the Board—
It is different when the fees to the surveyor are con-
cerned. In the case of these officers any failure to pro-
perly perform their duty may lead to disaster, and loss of
life. The importance of the work entrusted to them may
be gathered from a reply made to a question during the
course of the inquiry.
Question: “It rested entirely in your hands what
should be done ; your office was not acquainted with it at
all ? ” —Mr. Selfe : “ Not at all.”
Question: “It was left in your hands whether you
called upon the (shipping) company to spend thousands
of pounds or ten pounds ? ” —Mr. Selfe : “Yes. When on
board we were entirely masters of the situation. Every-
thing was in our hands.”
Question: “ Suppose one of them (the assistant
engineer surveyors) went aboard a ship and saw that a
tube was defective in a boiler, would he have the power
to say that the tube must come out?”—Mr. W. D. Oruick-
shank : “ Yes.’
Question: “Without sending a report to you?”—Mr.
Cruickshank: “Most decidedly. They have almost
unlimited power.”
Question : “If a surveyor wished to be lenient to a
company, he might make his requisitions lighter than
another surveyor would?”—Mr. Cruickshank : “ Yes, he
could make it either way ; he could make it very hard or
very light.”
It is easy to see to what lengths the system might
lead a surveyor weak in character, or embarrassed
in finances. To such a man “tips” and the ex-
pectation of “tips” would be a temptation to let
a bad tube go, or trust a rotten boiler for one
more voyage rather than offend the shipping
companies on whom he relied for bounty.
Beyond doubt, the shipping agents got value
for their money; they would not have paid
otherwise. Question is: What value did they
get ? Even if the surveyors knew that they
did their duty without fear or favor, despite the
“tips”; their position was one that no public
servant should place himself in. And they
have got off very lightly. So many Marine
Board officers have had their palms greased that
to suspend or dismiss them would, says the Publio
Service Board, disorganise the whole Department!
Some people will incline to think that, considering
the public interests at stake, the Department
should have been re-organised. But the Public
Service Board makes a scapegoat of one engineer-
surveyor who went a little further than the others,
and asked for his tip. And he only partially loses
salary and promotion. The Bulletin draws
special attention to the conduct of the Sydney
Morning Herald in failing to print the report
which other Sydney daily papers published more
or less fully. The Herald printed only a very
short and imperfect summary, mentioning no
names; and thus doing injustice to Mr. W. D.
Cruickshank and Capts. Newton and Sangster,
who are specially mentioned as “ exceptions from
the list of those who have compromised themselves
by unauthorised practices. “These gentlemen
had a sufficiently proper sense of the obligations
attaching to the important positions they occupied
not to lower themselves by accepting gratuities
which, to use Mr. Cruickshank’s expressive
phrase, “shut a man’s mouth up.” The Herald’s
Srocedure, by whatever extraordinary reasons in-
uenced, was, in The Bulletin’s opinion, un-
worthy of a public journal with the Herald’s
claims to be honestly conducted in the public in-
terest.
Constable Smith, tried at Capetown for
“ drilling holes ” through a nigger to accelerate
his movements in the service of the Empire, has,
it is cabled, been acquitted, on the ground that he
only obeyed the order of his officer, Capt. Cox, of
the N.S.W. Lanceahs. Query : If a murder really
was committed, as the authorities allege, who is
going to be docked for it now ?

The reunions of the missus and the boss
continue in the West. In ’96 there were 42‘28
females to every 100 males in Westralia; for Sept,
of this year the estimate was 5370, and the im-
provement goes on steadily month by month.
It is probable that in future M.L. Govt,
will either altogether prohibit Chinese from keep-
ing laundries, or compel them to observe the same
hours as women workers.
A man in Bowenfels district (N.S.W.)
was mainly instrumental in causing land to be
set apart for a local cemetery, measured it off,
saw about the fencing, &c., and then was the
first person to be interred therein.
Writes a Queensland contingenter:—“ We
used to laugh, when at home, at the Boer girls
over here, but I can tell you there are as nice girls
among them as any I ever saw in Queensland.
You oan tell some of the young ladies this from
me.” That hero doubtless wants to get square
with some Queensland girl who didn’t go crazy
over him in his khaki.

A lady who was in the Peking Legations
during the siege has contributed an account of her
fxporiencea to two Australian dailies. She says
that the Ambassadors were a weak-kneed lot, with
the exception of the Baron von Ketteler, who was
killed.
Mills, the latest addition to M.L. Cabinet,
will not increase its strength. Hogg would have
been the ablest man for the billet. He is a
staunch Radical, and has the land-settlement
question at his finger-ends. But he was barred as
an anti-Jingo.
Adelaide Electric Tramways.
SOME REASONS
Why the Bill of the British Westinghouse Electric
Manufacturing Company
AND THE
Callender’s Cable and Construction Company, Limited,
SHOULD BE THROWN OUT.
—o-<frqeos».!o
1. BECAUSE they HAVE TRESPASSED on the rights of the GOVERNMENT and the
CORPORATION by arranging to purchase certain of the Tramways within the City and Suburbs
without the previous sanction of either the Government or the Corporation, to the latter of whom
the sole right of purchase was originally granted by every private Act constituting and sanctioning
each Tramway Company, which right of purchase was by the Tramway Act of 1884 transferred to
the Government. The arrangement made for purchasing the Tramways without the express sanction
of the Government previously obtained, was therefore A DEFIANCE OF THE RIGHTS OF
THE PEOPLE and Corporation of Adelaide in the interests of certain private speculators.
2. BECAUSE the whole capital, one and a half millions sterling, of the British Westinghouse
Company is pledged in respect of its manufacturing work and other undertakings, and cannot be
alienated to the exclusive purchase and reconstruction of the Adelaide Tramways, whereas the Cor-
poration Bill, which has received no consideration from Parliament, is backed by a guaranteed capi-
tal of one and a half millions for the express purpose of the Adelaide Tramways only, which sum is
equal to the WHOLE of the present capital of the British Westinghouse Company.
3. BECAUSE the Bill of the British Westinghouse and Callender Companies is NOT THE
SHORTEST ROAD TO MUNICIPALISATION, as, although the Municipalities have at
present no right of purchase,
IT IS NOT TRUE
that even the Govt, cannot purchase the Tramways until 1904 and 1905. The Tramway Companies,
having, in defiance of the law governing the matter, offered to sell their property to private specula-
tors without the previous sanction of the Government, have forfeited any right they may claim to
retain the Tramways for the full term of seven years since the last period has elapsed when the Tram-
ways were purchasable by the Government. The Tramway Companies having declared their willing-
ness to sell to private speculators within the seven years’ term, the Government are at liberty to step
in and insist on effecting the purchase themselves, with a view to either nationalising the Trams or
handing them over to the municipalities, on such terms as they may think best. It is impossible to
suppose that Parliament would allow private speculators to usurp the functions of the Government in
this matter, and thereby rob the Citizens of Adelaide of their rights.
4. BECAUSE, although the British Westinghouse and Callender Companies’ last proposals have
been accepted by a majority of the Municipal Tramways Committee, two of the local governing
bodies there represented, viz., the Corporations of St. Peters and Norwood and Kensington, have
already withdrawn, and others are about to follow, influenced, no doubt, by the fact that FAR
BETTER PROPOSALS have been AND ARE STILL OFFERED to the Joint Municipalities,
proposals which have been accepted by the' Corporation of Adelaide, and embodied in a Bill, which
by a mere technicality has been prevented from receiving the consideration of Parliament. It is to
Parliament, therefore, that the citizens have to look to redress this injustice, and to see that no parti-
sanship or short-sightedness is allowed to stand in the way of the City and Suburbs of Adelaide re-
ceiving the benefits of the better proposals which have been offered them for seven months past.
5. BECAUSE the British Westinghouse and Callender Companies REFUSE, in their Bill,
to give the Municipalities any SHARE IN THE PROFITS, whereas the Corporation Bill, which
has received no consideration from Parliament, reserved 10 PER CENT, of the profits to the
municipalities, besides the payment of £l5OO a year for rent, and for some £5OO per annum for
licenses, a sum which is £5OO in excess of the annual contribution guaranteed by the British
Westinghouse and Callender Companies in the first ten years, and equal to that guaranteed in
the second ten years. Because whatever doubts the British Westinghouse and Callender Com-
panies may entertain, perhaps with reason, as to their ability to work the undertaking so as to
secure the municipalities receiving a substantial sum as the City’s Share of the Profits, the
guarantors of the Corporation Bill, which has received no consideration from Parliament, have
sufficient faith in their ability to make a fair profit on the million and a half they are willing to
invest, not only for themselves, but for the City also, with whom they propose to share it.
6. BECAUSE the Corporation Bill, which has received no consideration from Parliament,
but which is still available to the municipalities, does not propose to wait ten years, or till a
Greater Adelaide is formed, before placing the Tramways under municipal control, but provides
that the Tramways should be acquired from the outset in the name and on behalf of the Corpora-
tion of Adelaide, as Trustees for the whole of the local governing bodies interested, who should
control the undertaking from the outset, and should be represented on the Board of Management,
as also should the suburban municipalities, with whom the City Corporation proposed to divide
pro rata the receipts and profits derived from the undertaking.
7. BECAUSE it would be better for the Citizens to put up with the present Horse Trams for a
short period RATHER THAN BARTER THEIR RIGHTS for an inadequate rental of some
£l5OO per annum, even for the first ten years, and thereby deliver themselves over into the hands of
one particular manufacturing Company for perhaps half a century. If it is assumed by the British
Westinghouse and Callender Companies that the mere obtaining of the options of purchase and the
presenting of them, like a pistol at the head of the Citizens of Adelaide, as a threat that, unless their
proposals are agreed to, the conversion to electric traction will be delayed by them for the next five
years, this leaf, borrowed by the British Westinghouse Company from American practice, will per-
haps result in showing them that the Citizens of a British Colony are not to be so easily coerced to
their own disadvantage by any combination of capitalists whatsoever.
8. BECAUSE the British Westinghouse and Callender Companies were so anxious to complete
the conversion to electric traction in the shortest time possible that they made their arrangements
with the Tramway Companies without waiting for the consent and approval of their scheme by either
the City or Suburban Councils, whom they treated apparently as negligible quantities.
9. BECAUSE the British Westinghouse Company is NOT a Company of world-wide reputation
and experience, unless in the sense of having borrowed those characteristics from its parent, the
Westinghouse Company of America. Inasmuch as the British Westinghouse Company was, according
to “The Electrical Review,” registered on July 10, 1899, it is exactly fifteen months old, and it is to
be hoped that it will not repeat the experiment it is making at present—viz., that of masquerading in
borrowed plumes, but will modestly content itself with emulating the achievements of its foster parent
before claiming the recognition and reward which, and not till then, will be its due.
10. BECAUSE, while it is true that experience has shown that improvements in electrical science
have come from private persons and Companies so far, it is all the more advisable that the City of
Adelaide shall not confine itself to the improvements which may be made by the British Westing-
house and Callender Companies alone, and be prevented thereby from enjoying the benefit of
inventions and improvements made by all the other electrical firms, and perhaps in the end be com-
pelled to purchase, at an exorbitant figure, a system out of date and a type of plant which is obsolete.
The guarantors of the one and a-half millions sterling, which is provided for carrying out the better
proposals contained in the Corporation Bill, which has received no consideration from Parliament,
have no interest whatever in the profits to be made by the manufacture and sale of plant, but it is
their interest and business to see that all the plant used from time to time is of the highest class, the
latest type, and the most capable of producing economical results, and to that end will reserve to
themselves the liberty of purchasing the plant of that manufacturing firm which guarantees the best
results.
11. BECAUSE one of the guarantors of the Corporation Bill, which has received no considera-
tion from Parliament, is the great Exploration Company, who financed and equipped the Central
London Electric Railway, recently opened by his Royal Highness the Prince of Wales, which cost
over five millions sterling, the plant for which was supplied by the General Electric Company. It is
only right that the Citizens of Adelaide should be at liberty to obtain their plant where they choose,
and should not be bound to buy from any one or two manufacturing Companies, even if they be of
the standing of the Westinghouse and Callender Companies.
12. BECAUSE the British Westinghouse and Callender Companies have no monopoly for
the expenditure of British money in Adelaide, and that any other British Company would be obliged
to spend the same, while the same amount of capital will be released for local purposes whoever pur-
chases the Adelaide Tramways.
13 BECAUSE, although the British Westinghouse and Callender Companies offer a prior right
to the shareholders only of the present Adelaide Tramways of reinvesting their capital m the new
Company the guarantors of the Corporation Bill, which has received no consideration from Parlia-
ment are prepared to follow the precedent set by the Auckland Electric Tramways Company, by
giving to the whole of the inhabitants of Adelaide, and not to the shareholders in the Tramway
Companies alone, priority of allotment in the new shares before all outsiders.
W. OEHT&T BINGHAM.

HALF-YEARLY REPORT of the DIRECTORS of the BANK of AUSTRALASIA, with the Accounts for the Half-year
to 16th April, 1900, Presented to the Proprietors at the Half-yearly General Meeting, held on Thursday, 4th
October, 1900.
R E P O R T-
The Directors submit to the Proprietors the Balance-sheet as at 16th April last, with the Profit Account for the
half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for British and colonial rates and taxes, and for
all bad and doubtful debts, the net profit for the half year amounted to £148,422 10s. 3d. From this sum, increased
bv £11,152 12s. 2d. brought forward from the previous half-year, the directors have appropriated £40,000 to the
reserve fund, £20,000 in reduction of the cost of bank premises, and £15,000 for the restoration to those members of
the staff still in the service of a portion of the amount deducted from their salaries in 1895-7. The dividend declared
will absorb £72,000, and there will then remain £12,575 2s. sd. to be carried forward to next account.
As in previous half-year, the profit has been increased by recoveries from old dependencies, and, following the
course then pursued, the directors have added appreciably to the reserves.
The season has, on the whole, been satisfactory, though the rain, so much required in some parts of Queensland,
fell too late to prevent the loss of enormous numbers of stock.
Since the date of the last Report the Federation of the Australian colonies has been sanctioned by Parliament,
and will become an accomplished fact early next year, and there is reason to hope that it will lead to the benefit of
colonial trade.
The dividend declared is at the rate of 9 per cent, per annum, or £ll6s. per share for the half-year, and will be
payable, free of income-tax, in London and in the colonies on the sth October.
4 Threadneedle-street, London, 24th September, 1900. W. R. ARBUTHNOT, Chairman.

the Policy Holders and Agents of the
Ocean Accident and Guarantee Cor-
poration , Limited 9 of London .
Dear Sirs,
The business of this Corporation in Queensland, which has been entirely built up by me, has
now been placed under other management. The following letter will acquaint you of how I stand in
the estimation of the Corporation. I have declined the offer of a Directorship from the Ocean, and
my firm has joined forces with the New Zealand Accident Insurance Company. In the course of a
few days I purpose issuing, in pamphlet form, copy of all correspondence between the Ocean Corpora-
tion and myself, which I feel sure will be the means of securing a continuance of your business through
me, when your interests will be safeguarded as hitherto. Yours faithfully,
J. C. HASSALL.
[Copt.]
J. C. Hassall, Esq., Sydney, August 24, 1900.
Brisbane.
Dear Sir, —Confirming my recent interview with you, I have now with great regret to give
you notice that at the expiration of one month from date, it is our intention to terminate your services
as agent for Queensland.
We all very greatly appreciate the good and hard work put in by you for the Corporation during
the past three or four years, and deeply regret your determination not to be associated with us in the
future. If there is any way in which the connection between yourself and the Corporation can be
perpetuated, I shall be only too pleased to entertain any proposal from you in that direction.
I need not add what I have already told you, that the reason for our taking the step for Queensland
is to bring our organisation into uniformity in the colonies, in that our branch Manager is restricted to
the Corporation’s business only. Yours faithfully,
(Signed) J. J. ALLAN, Manager.
A formal notice is enclosed herewith.
New Zealand Accident Insurance Company.
I have this day appointed Messrs. J. C. Hassall and Company Special Agents for Queensland.
EDGAR W. WALKER,
October 25, 1900. Manager and Attorney for Queensland.

ANOTHER OBSTINATE
LIVER.
Another Effectual Cure.
Tho Cass of
Mr. E. TUCKER.
(A special report.)
In consequeuce of information received, a
reporter interviewed Mr. Edwin Tucker, of
No. 108 Wilson-street, Redfern. A cordial
reception was extended to him, when a de-
sire was expressed to let us know the follow-
ing facts :
“It is now fifteen years ago since my
physical well-being was upset for the first
time in my life,” said Mr. Tucker. “ I was
then living in Goulbum, where many friends
of mine are still located. My liver asserted-
undeniable proof that it was seriously de-
ranged, although I experienced more
anxiety from attacks of palpitation of the
heart, which I thought was diseased. It was
a common thing to be seized with sharp
spasms in my left breast, the violence of
which almost doubled me up in agony.
These fits took away my breath for a while,
leaving me gasping and in an exhausted
condition. I observed great care in regard
to food, everything that I ate and drank
being of an easily-digested character, with
a view to obviating the development of my
complaint.”
* ydur carefulness rewarded ? ”
ot in the least degree. Instead
■ the progress of the disease, my
.sufferings became more intense every day.
Pains in the stomach after meals were daily
increasing in severity. The wind arising
from the same quarter was in greater abun-
dance, belching continuing long after meals
were concluded. Vomiting often took
place, the matter generally being green and
sour in taste, which was a source of un-
pleasantness in my mouth. My tongue was
never free from a coating of an unhealthy
substance, do whatever 1 may to try and
remove it, and I often had ringing sensations
in the ears, accompanied by dizziness in the
head, which made my sight quite weak,
with black specks sometimes appearing
before my eyes. My complexion was
yellow, and, with the tbin, drawn aspect of
my face, I looked frightful. Behind the
shoulders there were continual pricking
puns, and i had a lot of trouble with my
idneys also. There was a never ending,
null, heavy, dragging pain in the lower
ortion of my back, which was particularly
i ad after lying down.”
“ How did your strength keep up 1 ”
“ Whilst I could eat a little food I was
muly strong, taking everything into con-
sideration ; but eventually my appetite left
me altogether. I used to try very hard to
take something sustaining, but my efforts
were fruitless. Things that I could have
eaten ravenously before my illness came on
were obnoxious, and the smell produced a
feeling of sickness. Sometimes I had a
feeble desire for food, but the fulness in my
sliest afterwards, besides the aggravation of
other pains, made me loathe the idea of eat-
ing for days. And I slept very indifferently
—turning from side to side in a restless
spirit of vexation, usually falling asleep
when it was time to get up. I felt more
dead than alive, with aching limbs and
leaden eyes. Just fancy a man getting up-
in that condition to go to work ! No
wonder I was always drowsy and disliked
exertion. Why, on Sundays and holidays
I made the most of my opportunities to rest,
b.ut the same old feelings of lassitude and
fatigue were always apparent. Relief from
occupation did wot diminish the acuteness
of my ailments. I believe, on the other
hand, my miseries were intensified when I
was not at business. Being too restless to
read or interest myself in anything, I spent |
the leisure time in reflecting upon the
seriousness of my illness. ”
“ Such ruminations would not benefit
you ! ”
“Of course not. But my general weak
ness took all pluck out of me. I was too
low-spirited to take a hopeful view of my
case. My future seemed dark and cheer
less, having been invalided so long. A
feeling of melancholy took possession of my
brain, and the unhappy thoughts which ran
through my aching head were enough to
distract me. Nervousness was one of tin
piost conspicuous symptoms of my disease.
Without knowing the reason, I frequently
found myself trembling most violently. 1
had shocks to the system from causes which
were so (rifling and insignificant that they
would have escaped a healthy person’s ob
servation. My health was irretrievably
lost, I thought, as every medicine I had
taken did not show any beneficial effect.
Pills also proved equally useless. Then a
friend told me to try Clements Tonic. I
told him it would be folly to waste any more
money on medicines, as they could not cure
me. His persuasive eloquence at length
prevailed, and when I had taken about two-
thirds of a bottle of Clements Tonic, I could
see I was making rapid headway towards
recovery. After taking a couple of doses I
had a really refreshing sleep—the first I had
enjoyed for weeks. The distressing agonies
of my li\ er complaint begin to ease off, and
the palpitation of my heart ceased to trouble
me. As a matter of fact, there was soon a
wonderful change in my condition. I com
menced to eat my food again with a relish,
and the disappearance of pains from the
stomach and .shoulders intimated that my
digestive organs were improving. Giddi-
ness, nervousness, and headaches submitted
before many days to the remarkable sooth
ing qualities! f Clements Tonic, and my de-
nuded frame regab ed its lost flesh and
healthy vigour.’’
“ Has your health failed yon since ? ”
“ With the exception of a had turn whilst
wor mg in tin mines at Mount Zeehan,
Tasmania, seven years ago, my health has
been grand. The dampness in the mines
accounted for a severe cold, which resulted
in distressing complications setting in. I
took on my old remedy—Clements Tonic—
and again it cured me. It is the only medi
cine that ever did’me good, and at a time,
too, when all other physics had failed.
Clements Tonic worked my complaint tight
out of my system, as 1 have not been
troubled with any of the symptoms since.
For the benefit of others, you are at liberty
to publish my statement how you like.”
STATUTORY DEC LARATION.
I, Edwin Tucker, of 108 Wilson-street, Redfern,
Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have care-
fully read the annexed document, consisting of three
folios, and consecutively numbered from one to three,
and that it contains and is a true and faithful account
of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and t.lso
oontains my full permission to publish in any way
my statements-which I give voluntarily, without
receiving any payment; and I this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of an Act made
and passed in the ninth year of the reign of t. er present
Majesty, intituled “An Act for the more effectual
abolition of Oaths and Affirmations taken and made
in the various Departments of the Government of
New South Wales, and to substitute Declarations in
lieu thereof, and for the suppression of voluntary and
Extra-judicial Oaths and Affidavits.’’
Declared at Redfern, this 27th day of September, one
thousand nine hundred, before me,

with the British army in S’Africa, writes in Sept.
Strand Mao. on the poisoned-bullet lie
Among the stones lay a packet of the venomous-looking
green cartridges still unfired. They talk of poison, but I
doubt it. Verdigris would be an antiseptic rather than a
poison in a wound. It is more likely that it is some de-
composition of the wax in which the bullets are dipped.

According to Baron Yon Richthofen the
coal-fields of China are of enormous extent and
richness. In Shansi, 20,000 sq. miles out of the
total area of 53,000 form one immense coal-held,
estimated to be equal to the world’s consumption
for thousands of years to come. Railways to this
district it is stated, will have to be tunnelled
through the coal itself. The local supplies of iron-
ore it is said, are practically inexhaustible ; and
altogether, in view of the fatness of the carcase,
it’s little wonder that the eagles are gathered
together!

“Orange, N.S.W.

Gentle and long-suffering was that Vic.
blacksmith and ex-parson who appeared the other
day in Court to complain about his wife and the
inevitable lodger. “ They used to walk together
in the bush on Sunday afternoons,” he said. He
had been “a subject of unconsciousness” for a
time, but when his wife’s habit of going into the
lodger’s room and remaining there for as much as
20 minutes at a time had lasted for about six
weeks he became partially awake. He had also,
he said, seen the lodger leaving his wife’s room at
4 a.m., and about this time he began to think that,
possibly, something was wrong. The husband said
in Court that he wasn’t “an unaccountable sole-
eism ” or an “organ of destruction ” but admitted
that on one occasion when he was suffering from
extreme me.ital anguish Policeman 72 had assisted
him into a cab. When this very gradual indi-
vidual had become convinced that something was
wrong, and when his wife told him the lodger hr d
been ‘fondling her,” he raised “no real row or
tongue hostility ” ; he merely asked the boarder
in a cursory, incidental sort of way to find other
apartments as soon as convenient, but not to un-
duly bustle himself about it. Truly a man long-
suffering and slow to think evil.


LAZY SUMMER DAYS.
STRANGE how many people feel lazy in Summer. “Too lazy to move almost ”is a
common expression at this time of the year, and yet these men and women are
not of lazy dispositions. They are lively enough in the winter. What is the
matter, then ? Why, they are victims of “ that weary worn-out,” which is brought on
by the hot weather weakening the system. The enervating effects of the hot,
“ muggy ” Australian Summer are too well-known to need describing here ; sufficient
to say that the violent heat of Summer debilitates and weakens the human system
entirely if it is not in the most perfect order. Thus it is that people “feel ready
to drop ”at all hours of the day without the slightest apparent cause. To avoid
this lassitude one thing is necessary—the whole system, and especially the digestive
organism, must be in perfect and regular working order. For this purpose there
is no preparation so effective as Bile Beans for Biliousness. Acting in a gentle
manner, they do not (as many other purgatives do) achieve a temporary abatement
of the trouble, but eventually leave the system weaker than ever. On the con-
trary, they strengthen and build up the whole digestive organism, purify the blood
and tone up the nervous system. They have already proved themselves the very
best medicine for all Summer ailments, and are admitted to be the foremost remedy
in Australia. A course of Bile Beans will enable you to move round this Summer
with a light step and increased vitality. There will then be no lack of energy to
mar the pleasures of the Summer season.
