The Camel in Westralia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Despite the perfectly natural and familiar way
in which the camel has established itself on Wes-
tralian soil, there is a peculiarity about it which
makes me feel as if I were looking at a ghost
This peculiarity is not so much its noiseless gait
as the reminder which it brings of a man I once
knew, who is now dead, and ought to be a ghost
in due course. He used to wear a very ill-used
look—a conceited, yet humble, air, which plainly
let you know that he bore injustice sublimely
because he intended to be in Heaven some day
when I was choking, for my wicked crimes, with
murky sulphur-fumes.
Well, the camel looks just like that; it is an
animal characterised by such a stupid, conceited,
uncomplainingly-resigned manner, that I never
saw it without remembering my friend, the ill-
used individual. (This manner is the camel’s
work-a-day expression; wait till it opens its
mouth and shows its jagged, ugly teeth, and you
will get some idea of the vicious part of its
nature.) I have also reflected that the camel
resembles a huge rat with the mange, an enormous
rodent with a big bubonic hump on its back.
The first camel to which I came close enough to
touch was a sick camel. It was resting on its
chest-pad, with one eye partly gouged out and its
head in an altogether battered condition. Curi-
osity tempted me to feel that famous hump. A
bushman called out to me to keep off. “You
want to look out mighty keerful, young feller,” he
said; “you’ve not got a hoss to deal with, you
know.” Naturally I wanted to know why I had
to be careful of a good, patient, docile camel—
man’s comrade of the desert. You see, I had read
all about the camel in that school-book which also
makes us wonder what we would do if we had no
cow. The bushman then started to explain that
the camel jumps up quick, catches you by the
arm or the neck, or maybe by the trousers, and
dashes you to the ground ; then proceeds to sit on
your head with its chest-pad, and finally grinds
that head to pulp. Then we got a bit further off
and studied the camel.
* * * *
After that the study of camels became an occu-
pation of mine. I mixed with the Afghan camel-
drivers, and stood and watched them coming and
goingabout Menzies with their longstringsof sixties
and. seventies. Probably there is no town in Wes-
tralia where the camel is in greater demand. It is
used for carrying purposes—some being yoked to
waggons, but most of them carrying freight on
their humps. People take rides on the camel;
men go prospecting with it. Every hour of the
day you see it shambling along, carrying the
necessaries of those living away from the railway,
in spots where the camel alone makes it possible
for Duman beings to live. Westralian camels
carry timber, machinery, food, water—in fact,
anything that may be required in that desolate
land of sand and scrub. They need no roads, but
take a bee-line to their destination, and the track
is made which only the Afghan who leads them
may know.
Each camel carries about 6cwt. or 7cwt. It is
tethered by a piece of string fastened from a nose-
peg to the saddle of the camel in front of it. The
nose-peg is made of mulga, fashioned like a chess
pawn with a squat bottom, thrust up the nostril
and passed through a hole made in the flesh. The
string is looped over the peg, and so the camels
pass on, “leading each other by the nose.” The
first camel is led by an Afghan, who plods
silently, the camels just as silently following ; the
only noise of the procession conies from a few
bullock-bells fastened to the necks of some of the
camels. It is a picture of monotony—a string of
ghosts from an Oriental clime.
There seems to be no sympathy between a camel
and its driver. You never hear a healthy bit of
swearing, such as usually accompanies a bullock-
team ; there is no cracking of whips. The camel
evidently understands two Afghan words and
nothing more. It is told to “ Hooshta ! ” and it
hooshtas —that is, sits down. Camels are excel-
lent workers, but terrible beasts for routine. If you
train a camel to carry lcwt., and then put 2cwt.
on its back, it won’t work. The camel-driver
nct ’® as ® s the load of his camel on a journey.
If he sets out with half-a-load he must finish with
half-a-load. If he knows that he must take up
freight on the way, lie will start with an equal
weight m rocks ; and throw the rocks off to pick
UP the freight. If .you overload a camel he will
hoosh-ta ’’ without being told, and nothing will
make him rise until the extra weight is taken off.
A teamster told me that he got bogged with a
load of five tons in his waggon. It was drawn by
eight camels. He kept them drawing until he
found that the harness was being broken and the
dray was straining to pieces, when he unloaded.
Now, with the load on, the camels had been draw-
ing three inches at a pull. With the load off they
still drew three inches at a pull.
* * * .
In its ordinary life the camel is a quiet beast
which does its work with very little trouble ; and
there is no danger in working it. The female is
never anything else but a patient, docile worker ;
and the bull is most of his time the same. But
the bull changes at certain seasons, and becomes
unmanageable and jealous of other bulls. To keep
the bull from fighting at these times a piece of
string is fastened from a ring in the ear to the
foreleg, and every time he attempts to bite, the
string pulls his ear. Then he becomes quiet.
The camel is tender about the nose, ear, and brain.
In fact there is very little protection to the camel’s
brain. It has no skull-bone, only a coating of
gristle. The camels seem well aware of this ;
consequently, when they fight, the aim of each
camel is to get its opponent’s head under its chest-
pad and to crush it. They fight only for jealousy.
The female is never in season. She flies from
the bull, who.catches her by the heel and throws
her. bhe resists for hours.
There is a certain amount of truth in the yarn
of a camel going days without water; but in Wes-
tralia this only happens when the camel is so
trained. If you train it to go a week without
water, it will take a drink that swells out its sides
like a barrel. If you don’t train it, it will die of
thirst as soon as any other beast. After all, there
is little to boast about in this resistance to thirst;
because brumbies and scrub cattle will do the
same as a camel. They only visit water at inter-
vals, and then take in a supply that will last them
a number of days among the ranges.
People generally suppose that the hump of the
camel is a sort of anatomical food-and-water sup-
ply. There is one thing certain : a camel in good
condition has a big hump, and a poor camel has
scarcely any hump at all. As a matter of fact,
the camel is like the seal. It lives on its own fat *
and the fat of the camel is its hump. The seal in
rutting time goes six and seven weeks without
food or water. This, as a record, leaves the camel
nowhere.
There is also truth in the idea that water can be
obtained from a camel. 1 know a man who got
some, but he didn’t drink it. He wanted a drink
badly, but he threw the camel-water away, lit a
fare, and got some sap out of a green log. To tap
a camel you insert the blade of a knifoin its flank,
and a billycanful of green, sloppy liquid comes
out; but nobody drinks it. It turns the stomach.
• • » *
I. he chest-pad of the camel is a very important
i P rotect: ‘ s the bone we call the sternum.
W hen a beast ‘ ‘ hooshtas ” you will notice it grind
the hard crust of the soil to dust in order to make
a comfortable spot to rest on. When a camel has
been resting you will notice patches of dust for
yards round. The pad is also a means of defence
or offence, as already stated. It gives the finish-
ing blow. When two bulls fight, the object of
each is to get his head over the opponent’s neck,
and when he succeeds in doing this he catches the
foreleg with his teeth and throws his foe. Then
the chest-pad comes into operation to complete
the conquest. If he can take his opponent un-
awares, he catches the hind-leg, when he can upset
the other camel with the greatest ease.
There is a very noxious slime in the camel’s
teeth, and this slime causes blood-poisoning when
any one is bitten. Bites seem to be the chief
cause of sickness in bulls, and many die ; but their
instinct guides them to those blood-purifying
plants which are indigenous to Westralia, and
often they recover. A man who is unlucky
enough to get bitten is likely to lose a limb.
I he gayest sight I’ve ever seen is a flash young
Afghan mounted on a camel. It is a con-
glomerated rainbow in motion. The man is
clothed in pure white linen, and a scarlet jacket
laced and trimmed with filigree stuff ; round his
gold-peaked hat is wound a voluminous blue
turban, and he sits on colors even brighter than
those he wears. .The rugs beneath him are of
green and orange and crimson; the bridle he
guides his beast with is similarly colored ; on the
head of the camel is a gay head-dress, and round
its knees are bright garters hung with bells which
jingle as the camel trots by. A Chinese pro-
cession is gay ; but a party of Afghans out for a
ride in regalia would eclipse it. The walk of a
camel is like a small boat in a rough sea, and its
motion sickens those out for a first ride. The trot
is easy. A camel will bolt. I heard of one bolt-
ing with a man, and then dashing him off and
crushing him with its chest-pad.
* * * *
The Afghans have made great fortunes out of
the camel in Westralia. Many of these Asiatics
are house-owners, and reap big harvests from rents
as well as from carrying. A couple have the
reputation of being millionaires ; and this is likely
enough, since very high prices are charged per
ton for carrying. The camel is a dry-weather
beast, however ; and,.of little use in rough country.
It cannot travel on boggy soil any better than
horses, and stony country appears to make it very
tender-footed. It is cheap to keep, as it subsists
largely on the salt-bush. It is a great nuisance
about a camp. The miner’s homestead rarely has
any fence about it, and when it has, the camel
breaks the fence down to get at the eatables in
the frail structure. To teach it a lesson the
miners make it “hooshta!” and then put some
boiling tar on a part not mentioned m polite
literature. The camel never returns to attack
that camp. It stands a few hundred yards off,
gazing pensively at the spot of its punishment.
The camel has come to Australia to stay. It
will live and thrive here like the rabbit and the
hare. The white man is beginning to understand
it and work it as well as the Afghan, and some
day he will rise up and tell the Afghan to go, for
he can work the camel without him. Probably
we will teach the camel to buck-jump, and we shall
run-in brumby oamels ; and it may yet be said of
the Australian youth that he dearly loves the
camel. Or the Commonwealth may hear of a
Melbourne Cup being raced for and won by a
camel, and on some stations all hands will turn
out to see Jimmy, the aboriginal, ride an outlaw
camel.
N.s.w.
ROBERT BROTHERS.
The President of the Japanese Hguse of
Peers recently visited Europe and America, and
on his return stated, in a public address, that the
one thing which especially impressed him was the
fact that the men who control affairs in Great
Britain, Germany and the United States are
sincere Christians. And yet the Japs are said to
have no sense of humor.
Dear Bulletin, —With regard to your
“Plea for the Loving Woman,” and the M.L.
lady’s gaol-cure for illegitimate child-bearing,
in the last century a woman named Polly
Baker was prosecuted in Connecticut, America,
for the fifth time—for giving birth to illegitimate
children. This is the speech which is handed
down as having been made by Polly Baker on
her own behalf: —
May it please the honorable Bench to indulge me in a
few words. I shall not trouble your honors with long
speeches. All that I humbly hope is that your Honors
will charitably move the Governor’s goodness on my be-
half that my fine may be remitted. This is the fifth time,
gentlemen, that I have been dragged before your court
on the same account,; twice I have paid heavy fines, and
twice have been brought to public punishment for want
of money to pay these fines. This may have been agree-
able to the laws, and Ido not dispute it; but since laws
are sometimes unreasonable in themselves, and therefore
repealed, and others bear too hard on the subjects in par-
ticular cases, therefore there is left a power somewhere
to dispense with the execution of them.
Abstracted from the law I cannot conceive what the
nature of my offence is. I have brought five fine children
into the world at the risk of my life. I have maintained
them well by my own industry. Can it be a crime to add
to the number of the King’s subjects, in a new country
that really wants people? I should think it a praise-
worthy, rather than a punishable action. ... I shall be
told, ’tis like, that were there no assembly in this case,
the precepts of religion are violated by my transgressions.
If mine is a religious offence, leave it to religious punish-
ment. You believe I have offended Heaven, and must
suffer eternal fire—will not that be sufficient ? . . . But
how can it be believed that Heaven is angry at my having
children when, to the little done by me toward it, God
has been pleased to add His Divine skill and workman-
ship in the formation of their bodies, and crowning it by
furnishing them with rational and immortal souls F
If you, gentlemen, must be making laws, do not turn
natSral and useful actions into crimes by your prohibi-
tions. Take into your wise consideration the great and
growing number of bachelors in the country who, by
their manner of living, leave unproduccd hundreds of
their posterity. Is not this a greater offence against the
public good than mine? . . . What shall poor young
women do, when custom hath forbid to solicit the men,
and who cannot force themselves upon husbands, when
the law takes no pains to provide them any, and yet
severely punish them if they do their duty without them
—the duty of the first great command' of Nature ?—a
duty from the steady performance of which nothing has
been able to deter me.
Compare those words with that Maoriland
lady’s utterances as reported, and decide on which
side are reason and womanhood. —Yours,
V.R.

The Wreck.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh ! Private Enterprise ! or public theft!
, commerce * or whatever is your name-
You’ve struck me with your very dirty left,
And so I leave the game.
And so, oh trade ! I pray thee step aside
Without unnecessary row or fuss.
I am the groom who’s lost his blessed bride—
The bloke who’s missed the ’bus.
Let all the waves of commerce o’er me roll
So long as one occasional roller to the bar
Shall lift me, ’fore the trade-wind of the bowl—
Steering by Martell’s star.
X am the water that does not excel,
I am the round peg in the hole that’s square,
I am the good intentions paving hell,
lam old self-neglect ! and—l don’t care !
Oh, Lord, the world and all it holds are thine !
If so, You own all mining shows that be,
And all the duffers on the Margaret line,
And all the stock that’s been the death of me.
So, Lord, I think it right to state that in Thy
Stock Exchange
(And if it isn’t Yours —who’s got the deeds?)
Slowly does manhood perish by the mange—
The mange of easy-gotten gold in change for
weeds.
Oh Lord, the canker of the Room is on them—-
They till with lying, and reap sordid fruits !
Saving Your presence, Lord, gold lust’s upon them,
And of Finance they are the prostitutes.
Thou knowest, Lord, there’s no false pride in me !
And so I pray Thee save these worthy men,
Give me their wealth—their everlasting fee—
And all their present pangs I’ll suffer then.
What was it, Lord ! that knocked Thy servant out?
The bear of “ Morgans, ” or the “ Garnet ” bull ?
But never mind the way it came about —
My glass is empty, though my cup is full.
’Tis all the same—Thine hand hath chastened me—
Thy righteous ire hath struck me in the mouth ;
For, Lord, I was too fresh —I flew too free—
I bought Kalgurlis South.
My luck is gone—l always throw the seven !
Each spec. I have doth fail—
-1 am the Only Genuine Jonah —yea and even
I’ve lost my blessed whale.
The wild ass does not bray when he hath oats,
Over his fodder the ox loweth not—
But hear my brayings when I hear the quotes
Of yesterday that sent my stock to pot.
Ob, Lord ! the wicked flourish like a green bay
horse -.
(And that quotation seems awry and wrong—
Quotations all are wrong to me, of course:
Ere they are right, how long, oh Lord, how
long!)
Send a blind boom in all the stocks I hold !
Force other people’s stocks their par beneath !
Break thou the jaws of them who would enfold,
As their collateral security, my teeth.
Ha, my once friend—Zophar the Naamathite !
From that man’s knavery to mine is far ;
I care not ! I shall drink with him to-night!
He nods ! —l’m called unto the bar !
Slumpville (Vic.)
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
“How-de-do?”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The vaunted progressiveness of the nineteenth
century was never more fully exposed as a fraud
than when lately I met a lady who once had been
queen of my heart, not only at morn but also at
noontide and in the gloaming, to say nothing of
the bridge at midnight. I don’t suppose any two
people loved as did we two. I used to fall asleep
in the office thinking of her. In fact, the only
time I could not sleep was when I went to bed,
for then, in the silent watches of the night, I
realised the hopelessness of my position. She
was the daughter of a retired and deceased publi-
can, and her mother had just enough .money to
live without working. Being pecuniarily nobody,
I had been given to understand that the likes of
Gwendoline M‘Manus was not for the likes of me;
and later a letter of mine was found in Gwen’s
pocket which caused my complete banishment
from the halls of M‘Manus. We still remained
engaged, and met furtively with joy, and parted
—heaven knows how. I don’t know why I did
not carry her off, unless it was because she was
built on a liberal scale, like her late lamented
father. She had a kind, broad, beaming face and
large crimson lips which to the privileged touch
were like red velvet cushions.
The inevitable discovery of our secret meetings
soon came, through yet another letter being found
in Gwen’s pocket. It was such a sweet one, she
wrote me, she could not bear to destroy it; so at
our next tryst she did not appear, and shortly
afterwards our engagement was broken off, mainly
because she got married to someone else. He was
a Greek prince, I believe—a wiry little bone of a
man, with diamond rings on every finger and a tall
hat tilted rakishly on a mass of black, curly, well-
oiled hair. I could not, at first, understand how
he got Mrs. Mac.’s consent.
But Mr. Kouparthphysgolos, who was called
“Koupar” and finally Mr. Cooper for short,
went about matters quite simply. He adored
Gwen from the moment he saw her, and told her
mother he would settle all his possessions upon
her. This sounded rather large, as she was not
aware that they consisted of a fish-shop and two
ice-cream barrows in Melbourne. As an alterna-
tive he promised that if she did not marry him he
would shoot everybody connected with the affair,
put the bodies in a heap, and slay himself on the
summit of the pile. Gwen had never before been
courted in this volcanic fashion, and she was
frightened and pleased and interested. Moreover,
she was too large and lazy to resist violently.
I lost her, and for some weeks life was a blank,
enlivened only when wild visions came to me of
plans to make large sums of money by backing
horses, or robbing a mail-steamer, and then carry-
ing off my large, lost love. Then somehow pro-
sperity began to come my way. I Was gradually
made welcome in better society than Gwen or her
mother could have commanded. Here I met
ladies of a much more refined stamp, but their
pale faces and thin lips seemed tame and lifeless
after Gwen’s robust, plate-like face, Junoesque
body, and cushiony mouth. I wouldn’t marry,
but began to make money. In a few years Mrs.
Mac. would have welcomed me as a suitor, if I
had been twice as black as Koupar.
Well, I met Gwen unexpectedly face to face in
Bourke-street, and said as I shook hands, “ How-
de-do?” The inexpressible stupidity of the people
who have charge of our language had neglected to
"find me anything more fitting to say than that
awful banality. She also said, like an echo,
“ How-de-do ?”
Neither of us told the other how we did. I was
too much confused to know, and she was the pic-
ture of health. Such health ! The full swelling
figure of 20 had ripened into one of 40, which now
nearly filled the sidewalk and compelled her to go
into a shop edgeways. She had a tiny moustache.
She breathed heavily, and ; her ample bosom rose
and fell like a large bellows. The last time we
met that bosom was palpitating on mine, and I
had implored her to elope. I had said that surely
for love’s sweet sake we could live on bread and
water for a while until I found out where the
neigh bor’s fowls roosted. Anyhow, I was confi-
dent my earnings would buy us a loaf every day,
and, as for water —I knew where there were tons
of it. But even in those old poetic days Gwen
was a slave to four square meals a day. She
nearly fainted at the thought of bread and water,
and I had to hold her in my arms and kiss her for
five minutes before she recovered sufficiently to
say “Good-night ” and go home.
Here I was in Bourke-street, with all these
years of accumulated sentiment, and able to say
only “How-de-do?”
Had I been a Frenchman I could have said
“Mon ange! ” for that beautiful language is full of
sweet phrases which sound lovely, but do not
mean much.
Our own tongue abounds with uncouth idioms,
which sound awful but have worlds of meaning.
These were well enough in the semi-savage days
of the middle ages. I have heard my grand-
mother tell of an ancestor who came home from a
picnic one day—about the time they began to use
forks in England—so full of spear-heads and
arrows that he could not get his undershirt off on
account of the points which stuck out between his
ribs. His wife said, “Grammercy, my lord !
How fare ye?” as if he were a tram-conductor
instead of being merely a temporary arsenal, and
he replied, “Odds bodikins,” or words to that
effect. He died soon after while an eminent
surgeon was bleeding him.
However, I felt the poverty of our language
keenly. I could not say “Mon ange!” for I
have a sense of the ridiculous. To a Frenchman
love is never ridiculous, except in other people.
There are no angels of Gwen’s build ; at least if
there are I have never seen them in Michael
Angelo’s_ pictures of Heaven, and he had a circle
of acquaintances in that way.
She recovered first. “Oh, I am so pleased to
see you ; I knew you at once, you don’t look a day
older. Do come to lunch with us, and how are
you ? ”
“I am quite as well as could be expected,” I
replied, ‘ ‘ but—about ma?”
“ Oh ! poor mater will be delighted to see you.
We all heard of your success.”
Here we hailed a tram, and I took her on the
“dummy,” fearing if we went inside I should have
to push her edgeways through the door with my
head, as elephants propel their loads. Her broad
face beamed upon me as we, rode on. I could see,
when she told me Mr. “Cooper” was no more,
that I should not have to say “goodnight” out
at the gate now, even if I wanted. It seemed
from her talk that the entertaining, dressing, and
other expenses necessary to the maintenance and
exhibition of such a fine woman had taxed Mr.
Cooper’s energies till he had perished in the
struggle. Possibly eating some of his own fried
fish at threepence a slab had hastened the event.
He had left Gwen three children, besides the fish-
shop and ice-cream stands. But three swarthy
Macedonian brigands came in on bills-of-sale, so
the three daughters were the only assets she saved
out of the Greek’s estate.
We got off at a neat cottage which bore evidence
of trying to look much more “swagger” than it
really was. Three girls were idling about the
garden, and Gwen called the eldest and presented
her as “ My daughter, Meda.”
And this Andromeda was as beautiful as her
fabled namesake by the sea could possibly have
been.
She blushed divinely, too, and watched me with
eager eyes all the afternoon while I talked, and
Gwen beamed as if Time were a ’bus and had just
stopped to pick us up where we last got out. I
have always been deemed a lucky man, and began
to think it true, when I watched Meda’s enrap-
tured face, as, with closed eyes, she drew divine
music from her violin that night. Had not Fate
and Mrs. Mac. known what was best for me I
might have been starving all the days of my youth
to provide luxuries for that magnificent woman
whose large, blue, plate-like eyes were gazing at
me with growing signs of ownership. I might
have been across the Styx with Koupar, watching
his countrymen enduring their fantastic punish-
ments. By the way, I wonder why the gods did
not invent one which compelled a man to stand
at a window and look down to earth upon his
wife’s second husband spending his hard-earned
wealth ! I might have paid the piper all these
years, but shaken never a leg myself. Now .
There were many nights like this, varied by
walks in the garden, when Gwen wanted to spoon
and made excuses to get rid of the children, but I
would not have it. I loved children—of Meda’s
age.
One memorable evening, Gwen went into the
house to entertain a caller. I sent two of the girls
to buy half-a-ton of lollies. Gwen came to ask us
in to make music. It happened strangely that
Meda was not one of the children who had gone
for sweets.
Like most people who speak English, Gwen
found the poverty of the language, when under
the influence of strong emotion, somewhat trying.
When she fully grasped the situation, her first
words were : “ Well, here’s a pretty how-de-do ! ”
I never saw her angry before or since.
Meda and I live at Potts Point now. It’s awk-
ward for a man with a good memory to have such
a mother-in-law within calling distance.
PERCE ABBOTT.
Opened 14 months ago, the Nordraoh
Sanatorium, near Dunedin (M.L.), for the treat-
ment of consumption by the open-air system,
has achieved excellent results. Of 21 cases dealt
with, all but three of the patients have improved
wonderfully; some have returned to work appa-
rently quite cured; others are continuing the
open-air treatment at their homes. Sanatorium
patients live in cottages, and their doors and
windows remain open day and night, except in
stormy weather. No fires are allowed, but wraps
are largely availed of in cold weather. Exercise
consists of slow tramps up the hills as the lungs
strengthen. An astonishing thing about the
regime is the enormous amount of food consumed
—three or four times as much at each meal as
patients were previously accustomed to. Some
weaklings with one foot in the grave, who tried
the institute as a last resource, put on over a stone
in a month.
Supposing Seddon accomplishes his South
Sea annexation scheme, which includes the
granting of responsible government to Fiji, it will
be interesting to note how the q uestion of giving the
franchise to local niggers is dealt with. In the
mixed black and white government of Thakom-
bau Bex, which ruled from 71 to 74, the darkies
were allowed the right of voting for M’sP.,
though they could not exercise it much owing to
their want of knowledge. The arrangement was
nevertheless most distasteful to a large section of
the whites, who also set up their backs against a
Fijian judge sitting on the Supreme Court Bench
in imitation scarlet-and-ermine. The present
population of the islands is about 2000 whites,
108,000 Fijians, 9000 imported colored laborers
(mostly coolies from India), and SOO half-castes.

TTEKE’S A. SHORT STORY.
A milk supplier to Sydney had three cows suffering from Tuberculosis, and as he was afraid his healthy cattle would become contaminated, he sold them at less than £1 each to another
supplier who sent his milk to the same distributors. The seller now feels annoyed to think that the Sydney purchaser had been paying for all the milk from the diseased animals for some
inonths past. THIS MILK TS BEING SOLD IN SYDNEY TO-DAY ! This is no fairy tale, but an absolute fact, capable of being easily proven. This is only an individual case ;
there are man v, alas ! too many of a similar nature. . v

The N.S. W. Fresh Food and Ice Coy., Ltd., have every cow in every dairy thoroughly inspected, as well as the premises and utensils-used. They have their own railway trucks. All milk
arriving under standard quality is condemned. They laugh at local inspection. They lose suppliers because of their strict methods of inspection.
The Company sells milk in bulk or in bottle that is PASTEURISED. If delicate people use other milk, they may meet a premature death. Use only the milk supplied by the
F.F. &I. Coy., and they will get'healthy and strong. Strong robust people will grow the stronger. If, after this NOTE OF WARNING, you continue to use milk from cows *uf»ring
from Tuberculosis—hr, in plain words, Consumption—you deserve to die, for the fault rests with yourself.
